모모The Poems of Goethe

모모Translated in the original metresby Edgar Alfred Bowring

모모THE TRANSLATOR'S ORIGINAL DEDICATION.

모모TO THE COUNTESS GRANVILLE.

모모MY DEAR LADY GRANVILLE,--

모모THE reluctance which must naturally be felt by any one inventuring to give to the world a book such as the present, wherethe beauties of the great original must inevitably be diminished,if not destroyed, in the process of passing through thetranslator's hands, cannot but be felt in all its force when thattranslator has not penetrated beyond the outer courts of thepoetic fane, and can have no hope of advancing further, or ofreaching its sanctuary. But it is to me a subject of peculiarsatisfaction that your kind permission to have your nameinscribed upon this page serves to attain a twofold end--onedirect and personal, and relating to the present day; the otherreflected and historical, and belonging to times long gone by. Ofthe first little need now be said, for the privilege is whollymine, in making this dedication: as to the second, one word ofexplanation will suffice for those who have made the greatestpoet of Germany, almost of the world, their study, and to whomthe story of his life is not unknown. All who have followed thecareer of GOETHE are familiar with the name and character ofDALBERG, and also with the deep and lasting friendship thatexisted between them, from which SCHILLER too was not absent;recalling to the mind the days of old, when a Virgil and a Horaceand a Maecenas sat side by side.

모모Remembering, then, the connection that, in a former century,was formed and riveted between your illustrious ancestor and himwhom it is the object of these pages to represent, I deem it ahappy augury that the link then established finds itself notwholly severed even now (although its strength may beimmeasurably weakened in the comparison), inasmuch as this pagebrings them once more in contact, the one in the person of hisown descendant, the other in that of the translator of his Poems.

모모Believe me, with great truth,Very faithfully yours,EDGAR A. BOWRING.London, April, 1853.

모모ORIGINAL PREFACE.

모모I feel no small reluctance in venturing to give to the public awork of the character of that indicated by the title-page to thepresent volume; for, difficult as it must always be to rendersatisfactorily into one's own tongue the writings of the bards ofother lands, the responsibility assumed by the translator isimmeasurably increased when he attempts to transfer the thoughtsof those great men, who have lived for all the world and for allages, from the language in which they were originally clothed, toone to which they may as yet have been strangers. Preeminentlyis this the case with Goethe, the most masterly of all the masterminds of modern times, whose name is already inscribed on thetablets of immortality, and whose fame already extends over theearth, although as yet only in its infancy. Scarcely have twodecades passed away since he ceased to dwell among men, yet henow stands before us, not as a mere individual, like those whomthe world is wont to call great, but as a type, as an emblem--therecognised emblem and representative of the human mind in itspresent stage of culture and advancement.

모모Among the infinitely varied effusions of Goethe's pen, perhapsthere are none which are of as general interest as his Poems,which breathe the very spirit of Nature, and embody the realmusic of the feelings. In Germany, they are universally known,and are considered as the most delightful of his works. Yet inthis country, this kindred country, sprung from the same stem,and so strongly resembling her sister in so many points, they arenearly unknown. Almost the only poetical work of the greatestPoet that the world has seen for ages, that is really andgenerally read in England, is Faust, the translations of whichare almost endless; while no single person has as yet appeared toattempt to give, in an English dress, in any collective orsystematic manner, those smaller productions of the genius ofGoethe which it is the object of the present volume to lay beforethe reader, whose indulgence is requested for its manyimperfections. In addition to the beauty of the language in whichthe Poet has given utterance to his thoughts, there is a depth ofmeaning in those thoughts which is not easily discoverable atfirst sight, and the translator incurs great risk of overlookingit, and of giving a prosaic effect to that which in the originalcontains the very essence of poetry. It is probably thisdifficulty that has deterred others from undertaking the task Ihave set myself, and in which I do not pretend to do more thanattempt to give an idea of the minstrelsy of one so unrivalled,by as truthful an interpretation of it as lies in my power.

모모The principles which have guided me on the present occasion arethe same as those followed in the translation of Schiller'scomplete Poems that was published by me in 1851, namely, asliteral a rendering of the original as is consistent with goodEnglish, and also a very strict adherence to the metre of theoriginal. Although translators usually allow themselves greatlicense in both these points, it appears to me that by so doingthey of necessity destroy the very soul of the work they professto translate. In fact, it is not a translation, but a paraphrasethat they give. It may perhaps be thought that the presenttranslations go almost to the other extreme, and that a renderingof metre, line for line, and word for word, makes it impossibleto preserve the poetry of the original both in substance and insound. But experience has convinced me that it is not so, andthat great fidelity is even the most essential element ofsuccess, whether in translating poetry or prose. It was thereforevery satisfactory to me to find that the principle laid down byme to myself in translating Schiller met with the very general,if not universal, approval of the reader. At the same time, Ihave endeavoured to profit in the case of this, the younger bornof the two attempts made by me to transplant the muse of Germanyto the shores of Britain, by the criticisms, whether friendly orhostile, that have been evoked or provoked by the appearance ofits elder brother.

모모As already mentioned, the latter contained the whole of thePoems of Schiller. It is impossible, in anything like the samecompass, to give all the writings of Goethe comprised under thegeneral title of Gedichte, or poems. They contain between 30,000and 40,000 verses, exclusive of his plays. and similar works.Very many of these would be absolutely without interest to theEnglish reader,--such as those having only a local application,those addressed to individuals, and so on. Others again, fromtheir extreme length, could only be published in separatevolumes. But the impossibility of giving all need form noobstacle to giving as much as possible; and it so happens thatthe real interest of Goethe's Poems centres in those classes ofthem which are not too diffuse to run any risk when translated ofoffending the reader by their too great number. Those by far themore generally admired are the Songs and Ballads, which are about150 in number, and the whole of which are contained in thisvolume (with the exception of one or two of the former, whichhave been, on consideration, left out by me owing to theirtrifling and uninteresting nature). The same may be said of theOdes, Sonnets, Miscellaneous Poems, &c.

모모In addition to those portions of Goethe's poetical works whichare given in this complete form, specimens of the different otherclasses of them, such as the Epigrams, Elegies, &c., are added,as well as a collection of the various Songs found in his Plays,making a total number of about 400 Poems, embraced in the presentvolume.

모모A sketch of the life of Goethe is prefixed, in order that thereader may have before him both the Poet himself and the Poet'soffspring, and that he may see that the two are but one--thatGoethe lives in his works, that his works lived in him.

모모The dates of the different Poems are appended throughout, thatof the first publication being given, when that of thecomposition is unknown. The order of arrangement adopted is thatof the authorized German editions. As Goethe would never arrangethem himself in the chronological order of their composition, ithas become impossible to do so, now that he is dead. The planadopted in the present volume would therefore seem to be thebest, as it facilitates reference to the original. Thecircumstances attending or giving rise to the production of anyof the Poems will be found specified in those cases in which theyhave been ascertained by me.

모모Having said thus much by way of explanation, I now leave thebook to speak for itself, and to testify to its own character.Whether viewed with a charitable eye by the kindly reader, whowill make due allowance for the difficulties attending itsexecution, or received by the critic, who will judge of it onlyby its own merits, with the unfriendly welcome which it veryprobably deserves, I trust that I shall at least be pardoned formaking an attempt, a failure in which does not necessarily implydisgrace, and which, by leading the way, may perhaps become themeans of inducing some abler and more worthy (but not moreearnest) labourer to enter upon the same field, the riches ofwhich will remain unaltered and undiminished in value, evenalthough they may be for the moment tarnished by the hands of theless skilful workman who first endeavours to transplant them to aforeign soil.

모모PREFACE TO THE SECOND EDITION.

모모I have taken advantage of the publication of a Second Editionof my translation of the Poems of Goethe (originally published in1853), to add to the Collection a version of the much admiredclassical Poem of Hermann and Dorothea, which was previouslyomitted by me in consequence of its length. Its universalpopularity, however, and the fact that it exhibits theversatility of Goethe's talents to a greater extent than,perhaps, any other of his poetical works, seem to call for itsadmission into the present volume.

모모On the other hand I have not thought it necessary to includethe sketch of Goethe's Life that accompanied the First Edition.At the time of its publication, comparatively little was known inthis country of the incidents of his career, and my sketch wasavowedly written as a temporary stop-gap, as it were, pending theproduction of some work really deserving the tittle of a life ofGoethe. Not to mention other contributions to the literature ofthe subject, Mr. Lewis's important volumes give the Englishreader all the information he is likely to require respectingGoethe's career, and my short memoir appeared to be no longerrequired.

모모I need scarcely add that I have availed myself of thisopportunity to make whatever improvements have suggestedthemselves to me in my original version of these Poems.

모모E. A. B.London, 1874.

모모L'ENVOI

모모THE POEMS OF GOETHE.

모모DEDICATION.

모모The morn arrived; his footstep quickly scared

모모The gentle sleep that round my senses clung,And I, awak'ning, from my cottage fared,

모모And up the mountain side with light heart sprung;At every step I felt my gaze ensnared

모모By new-born flow'rs that full of dew-drops hung;The youthful day awoke with ecstacy,And all things quicken'd were, to quicken me.

모모And as I mounted, from the valley rose

모모A streaky mist, that upward slowly spread,Then bent, as though my form it would enclose,

모모Then, as on pinions, soar'd above my head:My gaze could now on no fair view repose,

모모in mournful veil conceal'd, the world seem'd dead;The clouds soon closed around me, as a tomb,And I was left alone in twilight gloom.

모모At once the sun his lustre seem'd to pour,

모모And through the mist was seen a radiant light;Here sank it gently to the ground once more,

모모There parted it, and climb'd o'er wood and height.How did I yearn to greet him as of yore,

모모After the darkness waxing doubly bright!The airy conflict ofttimes was renew'd,Then blinded by a dazzling glow I stood.

모모Ere long an inward impulse prompted me

모모A hasty glance with boldness round to throw;At first mine eyes had scarcely strength to see,

모모For all around appear'd to burn and glow.Then saw I, on the clouds borne gracefully,

모모A godlike woman hov'ring to and fro.In life I ne'er had seen a form so fair--She gazed at me, and still she hover'd there.

모모"Dost thou not know me?" were the words she said

모모In tones where love and faith were sweetly bound;"Knowest thou not Her who oftentimes hath shed

모모The purest balsam in each earthly wound?Thou knows't me well; thy panting heart I led

모모To join me in a bond with rapture crown'd.Did I not see thee, when a stripling, yearningTo welcome me with tears, heartfelt and burning?"

모모"Yes!" I exclaim'd, whilst, overcome with joy,

모모I sank to earth; "I long have worshipp'd thee;Thou gav'st me rest, when passions rack'd the boy,

모모Pervading ev'ry limb unceasingly;Thy heav'nly pinions thou didst then employ

모모The scorching sunbeams to ward off from me.From thee alone Earth's fairest gifts I gain'd,Through thee alone, true bliss can be obtain'd.

모모"Thy name I know not; yet I hear thee nam'd

모모By many a one who boasts thee as his own;Each eye believes that tow'rd thy form 'tis aim'd,

모모Yet to most eyes thy rays are anguish-sown.Ah! whilst I err'd, full many a friend I claim'd,

모모Now that I know thee, I am left alone;With but myself can I my rapture share,I needs must veil and hide thy radiance fair.

모모She smiled, and answering said: "Thou see'st how wise,

모모How prudent 'twas but little to unveil!Scarce from the clumsiest cheat are clear'd thine eyes,

모모Scarce hast thou strength thy childish bars to scale,When thou dost rank thee 'mongst the deities,

모모And so man's duties to perform would'st fail!How dost thou differ from all other men?Live with the world in peace, and know thee then!"

모모"Oh, pardon me," I cried, "I meant it well:

모모Not vainly did'st thou bless mine eyes with light;For in my blood glad aspirations swell,

모모The value of thy gifts I know aright!Those treasures in my breast for others dwell,

모모The buried pound no more I'll hide from sight.Why did I seek the road so anxiously,If hidden from my brethren 'twere to be?"

모모And as I answer'd, tow'rd me turn'd her face,

모모With kindly sympathy, that god-like one;Within her eye full plainly could I trace

모모What I had fail'd in, and what rightly done.She smiled, and cured me with that smile's sweet grace,

모모To new-born joys my spirit soar'd anon;With inward confidence I now could dareTo draw yet closer, and observe her there.

모모Through the light cloud she then stretch'd forth her hand,

모모As if to bid the streaky vapour fly:At once it seemed to yield to her command,

모모Contracted, and no mist then met mine eye.My glance once more survey'd the smiling land,

모모Unclouded and serene appear'd the sky.Nought but a veil of purest white she held,And round her in a thousand folds it swell'd.

모모"I know thee, and I know thy wav'ring will.

모모I know the good that lives and glows in thee!"--Thus spake she, and methinks I hear her still--

모모"The prize long destined, now receive from me;That blest one will be safe from ev'ry ill,

모모Who takes this gift with soul of purity,--"The veil of Minstrelsy from Truth's own hand,Of sunlight and of morn's sweet fragrance plann'd.

모모"And when thou and thy friends at fierce noon-day

모모Are parched with heat, straight cast it in the air!Then Zephyr's cooling breath will round you play,

모모Distilling balm and flowers' sweet incense there;The tones of earthly woe will die away,

모모The grave become a bed of clouds so fair,To sing to rest life's billows will be seen,The day be lovely, and the night serene."--

모모Come, then, my friends! and whensoe'er ye find

모모Upon your way increase life's heavy load;If by fresh-waken'd blessings flowers are twin'd

모모Around your path, and golden fruits bestow'd,We'll seek the coming day with joyous mind!

모모Thus blest, we'll live, thus wander on our roadAnd when our grandsons sorrow o'er our tomb,Our love, to glad their bosoms, still shall bloom.

모모SONGS.-----Late resounds the early strain;Weal and woe in song remain.-----SOUND, SWEET SONG.

모모SOUND, sweet song, from some far land,Sighing softly close at hand,

모모Now of joy, and now of woe!

모모Stars are wont to glimmer so.

모모Sooner thus will good unfold;Children young and children oldGladly hear thy numbers flow.

모모1820.*-----

모모* In the cases in which the date is marked thus (*), itsignifies the original date of publication--the year ofcomposition not being known. In other cases, the date given isthat of the actual composition. All the poems are arranged in theorder of the recognised German editions.-----TO THE KIND READER.

모모No one talks more than a Poet;Fain he'd have the people know it.

모모Praise or blame he ever loves;None in prose confess an error,Yet we do so, void of terror,

모모In the Muses' silent groves.

모모What I err'd in, what corrected,What I suffer'd, what effected,

모모To this wreath as flow'rs belong;For the aged, and the youthful,And the vicious, and the truthful,

모모All are fair when viewed in song.

모모1800.*-----THE NEW AMADIS.

모모IN my boyhood's days so drear

모모I was kept confined;There I sat for many a year,

모모All alone I pined,As within the womb.

모모Yet thou drov'st away my gloom,

모모Golden phantasy!I became a hero true,

모모Like the Prince Pipi,And the world roam'd through,

모모Many a crystal palace built,

모모Crush'd them with like art,And the Dragon's life-blood spilt

모모With my glitt'ring dart.Yes! I was a man!

모모Next I formed the knightly plan

모모Princess Fish to free;She was much too complaisant,

모모Kindly welcomed me,--And I was gallant.

모모Heav'nly bread her kisses proved,

모모Glowing as the wine;Almost unto death I loved.

모모Sun-s appeared to shineIn her dazzling charms.

모모Who hath torn her from mine arms?

모모Could no magic bandMake her in her flight delay?

모모Say, where now her land?Where, alas, the way?

모모1775.*-----WHEN THE FOX DIES, HIS SKIN COUNTS.*

모모(* The name of a game, known in English as "Jack's alight.")

모모WE young people in the shade

모모Sat one sultry day;Cupid came, and "Dies the Fox"

모모With us sought to play.

모모Each one of my friends then sat

모모By his mistress dear;Cupid, blowing out the torch,

모모Said: "The taper's here!"

모모Then we quickly sent around

모모The expiring brand;Each one put it hastily

모모ln his neighbour's hand.

모모Dorilis then gave it me,

모모With a scoffing jest;Sudden into flame it broke,

모모By my fingers press'd.

모모And it singed my eyes and face,

모모Set my breast on fire;Then above my head the blaze

모모Mounted ever higher.

모모Vain I sought to put it out;

모모Ever burned the flame;Stead of dying, soon the Fox

모모Livelier still became.

모모1770.-----THE HEATHROSE.

모모ONCE a boy a Rosebud spied,

모모Heathrose fair and tender,All array'd in youthful pride,--Quickly to the spot he hied,

모모Ravished by her splendour.Rosebud, rosebud, rosebud red,

모모Heathrose fair and tender!

모모Said the boy, "I'll now pick thee,

모모Heathrose fair and tender!"Said the rosebud, "I'll prick thee,So that thou'lt remember me,

모모Ne'er will I surrender!"Rosebud, rosebud, rosebud red,

모모Heathrose fair and tender!

모모Now the cruel boy must pick

모모Heathrose fair and tender;Rosebud did her best to prick,--Vain 'twas 'gainst her fate to kick--

모모She must needs surrender.Rosebud, rosebud, rosebud red,

모모Heathrose fair and tender!

모모1779.*-----BLINDMAN'S BUFF.

모모OH, my Theresa dear!Thine eyes, I greatly fear,

모모Can through the bandage see!Although thine eyes are bound,By thee I'm quickly found,

모모And wherefore shouldst thou catch but me?

모모Ere long thou held'st me fast,With arms around me cast,

모모Upon thy breast I fell;Scarce was thy bandage gone,When all my joy was flown,

모모Thou coldly didst the blind repel.

모모He groped on ev'ry side,His limbs he sorely tried,

모모While scoffs arose all round;If thou no love wilt give,In sadness I shall live,

모모As if mine eyes remain'd still bound.

모모1770.-----CHRISTEL.

모모My senses ofttimes are oppress'd,

모모Oft stagnant is my blood;But when by Christel's sight I'm blest,

모모I feel my strength renew'd.I see her here, I see her there,

모모And really cannot tellThe manner how, the when, the where,

모모The why I love her well.

모모If with the merest glance I view

모모Her black and roguish eyes,And gaze on her black eyebrows too,

모모My spirit upward flies.Has any one a mouth so sweet,

모모Such love-round cheeks as she?Ah, when the eye her beauties meet,

모모It ne'er content can be.

모모And when in airy German dance

모모I clasp her form divine,So quick we whirl, so quick advance,

모모What rapture then like mine!And when she's giddy, and feels warm,

모모I cradle her, poor thing,Upon my breast, and in mine arm,--

모모I'm then a very king!

모모And when she looks with love on me,

모모Forgetting all but this,When press'd against my bosom, she

모모Exchanges kiss for kiss,All through my marrow runs a thrill,

모모Runs e'en my foot along!I feel so well, I feel so ill,

모모I feel so weak, so strong!

모모Would that such moments ne'er would end!

모모The day ne'er long I find;Could I the night too with her spend,

모모E'en that I should not mind.If she were in mine arms but held,

모모To quench love's thirst I'd try;And could my torments not be quell'd,

모모Upon her breast would die.

모모1776.*------THE COY ONE.

모모ONE Spring-morning bright and fair,

모모Roam'd a shepherdess and sang;Young and beauteous, free from care,

모모Through the fields her clear notes rang:So, Ia, Ia! le ralla, &c.

모모Of his lambs some two or three

모모Thyrsis offer'd for a kiss;First she eyed him roguishly,

모모Then for answer sang but this:So, Ia, Ia! le ralla, &c.

모모Ribbons did the next one offer,

모모And the third, his heart so trueBut, as with the lambs, the scoffer

모모Laugh'd at heart and ribbons too,--Still 'twas Ia! le ralla, &c.

모모1791.-----THE CONVERT.

모모As at sunset I was straying

모모Silently the wood along,Damon on his flute was playing,

모모And the rocks gave back the song,So la, Ia! &c.

모모Softly tow'rds him then he drew me;

모모Sweet each kiss he gave me then!And I said, "Play once more to me!"

모모And he kindly play'd again,So la, la! &c.

모모All my peace for aye has fleeted,

모모All my happiness has flown;Yet my ears are ever greeted

모모With that olden, blissful tone,So la, la! &c.

모모1791.-----PRESERVATION.

모모My maiden she proved false to me;

모모To hate all joys I soon began,

모모Then to a flowing stream I ran,--The stream ran past me hastily.

모모There stood I fix'd, in mute despair;

모모My head swam round as in a dream;

모모I well-nigh fell into the stream,And earth seem'd with me whirling there.

모모Sudden I heard a voice that cried--

모모I had just turn'd my face from thence--

모모It was a voice to charm each sense:"Beware, for deep is yonder tide!"

모모A thrill my blood pervaded now,

모모I look'd and saw a beauteous maid

모모I asked her name--twas Kate, she said--"Oh lovely Kate! how kind art thou!

모모"From death I have been sav'd by thee,

모모'Tis through thee only that I live;

모모Little 'twere life alone to give,My joy in life then deign to be!"

모모And then I told my sorrows o'er,

모모Her eyes to earth she sweetly threw;

모모I kiss'd her, and she kiss'd me too,And--then I talked of death no more.

모모1775.*-----THE MUSES' SON.

모모[Goethe quotes the beginning of this song in his Autobiography,as expressing the manner in which his poetical effusions used topour out from him.]

모모THROUGH field and wood to stray,And pipe my tuneful lay,--

모모'Tis thus my days are pass'd;And all keep tune with me,And move in harmony,

모모And so on, to the last.

모모To wait I scarce have powerThe garden's earliest flower,

모모The tree's first bloom in Spring;They hail my joyous strain,--When Winter comes again,

모모Of that sweet dream I sing.

모모My song sounds far and near,O'er ice it echoes clear,

모모Then Winter blossoms bright;And when his blossoms fly,Fresh raptures meet mine eye,

모모Upon the well-till'd height.

모모When 'neath the linden tree,Young folks I chance to see,

모모I set them moving soon;His nose the dull lad curls,The formal maiden whirls,

모모Obedient to my tune.

모모Wings to the feet ye lend,O'er hill and vale ye send

모모The lover far from home;When shall I, on your breast,.

모모Ye kindly muses, rest,And cease at length to roam?

모모1800.*------FOUND.

모모ONCE through the forest

모모Alone I went;To seek for nothing

모모My thoughts were bent.

모모I saw i' the shadow

모모A flower stand thereAs stars it glisten'd,

모모As eyes 'twas fair.

모모I sought to pluck it,--

모모It gently said:"Shall I be gather'd

모모Only to fade?"

모모With all its roots

모모I dug it with care,And took it home

모모To my garden fair.

모모In silent corner

모모Soon it was set;There grows it ever,

모모There blooms it yet.

모모1815.*-----LIKE AND LIKE.

모모A FAIR bell-flower

모모Sprang tip from the ground;And early its fragrance

모모It shed all around;A bee came thither

모모And sipp'd from its bell;That they for each other

모모Were made, we see well.

모모1814.-----RECIPROCAL INVITATION TO THE DANCE.

모모THE INDIFFERENT.

모모COME to the dance with me, come with me, fair one!

모모Dances a feast-day like this may well crown.If thou my sweetheart art not, thou canst be so,

모모But if thou wilt not, we still will dance on.Come to the dance with me, come with me, fair one!

모모Dances a feast-day like this may well crown.

모모THE TENDER.

모모Loved one, without thee, what then would all feast be?

모모Sweet one, without thee, what then were the dance?If thou my sweetheart wert not, I would dance not.

모모If thou art still so, all life is one feast.Loved one, without thee, what then would all feasts be?

모모Sweet one, without thee, what then were the dance?

모모THE INDIFFERENT.

모모Let them but love, then, and leave us the dancing!

모모Languishing love cannot bear the glad dance.Let us whirl round in the waltz's gay measure,

모모And let them steal to the dim-lighted wood.Let them but love, then, and leave us the dancing!

모모Languishing love cannot bear the glad dance.

모모THE TENDER.

모모Let them whirl round, then, and leave us to wander!

모모Wand'ring to love is a heavenly dance.Cupid, the near one, o'erhears their deriding,

모모Vengeance takes suddenly, vengeance takes soon.Let them whirl round, then, and leave us to wander!

모모Wand'ring to love is a heavenly dance.

모모1789.*-----SELF-DECEIT.

모모My neighbour's curtain, well I see,

모모Is moving to and fin.No doubt she's list'ning eagerly,

모모If I'm at home or no.

모모And if the jealous grudge I bore

모모And openly confess'd,Is nourish'd by me as before,

모모Within my inmost breast.

모모Alas! no fancies such as these

모모E'er cross'd the dear child's thoughts.I see 'tis but the ev'ning breeze

모모That with the curtain sports.

모모1803.-----DECLARATION OF WAR.

모모OH, would I resembled

모모The country girls fair,Who rosy-red ribbons

모모And yellow hats wear!

모모To believe I was pretty

모모I thought was allow'd;In the town I believed it

모모When by the youth vow'd.

모모Now that Spring hath return'd,

모모All my joys disappear;The girls of the country

모모Have lured him from here.

모모To change dress and figure,

모모Was needful I found,My bodice is longer,

모모My petticoat round.

모모My hat now is yellow.

모모My bodice like snow;The clover to sickle

모모With others I go.

모모Something pretty, e'er long

모모Midst the troop he explores;The eager boy signs me

모모To go within doors.

모모I bashfully go,--

모모Who I am, he can't trace;He pinches my cheeks,

모모And he looks in my face.

모모The town girl now threatens

모모You maidens with war;Her twofold charms pledges .

모모Of victory are.

모모1803.-----LOVER IN ALL SHAPES.

모모To be like a fish,Brisk and quick, is my wish;If thou cam'st with thy line.Thou wouldst soon make me thine.To be like a fish,Brisk and quick, is my wish.

모모Oh, were I a steed!Thou wouldst love me indeed.Oh, were I a carFit to bear thee afar!Oh, were I a steed!Thou wouldst love me indeed.

모모I would I were goldThat thy fingers might hold!If thou boughtest aught then,I'd return soon again.I would I were goldThat thy fingers might hold!

모모I would I were true,And my sweetheart still new!To be faithful I'd swear,And would go away ne'er.I would I were true,And my sweetheart still new!

모모I would I were old,And wrinkled and cold,So that if thou said'st No,I could stand such a blow!I would I were old,And wrinkled and cold.

모모An ape I would be,Full of mischievous glee;If aught came to vex thee,I'd plague and perplex thee.An ape I would be,Full of mischievous glee

모모As a lamb I'd behave,As a lion be brave,As a lynx clearly see,As a fox cunning be.As a lamb I'd behave,As a lion be brave.

모모Whatever I were,All on thee I'd confer;With the gifts of a princeMy affection evince.Whatever I were,All on thee I'd confer.

모모As nought diff'rent can make me,As I am thou must take me!If I'm not good enough,Thou must cut thine own stuff.As nought diff'rent can make me,As I am thou must take me!

모모1815.*-----THE GOLDSMITH'S APPRENTICE.

모모My neighbour, none can e'er deny,

모모Is a most beauteous maid;Her shop is ever in mine eye,

모모When working at my trade.

모모To ring and chain I hammer then

모모The wire of gold assay'd,And think the while: "For Kate, oh when

모모Will such a ring be made?"

모모And when she takes her shutters down,

모모Her shop at once invade,To buy and haggle, all the town,

모모For all that's there displayd.

모모I file, and maybe overfile

모모The wire of gold assay'd;My master grumbles all the while,--

모모Her shop the mischief made.

모모To ply her wheel she straight begins,

모모When not engaged in trade;I know full well for what she spins,--

모모'Tis hope guides that dear maid.

모모Her leg, while her small foot treads on,

모모Is in my mind portray'd;Her garter I recall anon,--

모모I gave it that dear maid.

모모Then to her lips the finest thread

모모Is by her hand convey'd.Were I there only in its stead,

모모How I would kiss the maid!

모모1808.-----ANSWERS IN A GAME OF QUESTIONS.

모모THE LADY.

모모IN the small and great world too,

모모What most charms a woman's heart?It is doubtless what is new,

모모For its blossoms joy impart;Nobler far is what is true,

모모For fresh blossoms it can shoot

모모Even in the time of fruit.

모모THE YOUNG GENTLEMAN.

모모With the Nymphs in wood and cave

모모Paris was acquainted well,Till Zeus sent, to make him rave,

모모Three of those in Heav'n who dwell;And the choice more trouble gave

모모Than e'er fell to mortal lot,

모모Whether in old times or not.

모모THE EXPERIENCED.

모모Tenderly a woman view,

모모And thoult win her, take my word;He who's quick and saucy too,

모모Will of all men be preferr'd;Who ne'er seems as if he knew

모모If he pleases, if he charms,--

모모He 'tis injures, he 'tis harms.

모모THE CONTENTED.

모모Manifold is human strife,

모모Human passion, human pain;Many a blessing yet is rife,

모모Many pleasures still remain.Yet the greatest bliss in life,

모모And the richest prize we find,

모모Is a good, contented mind.

모모THE MERRY COUNSEL.

모모He by whom man's foolish will

모모Is each day review'd and blamed,Who, when others fools are still,

모모Is himself a fool proclaim'd,--Ne'er at mill was beast's back press'd

모모With a heavier load than he.What I feel within my breast

모모That in truth's the thing for me!

모모1789.-----DIFFERENT EMOTIONS ON THE SAME SPOT.

모모THE MAIDEN.

모모I'VE seen him before me!What rapture steals o'er me!

모모Oh heavenly sight!He's coming to meet me;Perplex'd, I retreat me,

모모With shame take to flight.My mind seems to wander!Ye rocks and trees yonder,

모모Conceal ye my rapture.

모모Conceal my delight!

모모THE YOUTH.

모모'Tis here I must find her,'Twas here she enshrined her,

모모Here vanish'd from sight.She came, as to meet me,Then fearing to greet me,

모모With shame took to flight.Is't hope? Do I wander?Ye rocks and trees yonder,

모모Disclose ye the loved one,

모모Disclose my delight!

모모THE LANGUISHING.

모모O'er my sad, fate I sorrow,To each dewy morrow,

모모Veil'd here from man's sightBy the many mistaken,Unknown and forsaken,

모모Here I wing my flight!Compassionate spirit!Let none ever hear it,--

모모Conceal my affliction,

모모Conceal thy delight!

모모THE HUNTER.

모모To-day I'm rewarded;Rich booty's afforded

모모By Fortune so bright.My servant the pheasants,And hares fit for presents

모모Takes homeward at night;Here see I enrapturedIn nets the birds captured!--

모모Long life to the hunter!

모모Long live his delight!

모모1789.-----WHO'LL BUY GODS OF LOVE?

모모OF all the beauteous waresExposed for sale at fairs,None will give more delightThan those that to your sightFrom distant lands we bring.Oh, hark to what we sing!These beauteous birds behold,They're brought here to be sold.

모모And first the big one see,So full of roguish glee!With light and merry boundHe leaps upon the ground;Then springs up on the bougd,We will not praise him now.The merry bird behold,--He's brought here to be sold.

모모And now the small one see!A modest look has he,And yet he's such apotherAs his big roguish brother.'Tis chiefly when all's stillHe loves to show his will.The bird so small and bold,--He's brought here to be sold.

모모Observe this little love,This darling turtle dove!All maidens are so neat,So civil, so discreetLet them their charms set loose,And turn your love to use;The gentle bird behold,--She's brought here to be sold.

모모Their praises we won't tell;They'll stand inspection well.They're fond of what is new,--And yet, to show they're true,Nor seal nor letter's wanted;To all have wings been granted.The pretty birds behold,--Such beauties ne'er were sold!

모모1795.-----THE MISANTHROPE.

모모AT first awhile sits he,

모모With calm, unruffled brow;His features then I see,Distorted hideously,--

모모An owl's they might be now.

모모What is it, askest thou?Is't love, or is't ennui?

모모'Tis both at once, I vow.

모모1767-9.-----DIFFERENT THREATS.

모모I ONCE into a forest far

모모My maiden went to seek,And fell upon her neck, when: "Ah!"

모모She threaten'd, "I will shriek!"

모모Then cried I haughtily: "I'll crush

모모The man that dares come near thee!""Hush!" whisper'd she: "My loved one, hush!

모모Or else they'll overhear thee!"

모모1767-9.-----MAIDEN WISHES.

모모WHAT pleasure to meA bridegroom would be!When married we are,They call us mamma.No need then to sew,To school we ne'er go;Command uncontroll'd,Have maids, whom to scold;Choose clothes at our ease,Of what tradesmen we please;Walk freely about,And go to each rout,And unrestrained areBy papa or mamma.

모모1767-9.-----MOTIVES.

모모IF to a girl who loves us trulyHer mother gives instruction dulyIn virtue, duty, and what not,--And if she hearkens ne'er a jot,But with fresh-strengthen'd longing flies

모모To meet our kiss that seems to burn,--

모모Caprice has just as much concernedAs love in her bold enterprise.

모모But if her mother can succeedIn gaining for her maxims heed,And softening the girl's heart too,So that she coyly shuns our view,--The heart of youth she knows but ill;

모모For when a maiden is thus stern,

모모Virtue in truth has less concernIn this, than an inconstant will.

모모1767-9.-----TRUE ENJOYMENT.

모모VAINLY wouldst thou, to gain a heart,

모모Heap up a maiden's lap with gold;The joys of love thou must impart,

모모Wouldst thou e'er see those joys unfold.The voices of the throng gold buys,

모모No single heart 'twill win for thee;Wouldst thou a maiden make thy prize,

모모Thyself alone the bribe must be.

모모If by no sacred tie thou'rt bound,

모모Oh youth, thou must thyself restrain!Well may true liberty be found,

모모Tho' man may seem to wear a chain.Let one alone inflame thee e'er,

모모And if her heart with love o'erflows,Let tenderness unite you there,

모모If duty's self no fetter knows.

모모First feel, oh youth! A girl then find

모모Worthy thy choice,--let her choose thee,In body fair, and fair in mind,

모모And then thou wilt be blessed, like me.I who have made this art mine own,

모모A girl have chosen such as thisThe blessing of the priest alone

모모Is wanting to complete our bliss.

모모Nought but my rapture is her guide,

모모Only for me she cares to please,--Ne'er wanton save when by my side,

모모And modest when the world she sees;That time our glow may never chill,

모모She yields no right through frailty;Her favour is a favour still,

모모And I must ever grateful be.

모모Yet I'm content, and full of joy,

모모If she'll but grant her smile so sweet,Or if at table she'll employ,

모모To pillow hers, her lover's feet,Give me the apple that she bit,

모모The glass from which she drank, bestow,And when my kiss so orders it,

모모Her bosom, veil'd till then, will show.

모모And when she wills of love to speak,

모모In fond and silent hours of bliss,Words from her mouth are all I seek,

모모Nought else I crave,--not e'en a kiss.With what a soul her mind is fraught,

모모Wreath'd round with charms unceasingly!She's perfect,--and she fails in nought

모모Save in her deigning to love me.

모모My rev'rence throws me at her feet,

모모My longing throws me on her breast;This, youth, is rapture true and sweet,

모모Be wise, thus seeking to be blest.When death shall take thee from her side,

모모To join the angelic choir above,In heaven's bright mansions to abide,--No diff'rence at the change thoult prove.

모모1767-8.-----THE FAREWELL.

모모[Probably addressed to his mistress Frederica.]

모모LET mine eye the farewell say,

모모That my lips can utter ne'er;Fain I'd be a man to-day,

모모Yet 'tis hard, oh, hard to bear!

모모Mournful in an hour like this

모모Is love's sweetest pledge, I ween;Cold upon thy mouth the kiss,

모모Faint thy fingers' pressure e'en.

모모Oh what rapture to my heart

모모Used each stolen kiss to bring!As the violets joy impart,

모모Gather'd in the early spring.

모모Now no garlands I entwine,

모모Now no roses pluck. for thee,Though 'tis springtime, Fanny mine,

모모Dreary autumn 'tis to me!

모모1771.-----THE BEAUTIFUL NIGHT.

모모Now I leave this cottage lowly,

모모Where my love hath made her home,And with silent footstep slowly

모모Through the darksome forest roam,Luna breaks through oaks and bushes,

모모Zephyr hastes her steps to meet,And the waving birch-tree blushes,

모모Scattering round her incense sweet.

모모Grateful are the cooling breezes

모모Of this beauteous summer night,Here is felt the charm that pleases,

모모And that gives the soul delight.Boundless is my joy; yet, Heaven,

모모Willingly I'd leave to theeThousand such nights, were one given

모모By my maiden loved to me!

모모1767-8.-----HAPPINESS AND VISION.

모모TOGETHER at the altar weIn vision oft were seen by thee,

모모Thyself as bride, as bridegroom I.Oft from thy mouth full many a kissIn an unguarded hour of bliss

모모I then would steal, while none were by.

모모The purest rapture we then knew,The joy those happy hours gave too,

모모When tasted, fled, as time fleets on.What now avails my joy to me?Like dreams the warmest kisses flee,

모모Like kisses, soon all joys are gone.

모모1767-8.-----LIVING REMEMBRANCE.

모모HALF vex'd, half pleased, thy love will feel,Shouldst thou her knot or ribbon steal;To thee they're much--I won't conceal;

모모Such self-deceit may pardon'd be;A veil, a kerchief, garter, rings,In truth are no mean trifling things,

모모But still they're not enough for me.

모모She who is dearest to my heart,Gave me, with well dissembled smart,Of her own life, a living part,

모모No charm in aught beside I trace;How do I scorn thy paltry ware!A lock she gave me of the hair

모모That wantons o'er her beauteous face.

모모If, loved one, we must sever'd be,Wouldst thou not wholly fly from me,I still possess this legacy,

모모To look at, and to kiss in play.--My fate is to the hair's allied,We used to woo her with like pride,

모모And now we both are far away.

모모Her charms with equal joy we press'd,Her swelling cheeks anon caress'd,Lured onward by a yearning blest,

모모Upon her heaving bosom fell.Oh rival, free from envy's sway,Thou precious gift, thou beauteous prey.

모모Remain my joy and bliss to tell!

모모1767-9.-----THE BLISS OF ABSENCE.

모모DRINK, oh youth, joy's purest rayFrom thy loved one's eyes all day,

모모And her image paint at night!Better rule no lover knows,Yet true rapture greater grows,

모모When far sever'd from her sight.

모모Powers eternal, distance, time,Like the might of stars sublime,

모모Gently rock the blood to rest,O'er my senses softness steals,Yet my bosom lighter feels,

모모And I daily am more blest.

모모Though I can forget her ne'er,Yet my mind is free from care,

모모I can calmly live and move;Unperceived infatuationLonging turns to adoration,

모모Turns to reverence my love.

모모Ne'er can cloud, however light,Float in ether's regions bright,

모모When drawn upwards by the sun,As my heart in rapturous calm.Free from envy and alarm,

모모Ever love I her alone!

모모1767-9.-----TO LUNA.

모모SISTER of the first-born light,

모모Type of sorrowing gentleness!

모모Quivering mists in silv'ry dressFloat around thy features bright;When thy gentle foot is heard,

모모From the day-closed caverns then

모모Wake the mournful ghosts of men,I, too, wake, and each night-bird.

모모O'er a field of boundless span

모모Looks thy gaze both far and wide.

모모Raise me upwards to thy side!Grant this to a raving man!And to heights of rapture raised,

모모Let the knight so crafty peep

모모At his maiden while asleep,Through her lattice-window glazed.

모모Soon the bliss of this sweet view,

모모Pangs by distance caused allays;

모모And I gather all thy rays,And my look I sharpen too.Round her unveil'd limbs I see

모모Brighter still become the glow,

모모And she draws me down below,As Endymion once drew thee.

모모1767-9.-----THE WEDDING NIGHT.

모모WITHIN the chamber, far away

모모From the glad feast, sits Love in dreadLest guests disturb, in wanton play,

모모The silence of the bridal bed.His torch's pale flame serves to gild

모모The scene with mystic sacred glow;The room with incense-clouds is fil'd,

모모That ye may perfect rapture know.

모모How beats thy heart, when thou dost hear

모모The chime that warns thy guests to fly!How glow'st thou for those lips so dear,

모모That soon are mute, and nought deny!With her into the holy place

모모Thou hast'nest then, to perfect all;The fire the warder's hands embrace,

모모Grows, like a night-light, dim and small.

모모How heaves her bosom, and how burns

모모Her face at every fervent kiss!Her coldness now to trembling turns,

모모Thy daring now a duty is.Love helps thee to undress her fast,

모모But thou art twice as fast as he;And then he shuts both eye at last,

모모With sly and roguish modesty.

모모1767.-----MISCHIEVOUS JOY.

모모AS a butterfly renew'd,

모모When in life I breath'd my last,

모모To the spots my flight I wing,

모모Scenes of heav'nly rapture past,

모모Over meadows, to the spring,Round the hill, and through the wood.

모모Soon a tender pair I spy,

모모And I look down from my seat

모모On the beauteous maiden's head--

모모When embodied there I meet

모모All I lost as soon as dead,Happy as before am I.

모모Him she clasps with silent smile,

모모And his mouth the hour improves,

모모Sent by kindly Deities;

모모First from breast to mouth it roves,

모모Then from mouth to hands it flies,And I round him sport the while.

모모And she sees me hov'ring near;

모모Trembling at her lovers rapture,

모모Up she springs--I fly away,

모모"Dearest! let's the insect capture

모모Come! I long to make my preyYonder pretty little dear!"

모모1767-9.-----APPARENT DEATH.

모모WEEP, maiden, weep here o'er the tomb of Love;

모모He died of nothing--by mere chance was slain.But is he really dead?--oh, that I cannot prove:

모모A nothing, a mere chance, oft gives him life again.

모모1767-9.-----NOVEMBER SONG.

모모To the great archer--not to him

모모To meet whom flies the sun,And who is wont his features dim

모모With clouds to overrun--

모모But to the boy be vow'd these rhymes,

모모Who 'mongst the roses plays,Who hear us, and at proper times

모모To pierce fair hearts essays.

모모Through him the gloomy winter night,

모모Of yore so cold and drear,Brings many a loved friend to our sight,

모모And many a woman dear.

모모Henceforward shall his image fair

모모Stand in yon starry skies,And, ever mild and gracious there,

모모Alternate set and rise.

모모1815.*-----TO THE CHOSEN ONE.[This sweet song is doubtless one of those addressed toFrederica.]

모모HAND in hand! and lip to lip!

모모Oh, be faithful, maiden dear!Fare thee well! thy lover's ship

모모Past full many a rock must steersBut should he the haven see,

모모When the storm has ceased to break,And be happy, reft of thee,--

모모May the Gods fierce vengeance take!

모모Boldly dared is well nigh won!

모모Half my task is solved aright;Ev'ry star's to me a sun,

모모Only cowards deem it night.Stood I idly by thy side,

모모Sorrow still would sadden me;But when seas our paths divide,

모모Gladly toil I,--toil for thee!

모모Now the valley I perceive,

모모Where together we will go,And the streamlet watch each eve,

모모Gliding peacefully belowOh, the poplars on yon spot!

모모Oh, the beech trees in yon grove!And behind we'll build a cot,

모모Where to taste the joys of love!

모모1771.-----FIRST LOSS.

모모AH! who'll e'er those days restore,

모모Those bright days of early loveWho'll one hour again concede,

모모Of that time so fondly cherish'd!Silently my wounds I feed,And with wailing evermore

모모Sorrow o'er each joy now perish'd.Ah! who'll e'er the days restore

모모Of that time so fondly cherish'd.

모모1789.*-----AFTER-SENSATIONS.

모모WHEN the vine again is blowing,

모모Then the wine moves in the cask;When the rose again is glowing,

모모Wherefore should I feel oppress'd?

모모Down my cheeks run tears all-burning,

모모If I do, or leave my task;I but feel a speechless yearning,

모모That pervades my inmost breast.

모모But at length I see the reason,

모모When the question I would ask:'Twas in such a beauteous season,

모모Doris glowed to make me blest!

모모1797.-----PROXIMITY OF THE BELOVED ONE.

모모I THINK of thee, whene'er the sun his beams

모모O'er ocean flings;I think  of thee, whene'er the moonlight gleams

모모In silv'ry springs.

모모I see thee, when upon the distant ridge

모모The dust awakes;At midnight's hour, when on the fragile bridge

모모The wanderer quakes.

모모I hear thee, when yon billows rise on high,

모모With murmur deep.To tread the silent grove oft wander I,

모모When all's asleep.

모모I'm near thee, though thou far away mayst be--

모모Thou, too, art near!The sun then sets, the stars soon lighten me.

모모Would thou wert here!

모모1795.-----PRESENCE.

모모ALL things give token of thee!As soon as the bright sun is shining,Thou too wilt follow, I trust.

모모When in the garden thou walk'st,Thou then art the rose of all roses,Lily of lilies as well.

모모When thou dost move in the dance,Then each constellation moves also;With thee and round thee they move.

모모Night! oh, what bliss were the night!For then thou o'ershadow'st the lustre,Dazzling and fair, of the moon.

모모Dazzling and beauteous art thou,And flowers, and moon, and the planetsHomage pay, Sun, but to thee.

모모Sun! to me also be thouCreator of days bright and glorious;Life and Eternity this!

모모1813.-----TO THE DISTANT ONE.

모모AND have I lost thee evermore?

모모Hast thou, oh fair one, from me flown?Still in mine ear sounds, as of yore,

모모Thine ev'ry word, thine ev'ry tone.

모모As when at morn the wand'rer's eye

모모Attempts to pierce the air in vain,When, hidden in the azure sky,

모모The lark high o'er him chaunts his strain:

모모So do I cast my troubled gaze

모모Through bush, through forest, o'er the lea;Thou art invoked by all my lays;

모모Oh, come then, loved one, back to me!

모모1789.*-----BY THE RIVER.

모모FLOW on, ye lays so loved, so fair,

모모On to Oblivion's ocean flow!May no rapt boy recall you e'er,

모모No maiden in her beauty's glow!

모모My love alone was then your theme,

모모But now she scorns my passion true.Ye were but written in the stream;

모모As it flows on, then, flow ye too!

모모1798.*-----FAREWELL.

모모To break one's word is pleasure-fraught,

모모To do one's duty gives a smart;While man, alas! will promise nought,

모모That is repugnant to his heart.

모모Using some magic strains of yore,

모모Thou lurest him, when scarcely calm,On to sweet folly's fragile bark once more,

모모Renewing, doubling chance of harm.

모모Why seek to hide thyself from me?

모모Fly not my sight--be open then!Known late or early it must be,

모모And here thou hast thy word again.

모모My duty is fulfill'd to-day,

모모No longer will I guard thee from surprise;But, oh, forgive the friend who from thee turns away,

모모And to himself for refuge flies!

모모1797.-----THE EXCHANGE.

모모THE stones in the streamlet I make my bright pillow,And open my arms to the swift-rolling billow,

모모That lovingly hastens to fall on my breast.Then fickleness soon bids it onwards be flowing;A second draws nigh, its caresses bestowing,--

모모And so by a twofold enjoyment I'm blest.

모모And yet thou art trailing in sorrow and sadnessThe moments that life, as it flies, gave for gladness,

모모Because by thy love thou'rt remember'd no more!Oh, call back to mind former days and their blisses!The lips of the second will give as sweet kisses

모모As any the lips of the first gave before!

모모1767-9.-----WELCOME AND FAREWELL.

모모[Another of the love-songs addressed to Frederica.]

모모QUICK throbb'd my heart: to norse! haste, haste,

모모And lo! 'twas done with speed of light;The evening soon the world embraced,

모모And o'er the mountains hung the night.Soon stood, in robe of mist, the oak,

모모A tow'ring giant in his size,Where darkness through the thicket broke,

모모And glared with hundred gloomy eyes.

모모From out a hill of clouds the moon

모모With mournful gaze began to peer:The winds their soft wings flutter'd soon,

모모And murmur'd in mine awe-struck ear;The night a thousand monsters made,

모모Yet fresh and joyous was my mind;What fire within my veins then play'd!

모모What glow was in my bosom shrin'd!

모모I saw thee, and with tender pride

모모Felt thy sweet gaze pour joy on me;While all my heart was at thy side.

모모And every breath I breath'd for thee.The roseate hues that spring supplies

모모Were playing round thy features fair,And love for me--ye Deities!

모모I hoped it, I deserved it ne'er!

모모But, when the morning sun return'd,

모모Departure filled with grief my heart:Within thy kiss, what rapture burn'd!

모모But in thy look, what bitter smart!I went--thy gaze to earth first roved

모모Thou follow'dst me with tearful eye:And yet, what rapture to be loved!

모모And, Gods, to love--what ecstasy!

모모1771.-----NEW LOVE, NEW LIFE.

모모[Written at the time of Goethe's connection with Lily.]

모모HEART! my heart! what means this feeling?

모모What oppresseth thee so sore?What strange life is o'er me stealing!

모모I acknowledge thee no more.Fled is all that gave thee gladness,Fled the cause of all thy sadness,

모모Fled thy peace, thine industry--

모모Ah, why suffer it to be?

모모Say, do beauty's graces youthful,

모모Does this form so fair and bright,Does this gaze, so kind, so truthful,

모모Chain thee with unceasing might?Would I tear me from her boldly,Courage take, and fly her coldly,

모모Back to her. I'm forthwith led

모모By the path I seek to tread.

모모By a thread I ne'er can sever,

모모For 'tis 'twined with magic skill,Doth the cruel maid for ever

모모Hold me fast against my will.While those magic chains confine me,To her will I must resign me.

모모Ah, the change in truth is great!

모모Love! kind love! release me straight!

모모1775.-----TO BELINDA.

모모[This song was also written for Lily. Goethe mentions, at the endof his Autobiography, that he overheard her singing it oneevening after he had taken his last farewell of her.]

모모WHEREFORE drag me to yon glittering eddy,

모모With resistless might?Was I, then, not truly blest already

모모In the silent night?

모모In my secret chamber refuge taking,

모모'Neath the moon's soft ray,And her awful light around me breaking,

모모Musing there I lay.

모모And I dream'd of hours with joy o'erflowing,

모모Golden, truly blest,While thine image so beloved was glowing

모모Deep within my breast.

모모Now to the card-table hast thou bound me,

모모'Midst the torches glare?Whilst unhappy faces are around me,

모모Dost thou hold me there?

모모Spring-flow'rs are to me more rapture-giving,

모모Now conceal'd from view;Where thou, angel, art, is Nature living,

모모Love and kindness too.

모모1775.-----MAY SONG.

모모How fair doth Nature

모모Appear again!How bright the sunbeams!

모모How smiles the plain!

모모The flow'rs are bursting

모모From ev'ry bough,And thousand voices

모모Each bush yields now.

모모And joy and gladness

모모Fill ev'ry breast!Oh earth!--oh sunlight!

모모Oh rapture blest!

모모Oh love! oh loved one!

모모As golden bright,As clouds of morning

모모On yonder height!

모모Thou blessest gladly

모모The smiling field,--The world in fragrant

모모Vapour conceal'd.

모모Oh maiden, maiden,

모모How love I thee!Thine eye, how gleams it!

모모How lov'st thou me!

모모The blithe lark loveth

모모Sweet song and air,The morning flow'ret

모모Heav'n's incense fair,

모모As I now love thee

모모With fond desire,For thou dost give me

모모Youth, joy, and fire,

모모For new-born dances

모모And minstrelsy.Be ever happy,

모모As thou lov'st me!

모모1775.*-----WITH A PAINTED RIBBON.

모모LITTLE leaves and flow'rets too,

모모Scatter we with gentle hand,Kind young spring-gods to the view,

모모Sporting on an airy band.

모모Zephyr, bear it on the wing,

모모Twine it round my loved one's dress;To her glass then let her spring,

모모Full of eager joyousness.

모모Roses round her let her see,

모모She herself a youthful rose.Grant, dear life, one look to me!

모모'Twill repay me all my woes,

모모What this bosom feels, feel thou.

모모Freely offer me thy hand;Let the band that joins us now

모모Be no fragile rosy band!

모모1770.-----WITH A GOLDEN NECKLACE.

모모THIS page a chain to bring thee burns,

모모That, train'd to suppleness of old,On thy fair neck to nestle, yearns,

모모In many a hundred little fold.

모모To please the silly thing consent!

모모'Tis harmless, and from boldness free;By day a trifling ornament,

모모At night 'tis cast aside by thee.

모모But if the chain they bring thee ever,

모모Heavier, more fraught with weal or woe,I'd then, Lisette, reproach thee never

모모If thou shouldst greater scruples show.

모모1775.*-----ON THE LAKE,

모모[Written on the occasion of Goethe's starting with his friendPassavant on a Swiss Tour.]

모모I DRINK fresh nourishment, new blood

모모From out this world more free;The Nature is so kind and good

모모That to her breast clasps me!The billows toss our bark on high,

모모And with our oars keep time,While cloudy mountains tow'rd the sky

모모Before our progress climb.

모모Say, mine eye, why sink'st thou down?Golden visions, are ye flown?

모모Hence, thou dream, tho' golden-twin'd;

모모Here, too, love and life I find.

모모Over the waters are blinking

모모Many a thousand fair star;Gentle mists are drinking

모모Round the horizon afar.Round the shady creek lightly

모모Morning zephyrs awake,And the ripen'd fruit brightly

모모Mirrors itself in the lake.

모모1775.-----FROM THE MOUNTAIN.

모모[Written just after the preceding one, on a mountain overlookingthe Lake of Zurich.]

모모IF I, dearest Lily, did not love thee,

모모How this prospect would enchant my sight!And yet if I, Lily, did not love thee,

모모Could I find, or here, or there, delight?

모모1775.-----FLOWER-SALUTE.

모모THIS nosegay,--'twas I dress'd it,--

모모Greets thee a thousand times!Oft stoop'd I, and caress'd it,

모모Ah! full a thousand times,And 'gainst my bosom press'd it

모모A hundred thousand times!

모모1815.*-----IN SUMMER.

모모How plain and heightWith dewdrops are bright!How pearls have crown'dThe plants all around!How sighs the breezeThro' thicket and trees!How loudly in the sun's clear raysThe sweet birds carol forth their lays!

모모But, ah! above,Where saw I my love,Within her room,Small, mantled in gloom,Enclosed around,Where sunlight was drown'd,How little there was earth to me,With all its beauteous majesty!

모모1776.*-----MAY SONG.

모모BETWEEN wheatfield and corn,Between hedgerow and thorn,Between pasture and tree,Where's my sweetheartTell it me!

모모Sweetheart caught I

모모Not at home;She's then, thought I.

모모Gone to roam.Fair and loving

모모Blooms sweet May;Sweetheart's roving,

모모Free and gay.

모모By the rock near the wave,Where her first kiss she gave,On the greensward, to me,--Something I see!Is it she?

모모1812.-----PREMATURE SPRING.

모모DAYS full of rapture,

모모Are ye renew'd ?--Smile in the sunlight

모모Mountain and wood?

모모Streams richer laden

모모Flow through the dale,Are these the meadows?

모모Is this the vale?

모모Coolness cerulean!

모모Heaven and height!Fish crowd the ocean,

모모Golden and bright.

모모Birds of gay plumage

모모Sport in the grove,Heavenly numbers

모모Singing above.

모모Under the verdure's

모모Vigorous bloom,Bees, softly bumming,

모모Juices consume.

모모Gentle disturbance

모모Quivers in air,Sleep-causing fragrance,

모모Motion so fair.

모모Soon with more power

모모Rises the breeze,Then in a moment

모모Dies in the trees.

모모But to the bosom

모모Comes it again.Aid me, ye Muses,

모모Bliss to sustain!

모모Say what has happen'd

모모Since yester e'en?Oh, ye fair sisters,

모모Her I have seen!

모모1802.-----AUTUMN FEELINGS.

모모FLOURISH greener, as ye clamber,Oh ye leaves, to seek my chamber,

모모Up the trellis'd vine on high!May ye swell, twin-berries tender,Juicier far,--and with more splendour

모모Ripen, and more speedily!O'er ye broods the sun at evenAs he sinks to rest, and heaven

모모Softly breathes into your earAll its fertilising fullness,While the moon's refreshing coolness,

모모Magic-laden, hovers near;And, alas! ye're watered ever

모모By a stream of tears that rillFrom mine eyes--tears ceasing never,

모모Tears of love that nought can still!

모모1775.*-----RESTLESS LOVE.

모모THROUGH rain, through snow,Through tempest go!'Mongst streaming caves,O'er misty waves,On, on! still on!Peace, rest have flown!

모모Sooner through sadness

모모I'd wish to be slain,Than all the gladness

모모Of life to sustainAll the fond yearning

모모That heart feels for heart,Only seems burning

모모To make them both smart.

모모How shall I fly?Forestwards hie?Vain were all strife!Bright crown of life.Turbulent bliss,--Love, thou art this!

모모1789.-----THE SHEPHERD'S LAMENT.

모모ON yonder lofty mountain

모모A thousand times I stand,And on my staff reclining,

모모Look down on the smiling land.

모모My grazing flocks then I follow,

모모My dog protecting them well;I find myself in the valley,

모모But how, I scarcely can tell.

모모The whole of the meadow is cover'd

모모With flowers of beauty rare;I pluck them, but pluck them unknowing

모모To whom the offering to bear.

모모In rain and storm and tempest,

모모I tarry beneath the tree,But closed remaineth yon portal;

모모'Tis all but a vision to me.

모모High over yonder dwelling,

모모There rises a rainbow gay;But she from home hath departed

모모And wander'd far, far away.

모모Yes, far away bath she wander'd,

모모Perchance e'en over the sea;Move onward, ye sheep, then, move onward!

모모Full sad the shepherd must be.

모모1803.*-----COMFORT IN TEARS.

모모How happens it that thou art sad,

모모While happy all appear?Thine eye proclaims too well that thou

모모Hast wept full many a tear.

모모"If I have wept in solitude,

모모None other shares my grief,And tears to me sweet balsam are,

모모And give my heart relief."

모모Thy happy friends invite thee now,--

모모Oh come, then, to our breast!And let the loss thou hast sustain'd

모모Be there to us confess'd!

모모"Ye shout, torment me, knowing not

모모What 'tis afflicteth me;Ah no! I have sustained no loss,

모모Whate'er may wanting be."

모모If so it is, arise in haste!

모모Thou'rt young and full of life.At years like thine, man's blest with strength.

모모And courage for the strife.

모모"Ah no! in vain 'twould be to strive,

모모The thing I seek is far;It dwells as high, it gleams as fair

모모As yonder glitt'ring star."

모모The stars we never long to clasp,

모모We revel in their light,And with enchantment upward gaze,

모모Each clear and radiant night.

모모"And I with rapture upward gaze,

모모On many a blissful day;Then let me pass the night in tears,

모모Till tears are wip'd away!

모모1803.*-----NIGHT SONG,

모모WHEN on thy pillow lying,

모모Half listen, I implore,And at my lute's soft sighing,

모모Sleep on! what wouldst thou more?

모모For at my lute's soft sighing

모모The stars their blessings pourOn feelings never-dying;

모모Sleep on! what wouldst thou more?

모모Those feelings never-dying

모모My spirit aid to soarFrom earthly conflicts trying;

모모Sleep on! what wouldst thou more?

모모From earthly conflicts trying

모모Thou driv'st me to this shore;Through thee I'm thither flying,--

모모Sleep on! what wouldst thou more?

모모Through thee I'm hither flying,

모모Thou wilt not list beforeIn slumbers thou art lying:

모모Sleep on! what wouldst thou more?

모모1803.*-----LONGING.

모모WHAT pulls at my heart so?

모모What tells me to roam?What drags me and lures me

모모From chamber and home?How round the cliffs gather

모모The clouds high in air!I fain would go thither,

모모I fain would be there!

모모The sociable flight

모모Of the ravens comes back;I mingle amongst them,

모모And follow their track.Round wall and round mountain

모모Together we fly;She tarries below there,

모모I after her spy.

모모Then onward she wanders,

모모My flight I wing soonTo the wood fill'd with bushes,

모모A bird of sweet tune.She tarries and hearkens,

모모And smiling, thinks she:"How sweetly he's singing!

모모He's singing to me!"

모모The heights are illum'd

모모By the fast setting sun;The pensive fair maiden

모모Looks thoughtfully on;She roams by the streamlet,

모모O'er meadows she goes,And darker and darker

모모The pathway fast grows.

모모I rise on a sudden,

모모A glimmering star;"What glitters above me,

모모So near and so far?"

모모And when thou with wonder

모모Hast gazed on the light,I fall down before thee,

모모Entranced by thy sight!

모모1803.-----TO MIGNON.

모모OVER vale and torrent farRolls along the sun's bright car.Ah! he wakens in his course

모모Mine, as thy deep-seated smart

모모In the heart.Ev'ry morning with new force.

모모Scarce avails night aught to me;E'en the visions that I seeCome but in a mournful guise;

모모And I feel this silent smart

모모In my heartWith creative pow'r arise.

모모During many a beauteous yearI have seen ships 'neath me steer,As they seek the shelt'ring bay;

모모But, alas, each lasting smart

모모In my heartFloats not with the stream away.

모모I must wear a gala dress,Long stored up within my press,For to-day to feasts is given;

모모None know with what bitter smart

모모Is my heartFearfully and madly riven.

모모Secretly I weep each tear,Yet can cheerful e'en appear,With a face of healthy red;

모모For if deadly were this silent smart

모모In my heart,Ah, I then had long been dead!-----THE MOUNTAIN CASTLE.

모모THERE stands on yonder high mountain

모모A castle built of yore,Where once lurked horse and horseman

모모In rear of gate and of door.

모모Now door and gate are in ashes,

모모And all around is so still;And over the fallen ruins

모모I clamber just as I will.

모모Below once lay a cellar,

모모With costly wines well stor'd;No more the glad maid with her pitcher

모모Descends there to draw from the hoard.

모모No longer the goblet she places

모모Before the guests at the feast;The flask at the meal so hallow'd

모모No longer she fills for the priest.

모모No more for the eager squire

모모The draught in the passage is pour'd;No more for the flying present

모모Receives she the flying reward.

모모For all the roof and the rafters,

모모They all long since have been burn'd,And stairs and passage and chapel

모모To rubbish and ruins are turn'd.

모모Yet when with lute and with flagon,

모모When day was smiling and bright,I've watch'd my mistress climbing

모모To gain this perilous height,

모모Then rapture joyous and radiant

모모The silence so desolate brake,And all, as in days long vanish'd,

모모Once more to enjoyment awoke;

모모As if for guests of high station

모모The largest rooms were prepared;As if from those times so precious

모모A couple thither had fared;

모모As if there stood in his chapel

모모The priest in his sacred dress,And ask'd: "Would ye twain be united?"

모모And we, with a smile, answer'd, "Yes!"

모모And songs that breath'd a deep feeling,

모모That touched the heart's innermost chord,The music-fraught mouth of sweet echo,

모모Instead of the many, outpour'd.

모모And when at eve all was hidden

모모In silence unbroken and deep,The glowing sun then look'd upwards,

모모And gazed on the summit so steep.

모모And squire and maiden then glitter'd

모모As bright and gay as a lord,She seized the time for her present,

모모And he to give her reward.

모모1803.*-----THE SPIRIT'S SALUTE.

모모THE hero's noble shade stands high

모모On yonder turret grey;And as the ship is sailing by,

모모He speeds it on his way.

모모"See with what strength these sinews thrill'd!

모모This heart, how firm and wild!These bones, what knightly marrow fill'd!

모모This cup, how bright it smil'd!

모모"Half of my life I strove and fought,

모모And half I calmly pass'd;And thou, oh ship with beings fraught,

모모Sail safely to the last!"

모모1774.-----TO A GOLDEN HEART THAT HE WORE ROUND HIS NECK.

모모[Addressed, during the Swiss tour already mentioned, to a presentLily had given him, during the time of their happy connection,which was then about to be terminated for ever.]

모모OH thou token loved of joys now perish'd

모모That I still wear from my neck suspended,Art thou stronger than our spirit-bond so cherish'd?

모모Or canst thou prolong love's days untimely ended?

모모Lily, I fly from thee! I still am doom'd to rangeThro' countries strange,

모모Thro' distant vales and woods, link'd on to thee!Ah, Lily's heart could surely never fall

모모So soon away from me!

모모As when a bird bath broken from his thrall,

모모And seeks the forest green,Proof of imprisonment he bears behind him,A morsel of the thread once used to bind him;

모모The free-born bird of old no more is seen,

모모For he another's prey bath been.

모모1775.-----THE BLISS OF SORROW.

모모NEVER dry, never dry,

모모Tears that eternal love sheddeth!How dreary, how dead doth the world still appear,When only half-dried on the eye is the tear!

모모Never dry, never dry,

모모Tears that unhappy love sheddeth!

모모1789.*-----THE WANDERER'S NIGHT-SONG.

모모THOU who comest from on high,

모모Who all woes and sorrows stillest,Who, for twofold misery,

모모Hearts with twofold balsam fillest,Would this constant strife would cease!

모모What are pain and rapture now?Blissful Peace,

모모To my bosom hasten thou!

모모1789.*-----THE SAME.

모모[Written at night on the Kickelhahn, a hill in the forest ofIlmenau, on the walls of a little hermitage where Goethe composedthe last act of his Iphigenia.]

모모HUSH'D on the hill

모모Is the breeze;

모모Scarce by the zephyr

모모The trees

모모Softly are press'd;The woodbird's asleep on the bough.Wait, then, and thou

모모Soon wilt find rest.

모모1783.-----THE HUNTER'S EVEN-SONG.

모모THE plain with still and wand'ring feet,

모모And gun full-charged, I tread,And hov'ring see thine image sweet,

모모Thine image dear, o'er head.

모모In gentle silence thou dost fare

모모Through field and valley dear;But doth my fleeting image ne'er

모모To thy mind's eye appear?

모모His image, who, by grief oppress'd,

모모Roams through the world forlorn,And wanders on from east to west,

모모Because from thee he's torn?

모모When I would think of none but thee,

모모Mine eyes the moon survey;A calm repose then steals o'er me,

모모But how, 'twere hard to say.

모모1776,*-----TO THE MOON.

모모BUSH and vale thou fill'st again

모모With thy misty ray,And my spirit's heavy chain

모모Castest far away.

모모Thou dost o'er my fields extend

모모Thy sweet soothing eye,Watching like a gentle friend,

모모O'er my destiny.

모모Vanish'd days of bliss and woe

모모Haunt me with their tone,Joy and grief in turns I know,

모모As I stray alone.

모모Stream beloved, flow on! flow on!

모모Ne'er can I be gay!Thus have sport and kisses gone,

모모Truth thus pass'd away.

모모Once I seem'd the lord to be

모모Of that prize so fair!Now, to our deep sorrow, we

모모Can forget it ne'er.

모모Murmur, stream, the vale along,

모모Never cease thy sighs;Murmur, whisper to my song

모모Answering melodies!

모모When thou in the winter's night

모모Overflow'st in wrath,Or in spring-time sparklest bright,

모모As the buds shoot forth.

모모He who from the world retires,

모모Void of hate, is blest;Who a friend's true love inspires,

모모Leaning on his breast!

모모That which heedless man ne'er knew,

모모Or ne'er thought aright,Roams the bosom's labyrinth through,

모모Boldly into night.

모모1789.*-----TO LINA.

모모SHOULD these songs, love, as they fleet,

모모Chance again to reach thy hand,At the piano take thy seat,

모모Where thy friend was wont to stand!

모모Sweep with finger bold the string,

모모Then the book one moment see:But read not! do nought but sing!

모모And each page thine own will be!

모모Ah, what grief the song imparts

모모With its letters, black on white,That, when breath'd by thee, our hearts

모모Now can break and now delight!

모모1800.*-----EVER AND EVERYWHERE.

모모FAR explore the mountain hollow,High in air the clouds then follow!

모모To each brook and vale the Muse

모모Thousand times her call renews.

모모Soon as a flow'ret blooms in spring,It wakens many a strain;

모모And when Time spreads his fleeting wing,

모모The seasons come again.

모모1820.*-----PETITION.

모모OH thou sweet maiden fair,Thou with the raven hair,

모모Why to the window go?

모모While gazing down below,Art standing vainly there?

모모Oh, if thou stood'st for me,And lett'st the latch but fly,

모모How happy should I be!How soon would I leap high!

모모1789.*-----TO HIS COY ONE.

모모SEEST thou yon smiling Orange?Upon the tree still hangs it;Already March bath vanish'd,And new-born flow'rs are shooting.I draw nigh to the tree then,And there I say: Oh Orange,Thou ripe and juicy Orange,Thou sweet and luscious Orange,I shake the tree, I shake it,Oh fall into my lap!

모모1789.*-----NIGHT THOUGHTS.

모모OH, unhappy stars! your fate I mourn,

모모Ye by whom the sea-toss'd sailor's lighted,Who with radiant beams the heav'ns adorn,

모모But by gods and men are unrequited:For ye love not,--ne'er have learnt to love!Ceaselessly in endless dance ye move,In the spacious sky your charms displaying,

모모What far travels ye have hasten'd through,Since, within my loved one's arms delaying,

모모I've forgotten you and midnight too!

모모1789.*-----TO LIDA.

모모THE only one whom, Lida, thou canst love,

모모Thou claim'st, and rightly claim'st, for only thee;He too is wholly thine; since doomed to rove

모모Far from thee, in life's turmoils nought I seeSave a thin veil, through which thy form I view,As though in clouds; with kindly smile and true,

모모It cheers me, like the stars eterne that gleamAcross the northern-lights' far-flick'ring beam.

모모1789.*-----PROXIMITY.

모모I KNOW not, wherefore, dearest love,

모모Thou often art so strange and coyWhen 'mongst man's busy haunts we move,

모모Thy coldness puts to flight my joy.But soon as night and silence round us reign,I know thee by thy kisses sweet again!

모모1789.*-----RECIPROCAL.

모모MY mistress, where sits she?

모모What is it that charms?The absent she's rocking,

모모Held fast in her arms.

모모In pretty cage prison'd

모모She holds a bird still;Yet lets him fly from her,

모모Whenever he will.

모모He pecks at her finger,

모모And pecks at her lips,And hovers and flutters,

모모And round her he skips.

모모Then hasten thou homeward,

모모In fashion to be;If thou hast the maiden,

모모She also hath thee.

모모1816.-----ROLLICKING HANS.

모모HALLO there! A glass!

모모Ha! the draught's truly sweet!If for drink go my shoes,

모모I shall still have my feet.

모모A maiden and wine,

모모With sweet music and song,--I would they were mine,

모모All life's journey along!

모모If I depart from this sad sphere,And leave a will behind me here,A suit at law will be preferr'd,But as for thanks,--the deuce a word!So ere I die, I squander all,And that a proper will I call.

모모HIS COMRADE.

모모Hallo there! A glass!

모모Ha! the draught's truly sweetIf thou keepest thy shoes,

모모Thou wilt then spare thy feet.

모모A maiden and wine,

모모With sweet music and song,On pavement, are thine,

모모All life's journey along!-----THE FREEBOOTER,

모모No door has my house,

모모No house has my door;And in and out ever

모모I carry my store.

모모No grate has my kitchen,

모모No kitchen my grate;Yet roasts it and boils it

모모Both early and late.

모모My bed has no trestles,

모모My trestles no bed;Yet merrier moments

모모No mortal e'er led.

모모My cellar is lofty,

모모My barn is full deep,From top to the bottom,--

모모There lie I and sleep.

모모And soon as I waken,

모모All moves on its race;My place has no fixture,

모모My fixture no place.

모모1827.*-----JOY AND SORROW.

모모As a fisher-boy I fared

모모To the black rock in the sea,And, while false gifts I prepared.

모모Listen'd and sang merrily,Down descended the decoy,

모모Soon a fish attack'd the bait;One exultant shout of joy,--

모모And the fish was captured straight.

모모Ah! on shore, and to the wood

모모Past the cliffs, o'er stock and stone,One foot's traces I pursued,

모모And the maiden was alone.Lips were silent, eyes downcast

모모As a clasp-knife snaps the bait,With her snare she seized me fast,

모모And the boy was captured straight.

모모Heav'n knows who's the happy swain

모모That she rambles with anew!I must dare the sea again,

모모Spite of wind and weather too.When the great and little fish

모모Wail and flounder in my net,Straight returns my eager wish

모모In her arms to revel yet!

모모1815.-----MARCH.

모모THE snow-flakes fall in showers,

모모The time is absent still,When all Spring's beauteous flowers,When all Spring's beauteous flowers

모모Our hearts with joy shall fill.

모모With lustre false and fleeting

모모The sun's bright rays are thrown;The swallow's self is cheating:The swallow's self is cheating,

모모And why? He comes alone!

모모Can I e'er feel delighted

모모Alone, though Spring is near?Yet when we are united,Yet when we are united,

모모The Summer will be here.

모모1817.-----APRIL.

모모TELL me, eyes, what 'tis ye're seeking;

모모For ye're saying something sweet,

모모Fit the ravish'd ear to greet,Eloquently, softly speaking.

모모Yet I see now why ye're roving;

모모For behind those eyes so bright,

모모To itself abandon'd quite,Lies a bosom, truthful, loving,--

모모One that it must fill with pleasure

모모'Mongst so many, dull and blind,

모모One true look at length to find,That its worth can rightly treasure.

모모Whilst I'm lost in studying ever

모모To explain these cyphers duly,--

모모To unravel my looks trulyIn return be your endeavour!

모모1820.-----MAY.

모모LIGHT and silv'ry cloudlets hover

모모In the air, as yet scarce warm;Mild, with glimmer soft tinged over,

모모Peeps the sun through fragrant balm.Gently rolls and heaves the ocean

모모As its waves the bank o'erflow.And with ever restless motion

모모Moves the verdure to and fro,

모모Mirror'd brightly far below.

모모What is now the foliage moving?

모모Air is still, and hush'd the breeze,Sultriness, this fullness loving,

모모Through the thicket, from the trees.Now the eye at once gleams brightly,

모모See! the infant band with mirthMoves and dances nimbly, lightly,

모모As the morning gave it birth,

모모Flutt'ring two and two o'er earth.

모모*      *      *      *

모모1816.-----JUNE.

모모SHE behind yon mountain lives,Who my love's sweet guerdon gives.Tell me, mount, how this can be!Very glass thou seem'st to me,And I seem to be close by,For I see her drawing nigh;Now, because I'm absent, sad,Now, because she sees me, glad!

모모Soon between us rise to sightValleys cool, with bushes light,Streams and meadows; next appear

모모Mills and wheels, the surest tokenThat a level spot is near,

모모Plains far-stretching and unbroken.And so onwards, onwards roam,To my garden and my home!

모모But how comes it then to pass?All this gives no joy, alas!--I was ravish'd by her sight,By her eyes so fair and bright,By her footstep soft and light.How her peerless charms I praised,When from head to foot I gazed!I am here, she's far away,--I am gone, with her to stay.

모모If on rugged hills she wander,

모모If she haste the vale along,Pinions seem to flutter yonder,

모모And the air is fill'd with song;With the glow of youth still playing,

모모Joyous vigour in each limb,One in silence is delaying,

모모She alone 'tis blesses him.

모모Love, thou art too fair, I ween!Fairer I have never seen!From the heart full easilyBlooming flowers are cull'd by thee.If I think: "Oh, were it so,"Bone and marrow seen to glow!If rewarded by her love,Can I greater rapture prove?

모모And still fairer is the bride,When in me she will confide,When she speaks and lets me knowAll her tale of joy and woe.All her lifetime's historyNow is fully known to me.Who in child or woman e'erSoul and body found so fair?

모모1815.-----NEXT YEAR'S SPRING.

모모THE bed of flowers

모모Loosens amain,The beauteous snowdrops

모모Droop o'er the plain.The crocus opens

모모Its glowing bud,Like emeralds others,

모모Others, like blood.With saucy gesture

모모Primroses flare,And roguish violets,

모모Hidden with care;And whatsoever

모모There stirs and strives,The Spring's contented,

모모If works and thrives.

모모'Mongst all the blossoms

모모That fairest are,My sweetheart's sweetness

모모Is sweetest far;Upon me ever

모모Her glances light,My song they waken,

모모My words make bright,An ever open

모모And blooming mind,In sport, unsullied,

모모In earnest, kind.Though roses and lilies

모모By Summer are brought,Against my sweetheart

모모Prevails he nought.

모모1816.-----AT MIDNIGHT HOUR.

모모[Goethe relates that a remarkable situation he was in one brightmoonlight night led to the composition of this sweet song, whichwas "the dearer to him because he could not say whence it cameand whither it would."]

모모AT midnight hour I went, not willingly,

모모A little, little boy, yon churchyard past,To Father Vicar's house; the stars on high

모모On all around their beauteous radiance cast,

모모At midnight hour.

모모And when, in journeying o'er the path of life,

모모My love I follow'd, as she onward moved,With stars and northern lights o'er head in strife,

모모Going and coming, perfect bliss I proved

모모At midnight hour.

모모Until at length the full moon, lustre-fraught,

모모Burst thro' the gloom wherein she was enshrined;And then the willing, active, rapid thought

모모Around the past, as round the future twined,

모모At midnight hour.

모모1818.-----TO THE RISING FULL MOON.

모모Dornburg, 25th August, 1828.

모모WILT thou suddenly enshroud thee,

모모Who this moment wert so nigh?Heavy rising masses cloud thee,

모모Thou art hidden from mine eye.

모모Yet my sadness thou well knowest,

모모Gleaming sweetly as a star!That I'm loved, 'tis thou that showest,

모모Though my loved one may be far.

모모Upward mount then! clearer, milder,

모모Robed in splendour far more bright!Though my heart with grief throbs wilder,

모모Fraught with rapture is the night!

모모1828.-----THE BRIDEGROOM.*

모모(Not in the English sense of the word, but the German, where ithas the meaning of betrothed.)

모모I SLEPT,--'twas midnight,--in my bosom woke,

모모As though 'twere day, my love-o'erflowing heart;To me it seemed like night, when day first broke;

모모What is't to me, whate'er it may impart?

모모She was away; the world's unceasing strife

모모For her alone I suffer'd through the heatOf sultry day; oh, what refreshing life

모모At cooling eve!--my guerdon was complete.

모모The sun now set, and wand'ring hand in hand,

모모His last and blissful look we greeted then;While spake our eyes, as they each other scann'd:

모모"From the far east, let's trust, he'll come again!"

모모At midnight!--the bright stars, in vision blest,

모모Guide to the threshold where she slumbers calm:Oh be it mine, there too at length to rest,--

모모Yet howsoe'er this prove, life's full of charm!

모모1828.-----SUCH, SUCH IS HE WHO PLEASETH ME.

모모FLY, dearest, fly! He is not nigh!

모모He who found thee one fair morn in Spring

모모In the wood where thou thy flight didst wing.Fly, dearest, fly! He is not nigh!Never rests the foot of evil spy.

모모Hark! flutes' sweet strains and love's refrains

모모Reach the loved one, borne there by the wind,

모모In the soft heart open doors they find.Hark! flutes' sweet strains and love's refrains,Hark!--yet blissful love their echo pains.

모모Erect his head, and firm his tread,

모모Raven hair around his smooth brow strays,

모모On his cheeks a Spring eternal plays.Erect his head, and firm his tread,And by grace his ev'ry step is led.

모모Happy his breast, with pureness bless'd,

모모And the dark eyes 'neath his eyebrows placed,
