모모With full many a beauteous line are graced.Happy his breast, with pureness bless'd,Soon as seen, thy love must be confess'd.

모모His mouth is red--its power I dread,

모모On his lips morn's fragrant incense lies,

모모Round his lips the cooling Zephyr sighs.His mouth is red--its power I dread,With one glance from him, all sorrow's fled.

모모His blood is true, his heart bold too,

모모In his soft arms, strength, protection, dwells

모모And his face with noble pity swells.His blood is true, his heart bold too,Blest the one whom those dear arms may woo!

모모1816..-----SICILIAN SONG.

모모YE black and roguish eyes,

모모If ye command.Each house in ruins lies,

모모No town can stand.And shall my bosom's chain,--

모모This plaster wall,?To think one moment, deign,--

모모Shall ii not fall?

모모1811.-----SWISS SONG,

모모Up in th' mountainI was a-sitting,With the bird thereAs my guest,Blithely singing,Blithely springing,And buildingHis nest.

모모In the gardenI was a-standing,And the bee thereSaw as well,Buzzing, humming,Going, coming,And buildingHis cell.

모모O'er the meadowI was a-going,And there saw theButterflies,Sipping, dancing,Flying, glancing,And charmingThe eyes.

모모And then came myDear Hansel,And I show'd themWith glee,Sipping, quaffing,And he, laughing,Sweet kissesGave me.

모모1811.-----FINNISH SONG.

모모IF the loved one, the well-known one,Should return as he departed,On his lips would ring my kisses,Though the wolf's blood might have dyed them;And a hearty grasp I'd give him,Though his finger-ends were serpents.

모모Wind! Oh, if thou hadst but reason,Word for word in turns thou'dst carry,E'en though some perchance might perish'Tween two lovers so far distant.

모모All choice morsels I'd dispense with,Table-flesh of priests neglect too,Sooner than renounce my lover,Whom, in Summer having vanquish'd,I in Winter tamed still longer.

모모1810.-----GIPSY SONG.

모모IN the drizzling mist, with the snow high-pil'd,In the Winter night, in the forest wild,I heard the wolves with their ravenous howl,I heard the screaming note of the owl:

모모Wille wau wau wau!

모모Wille wo wo wo!

모모Wito hu!

모모I shot, one day, a cat in a ditch--The dear black cat of Anna the witch;Upon me, at night, seven were-wolves came down,Seven women they were, from out of the town.

모모Wille wau wau wau!

모모Wille wo wo wo!

모모Wito hu!

모모I knew them all; ay, I knew them straight;First, Anna, then Ursula, Eve, and Kate,And Barbara, Lizzy, and Bet as well;And forming a ring, they began to yell:

모모Wille wau wau wau!

모모Wille wo wo wo!

모모Wito hu!

모모Then call'd I their names with angry threat:"What wouldst thou, Anna? What wouldst thou, Bet?"At hearing my voice, themselves they shook,And howling and yelling, to flight they took.

모모Wille wau wau wau!

모모Wille wo wo wo!

모모Wito hu!

모모1772.-----THE DESTRUCTION OF MAGDEBURG.

모모[For a fine account of the fearful sack of Magdeburg, by Tilly,in the year 1613, see SCHILLER's History of the Thirty Years'War.]

모모OH, Magdeberg the town!Fair maids thy beauty crown,Thy charms fair maids and matrons crown;Oh, Magdeburg the town!

모모Where all so blooming stands,Advance fierce Tilly's bands;O'er gardens and o'er well--till'd landsAdvance fierce Tilly's bands.

모모Now Tilly's at the gate.Our homes who'll liberate?Go, loved one, hasten to the gate,And dare the combat straight!

모모There is no need as yet,However fierce his threat;Thy rosy cheeks I'll kiss, sweet pet!There is no need as yet.

모모My longing makes me pale.Oh, what can wealth avail?E'en now thy father may be pale.Thou mak'st my courage fail.

모모Oh, mother, give me bread!Is then my father dead?Oh, mother, one small crust of bread!Oh, what misfortune dread!

모모Thy father, dead lies he,The trembling townsmen flee,Adown the street the blood runs free;Oh, whither shall we flee?

모모The churches ruined lie,The houses burn on high,The roofs they smoke, the flames out fly,Into the street then hie!

모모No safety there they meet!The soldiers fill the Street,With fire and sword the wreck complete:No safety there they meet!

모모Down falls the houses' line,Where now is thine or mine?That bundle yonder is not thine,Thou flying maiden mine!

모모The women sorrow sore.The maidens far, far more.The living are no virgins more;Thus Tilly's troops make war!-----

모모FAMILIAR SONGS.

모모------What we sing in companySoon from heart to heart will fly.-----

모모THE Gesellige Lieder, which I have angicisled as above, asseveral of them cannot be called convivial songs, are separatedby Goethe from his other songs, and I have adhered to the samearrangement. The Ergo bibamus is a well-known drinking song inGermany, where it enjoys vast popularity.

모모ON THE NEW YEAR.

모모[Composed for a merry party that used to meet, in 1802, atGoethe's house.]

모모FATE now allows us,

모모'Twixt the departing

모모And the upstarting,Happy to be;And at the call of

모모Memory cherish'd,

모모Future and perish'dMoments we see.

모모Seasons of anguish,--

모모Ah, they must ever

모모Truth from woe sever,Love and joy part;Days still more worthy

모모Soon will unite us,

모모Fairer songs light us,Strength'ning the heart.

모모We, thus united,

모모Think of, with gladness,

모모Rapture and sadness,Sorrow now flies.Oh, how mysterious

모모Fortune's direction!

모모Old the connection,

모모New-born the prize!

모모Thank, for this, Fortune,

모모Wavering blindly!

모모Thank all that kindlyFate may bestow!Revel in change's

모모Impulses clearer,

모모Love far sincerer,More heartfelt glow!

모모Over the old one,

모모Wrinkles collected,

모모Sad and dejected,Others may view;But, on us gently

모모Shineth a true one,

모모And to the new oneWe, too, are new.

모모As a fond couple

모모'Midst the dance veering,

모모First disappearing,Then reappear,So let affection

모모Guide thro' life's mazy

모모Pathways so hazyInto the year!

모모1802.-----ANNIVERSARY SONG.

모모[This little song describes the different members of the partyjust spoken of.]

모모WHY pacest thou, my neighbour fair,

모모The garden all alone?If house and land thou seek'st to guard,

모모I'd thee as mistress own.

모모My brother sought the cellar-maid,

모모And suffered her no rest;She gave him a refreshing draught,

모모A kiss, too, she impress'd.

모모My cousin is a prudent wight,

모모The cook's by him ador'd;He turns the spit round ceaselessly,

모모To gain love's sweet reward.

모모We six together then began

모모A banquet to consume,When lo! a fourth pair singing came,

모모And danced into the room.

모모Welcome were they,--and welcome too

모모Was a fifth jovial pair.Brimful of news, and stored with tales

모모And jests both new and rare.

모모For riddles, spirit, raillery,

모모And wit, a place remain'd;A sixth pair then our circle join'd,

모모And so that prize was gain'd.

모모And yet to make us truly blest,

모모One miss'd we, and full sore;A true and tender couple came,--

모모We needed them no more.

모모The social banquet now goes on,

모모Unchequer'd by alloy;The sacred double-numbers then

모모Let us at once enjoy!

모모1802.-----THE SPRING ORACLE.

모모OH prophetic bird so bright,Blossom-songster, cuckoo bight!In the fairest time of year,Dearest bird, oh! deign to hearWhat a youthful pair would pray,Do thou call, if hope they may:Thy cuck-oo, thy cuck-oo.Ever more cuck-oo, cuck-oo!

모모Hearest thou? A loving pairFain would to the altar fare;Yes! a pair in happy youth,Full of virtue, full of truth.Is the hour not fix'd by fate?Say, how long must they still wait?Hark! cuck-oo! hark! cuck-oo!Silent yet! for shame, cuck-oo!

모모'Tis not our fault, certainly!Only two years patient be!But if we ourselves please here,Will pa-pa-papas appear?Know that thou'lt more kindness do us,More thou'lt prophesy unto us.One! cuck-oo! Two! cuck-oo!Ever, ever, cuck-oo, cuck-oo, coo!

모모If we've calculated clearly,We have half a dozen nearly.If good promises we'll give,Wilt thou say how long we'II live?Truly, we'll confess to thee,We'd prolong it willingly.Coo cuck-oo, coo cuck-oo,Coo, coo, coo, coo, coo, coo, coo, coo, coo!

모모Life is one continued feast--(If we keep no score, at least).If now we together dwell,Will true love remain as well?For if that should e'er decay,Happiness would pass away.Coo cuck-oo, coo cuck-oo,Coo, coo, coo, coo, coo, coo, coo, coo, coo!

모모1803.*(Gracefully in infinitum.)-----THE HAPPY COUPLE.

모모AFTER these vernal rains

모모That we so warmly sought,Dear wife, see how our plains

모모With blessings sweet are fraught!We cast our distant gaze

모모Far in the misty blue;Here gentle love still strays,

모모Here dwells still rapture true.

모모Thou seest whither go

모모Yon pair of pigeons white,Where swelling violets blow

모모Round sunny foliage bright.'Twas there we gather'd first

모모A nosegay as we roved;There into flame first burst

모모The passion that we proved.

모모Yet when, with plighted troth,

모모The priest beheld us fareHome from the altar both,

모모With many a youthful pair,--Then other moons had birth,

모모And many a beauteous sun,Then we had gain'd the earth

모모Whereon life's race to run.

모모A hundred thousand fold

모모The mighty bond was seal'd;In woods, on mountains cold,

모모In bushes, in the field,Within the wall, in caves,

모모And on the craggy height,And love, e'en o'er the waves,

모모Bore in his tube the light.

모모Contented we remain'd,

모모We deem'd ourselves a pair;'Twas otherwise ordain'd,

모모For, lo! a third was there;A fourth, fifth, sixth appear'd,

모모And sat around our board;And now the plants we've rear'd

모모High o'er our heads have soar'd!

모모How fair and pleasant looks,

모모On yonder beauteous spot,Embraced by poplar-brooks,

모모The newly-finish'd cot!Who is it there that sits

모모In that glad home above?Is't not our darling Fritz

모모With his own darling love?

모모Beside yon precipice,

모모Whence pent-up waters steal,And leaving the abyss,

모모Fall foaming through the wheel,Though people often tell

모모Of millers' wives so fair,Yet none can e'er excel

모모Our dearest daughter there!

모모Yet where the thick-set green

모모Stands round yon church and sad,Where the old fir-tree's seen

모모Alone tow'rd heaven to nod,--'Tis there the ashes lie

모모Of our untimely dead;From earth our gaze on high

모모By their blest memory's led.

모모See how yon hill is bright

모모With billowy-waving arms!The force returns, whose might

모모Has vanquished war's alarms.Who proudly hastens here

모모With wreath-encircled brow?'Tis like our child so dear

모모Thus Charles comes homeward now.

모모That dearest honour'd guest

모모Is welcom'd by the bride;She makes the true one blest,

모모At the glad festal tide.And ev'ry one makes haste

모모To join the dance with glee;While thou with wreaths hast graced

모모The youngest children three.

모모To sound of flute and horn

모모The time appears renew'd,When we, in love's young morn,

모모In the glad dance upstood;And perfect bliss I know

모모Ere the year's course is run,For to the font we go

모모With grandson and with son!

모모1803.*-----SONG OF FELLOWSHIP.

모모[Written and sung in honour of the birthday of the Pastor Ewaldat the time of Goethe's happy connection with Lily.]

모모IN ev'ry hour of joy

모모That love and wine prolong,The moments we'll employ

모모To carol forth this song!We're gathered in His name,

모모Whose power hath brought us here;He kindled first our flame,

모모He bids it burn more clear.

모모Then gladly glow to-night,

모모And let our hearts combine!Up! quaff with fresh delight

모모This glass of sparkling wine!Up! hail the joyous hour,

모모And let your kiss be true;With each new bond of power

모모The old becomes the new!

모모Who in our circle lives,

모모And is not happy there?True liberty it gives,

모모And brother's love so fair.Thus heart and heart through life

모모With mutual love are fill'd;And by no causeless strife

모모Our union e'er is chill'd.

모모Our hopes a God has crown'd

모모With life-discernment free,And all we view around,

모모Renews our ecstasy.Ne'er by caprice oppress'd,

모모Our bliss is ne'er destroy'd;More freely throbs our breast,

모모By fancies ne'er alloy'd.

모모Where'er our foot we set,

모모The more life's path extends,And brighter, brighter yet

모모Our gaze on high ascends.We know no grief or pain,

모모Though all things fall and rise;Long may we thus remain!

모모Eternal be our ties!

모모1775.-----CONSTANCY IN CHANGE.

모모COULD this early bliss but rest

모모Constant for one single hour!But e'en now the humid West

모모Scatters many a vernal shower.Should the verdure give me joy?

모모'Tis to it I owe the shade;Soon will storms its bloom destroy,

모모Soon will Autumn bid it fade.

모모Eagerly thy portion seize,

모모If thou wouldst possess the fruit!Fast begin to ripen these,

모모And the rest already shoot.With each heavy storm of rain

모모Change comes o'er thy valley fair;Once, alas! but not again

모모Can the same stream hold thee e'er.

모모And thyself, what erst at least

모모Firm as rocks appear'd to rise,Walls and palaces thou seest

모모But with ever-changing eyes.Fled for ever now the lip

모모That with kisses used to glow,And the foot, that used to skip

모모O'er the mountain, like the roe.

모모And the hand, so true and warm,

모모Ever raised in charity,And the cunning-fashion'd form,--

모모All are now changed utterly.And what used to bear thy name,

모모When upon yon spot it stood,Like a rolling billow came,

모모Hast'ning on to join the flood.

모모Be then the beginning found

모모With the end in unison,Swifter than the forms around

모모Are themselves now fleeting on!Thank the merit in thy breast,

모모Thank the mould within thy heart,That the Muses' favour blestNe'er will perish, ne'er depart.

모모1803.*-----TABLE SONG.

모모[Composed for the merry party already mentioned, on the occasionof the departure for France of the hereditary prince, who was oneof the number, and who is especially alluded to in the 3rdverse.]

모모O'ER me--how I cannot say,--

모모Heav'nly rapture's growing.Will it help to guide my way

모모To yon stars all-glowing?Yet that here I'd sooner be,

모모To assert I'm able,Where, with wine and harmony,

모모I may thump the table.

모모Wonder not, my dearest friends,

모모What 'tis gives me pleasure;For of all that earth e'er lends,

모모'Tis the sweetest treasure.Therefore solemnly I swear,

모모With no reservation,That maliciously I'll ne'er

모모Leave my present station.

모모Now that here we're gather'd round,

모모Chasing cares and slumbers,Let, methought, the goblet sound

모모To the bard's glad numbers!Many a hundred mile away,

모모Go those we love dearly;Therefore let us here to-day

모모Make the glass ring clearly!

모모Here's His health, through Whom we live!

모모I that faith inherit.To our king the next toast give,

모모Honour is his merit,'Gainst each in-- and outward foe

모모He's our rock and tower.Of his maintenance thinks he though,

모모More that grows his power.

모모Next to her good health I drink,

모모Who has stirr'd my passion;Of his mistress let each think,

모모Think in knightly fashion.If the beauteous maid but see

모모Whom 'tis I now call so,Let her smiling nod to me:

모모"Here's my love's health also!"

모모To those friends,--the two or three,--

모모Be our next toast given,In whose presence revel we,

모모In the silent even,--Who the gloomy mist so cold

모모Scatter gently, lightly;To those friends, then, new or old,

모모Let the toast ring brightly.

모모Broader now the stream rolls on,

모모With its waves more swelling,While in higher, nobler tone,

모모Comrades, we are dwelling,--We who with collected might,

모모Bravely cling together,Both in fortune's sunshine bright,

모모And in stormy weather.

모모Just as we are gather'd thus,

모모Others are collected;On them, therefore, as on us,

모모Be Fate's smile directed!From the springhead to the sea,

모모Many a mill's revolving,And the world's prosperity

모모Is the task I'm solving.

모모1802.-----WONT AND DONE.

모모I HAVE loved; for the first time with passion I rave!I then was the servant, but now am the slave;

모모I then was the servant of all:By this creature so charming I now am fast bound,To love and love's guerdon she turns all around,

모모And her my sole mistress I call.

모모l've had faith; for the first time my faith is now strong!And though matters go strangely, though matters go wrong,

모모To the ranks of the faithful I'm true:Though ofttimes 'twas dark and though ofttimes 'twas drear,In the pressure of need, and when danger was near,

모모Yet the dawning of light I now view.

모모I have eaten; but ne'er have thus relish'd my food!For when glad are the senses, and joyous the blood,

모모At table all else is effacedAs for youth, it but swallows, then whistles an air;As for me, to a jovial resort I'd repair,

모모Where to eat, and enjoy what I taste.

모모I have drunk; but have never thus relish'd the bowl!For wine makes us lords, and enlivens the soul,

모모And loosens the trembling slave's tongue.Let's not seek to spare then the heart-stirring drink,For though in the barrel the old wine may sink,

모모In its place will fast mellow the young.

모모I have danced, and to dancing am pledged by a vow!Though no caper or waltz may be raved about now,

모모In a dance that's becoming, whirl round.And he who a nosegay of flowers has dress'd,And cares not for one any more than the rest,

모모With a garland of mirth is aye crown'd.

모모Then once more be merry, and banish all woes!For he who but gathers the blossoming rose.

모모By its thorns will be tickled alone.To-day still, as yesterday, glimmers the star;Take care from all heads that hang down to keep far,

모모And make but the future thine own.

모모1813.-----GENERAL CONFESSION.

모모In this noble ring to-day

모모Let my warning shame ye!Listen to my solemn voice,--

모모Seldom does it name ye.Many a thing have ye intended,

모모Many a thing have badly ended,And now I must blame ye.

모모At some moment in our lives

모모We must all repent us!So confess, with pious trust,

모모All your sins momentous!Error's crooked pathways shunning.

모모Let us, on the straight road running,Honestly content us!

모모Yes! we've oft, when waking, dream'd,

모모Let's confess it rightly;Left undrain'd the brimming cup,

모모When it sparkled brightly;Many a shepherd's-hour's soft blisses,

모모Many a dear mouth's flying kissesWe've neglected lightly.

모모Mute and silent have we sat,

모모Whilst the blockheads prated,And above e'en song divine

모모Have their babblings rated;To account we've even call'd us

모모For the moments that enthrall'd us,With enjoyment freighted.

모모If thou'lt absolution grant

모모To thy true ones ever,We, to execute thy will,

모모Ceaseless will endeavour,From half-measures strive to wean us,

모모Wholly, fairly, well demean us,Resting, flagging never.

모모At all blockheads we'll at once

모모Let our laugh ring clearly,And the pearly-foaming wine

모모Never sip at merely.Ne'er with eye alone give kisses,

모모But with boldness suck in blissesFrom those lips loved dearly.

모모1803.*-----COPTIC SONG.

모모LEAVE we the pedants to quarrel and strive,

모모Rigid and cautious the teachers to be!All of the wisest men e'er seen alive

모모Smile, nod, and join in the chorus with me:"Vain 'tis to wait till the dolt grows less silly!Play then the fool with the fool, willy-nilly,--

모모Children of wisdom,--remember the word!"

모모Merlin the old, from his glittering grave,When I, a stripling, once spoke to him,--gave

모모Just the same answer as that I've preferr'd;"Vain 'tis to wait till the dolt grows less silly!Play then the fool with the fool, willy-nilly,--

모모Children of wisdom,--remember the word!"

모모And on the Indian breeze as it booms,And in the depths of Egyptian tombs,

모모Only the same holy saying I've heard:"Vain 'tis to wait till the dolt grows less silly!Play then the fool with the fool, willy-nilly,--

모모Children of wisdom,--remember the word!"

모모1789.*-----ANOTHER.

모모Go! obedient to my call,

모모Turn to profit thy young days,

모모Wiser make betimes thy breast

모모In Fate's balance as it sways,

모모Seldom is the cock at rest;Thou must either mount, or fall,

모모Thou must either rule and win,

모모Or submissively give in,Triumph, or else yield to clamour:Be the anvil or the hammer.

모모1789.-----VANITAS! VANITATUM VANITAS!

모모MY trust in nothing now is placed,

모모Hurrah!So in the world true joy I taste,

모모Hurrah!Then he who would be a comrade of mineMust rattle his glass, and in chorus combine,Over these dregs of wine.

모모I placed my trust in gold and wealth,

모모Hurrah!But then I lost all joy and health,

모모Lack-a-day!Both here and there the money roll'd,And when I had it here, behold,From there had fled the gold!

모모I placed my trust in women next,

모모Hurrah!But there in truth was sorely vex'd,

모모Lack-a-day!The False another portion sought,The True with tediousness were fraught,The Best could not be bought.

모모My trust in travels then I placed,

모모Hurrah!And left my native land in haste.

모모Lack-a-day!But not a single thing seem'd good,The beds were bad, and strange the food,And I not understood.

모모I placed my trust in rank and fame,

모모Hurrah!Another put me straight to shame,

모모Lack-a-day!And as I had been prominent,All scowl'd upon me as I went,I found not one content.

모모I placed my trust in war and fight,

모모Hurrah!We gain'd full many a triumph bright,

모모Hurrah!Into the foeman's land we cross'd,We put our friends to equal cost,And there a leg I lost.

모모My trust is placed in nothing now,

모모Hurrah!At my command the world must bow,

모모Hurrah!And as we've ended feast and strain,The cup we'll to the bottom drain;No dregs must there remain!

모모1806.-----FORTUNE OF WAR.

모모NOUGHT more accursed in war I know

모모Than getting off scot-free;Inured to danger, on we go

모모In constant victory;We first unpack, then pack again,

모모With only this reward,That when we're marching, we complain,

모모And when in camp, are bor'd.

모모The time for billeting comes next,--

모모The peasant curses it;Each nobleman is sorely vex'd,

모모'Tis hated by the cit.Be civil, bad though be thy food,

모모The clowns politely treat;If to our hosts we're ever rude,

모모Jail-bread we're forced to eat.

모모And when the cannons growl around,

모모And small arms rattle clear,And trumpet, trot, and drum resound,

모모We merry all appear;And as it in the fight may chance,

모모We yield, then charge amain,And now retire, and now advance,

모모And yet a cross ne'er gain.

모모At length there comes a musket-ball,

모모And hits the leg, please Heaven;And then our troubles vanish all,

모모For to the town we're driven,(Well cover'd by the victor's force,)

모모Where we in wrath first came,--The women, frightened then, of course,

모모Are loving now and tame.

모모Cellar and heart are open'd wide,

모모The cook's allow'd no rest;While beds with softest down supplied

모모Are by our members press'd.The nimble lads upon us wait,

모모No sleep the hostess takesHer shift is torn in pieces straight,--

모모What wondrous lint it makes!

모모If one has tended carefully

모모The hero's wounded limb,Her neighbour cannot rest, for she

모모Has also tended him.A third arrives in equal haste,

모모At length they all are there,And in the middle he is placed

모모Of the whole band so fair!

모모On good authority the king

모모Hears how we love the fight,And bids them cross and ribbon bring,

모모Our coat and breast to dight.Say if a better fate can e'er

모모A son of Mars pursue!'Midst tears at length we go from there,

모모Beloved and honour'd too.

모모1814.-----OPEN TABLE.

모모MANY a guest I'd see to-day,

모모Met to taste my dishes!Food in plenty is prepar'd,

모모Birds, and game, and fishes.Invitations all have had,

모모All proposed attending.Johnny, go and look around!

모모Are they hither wending?

모모Pretty girls I hope to see,

모모Dear and guileless misses,Ignorant how sweet it is

모모Giving tender kisses.Invitations all have had,

모모All proposed attending.Johnny, go and look around!

모모Are they hither wending?

모모Women also I expect,

모모Loving tow'rd their spouses,Whose rude grumbling in their breasts

모모Greater love but rouses.Invitations they've had too,

모모All proposed attending!Johnny, go and look around!

모모Are they hither wending?

모모I've too ask'd young gentlemen,

모모Who are far from haughty,And whose purses are well-stock'd,

모모Well-behaved, not haughty.These especially I ask'd,

모모All proposed attending.Johnny, go and look around!

모모Are they hither wending?

모모Men I summon'd with respect,

모모Who their own wives treasure;Who in ogling other Fair

모모Never take a pleasure.To my greetings they replied,

모모All proposed attending.Johnny, go and look around!

모모Are they hither wending?

모모Then to make our joy complete,

모모Poets I invited,Who love other's songs far more

모모Than what they've indited.All acceded to my wish,

모모All proposed attending.Johnny, go and look around!

모모Are they hither wending?

모모Not a single one appears,

모모None seem this way posting.All the soup boils fast away,

모모Joints are over-roasting.Ah, I fear that we have been

모모Rather too unbending!Johnny, tell me what you think!

모모None are hither wending.

모모Johnny, run and quickly bring

모모Other guests to me now!Each arriving as he is--

모모That's the plan, I see now.In the town at once 'tis known,

모모Every one's commending.Johnny, open all the doors:

모모All are hither wending!

모모1815.*-----THE RECKONING.

모모LEADER.

모모LET no cares now hover o'er us

모모Let the wine unsparing run!Wilt thou swell our merry chorus?

모모Hast thou all thy duty done?

모모SOLO.

모모Two young folks--the thing is curious--

모모Loved each other; yesterdayBoth quite mild, to-day quite furious,

모모Next day, quite the deuce to pay!If her neck she there was stooping,

모모He must here needs pull his hair.I revived their spirits drooping,

모모And they're now a happy pair.

모모CHORUS.

모모Surely we for wine may languish!

모모Let the bumper then go round!For all sighs and groans of anguish

모모Thou to-day in joy hast drown'd.

모모SOLO.

모모Why, young orphan, all this wailing?

모모"Would to heaven that I were dead!For my guardian's craft prevailing

모모Soon will make me beg my bread."Knowing well the rascal genus,

모모Into court I dragg'd the knave;Fair the judges were between us,

모모And the maiden's wealth did save.

모모CHORUS.

모모Surely we for wine may languish!

모모Let the bumper then go round!For all sighs and groans of anguish

모모Thou to-day in joy hast drown'd.

모모SOLO.

모모To a little fellow, quiet,

모모Unpretending and subdued,Has a big clown, running riot,

모모Been to-day extremely rude.I bethought me of my duty,

모모And my courage swell'd apace,So I spoil'd the rascal's beauty,

모모Slashing him across the face.

모모CHORUS.

모모Surely we for wine may languish!

모모Let the bumper then go round!For all sighs and groans of anguish

모모Thou to-day in joy hast drown'd.

모모SOLO.

모모Brief must be my explanation,

모모For I really have done nought.Free from trouble and vexation,

모모I a landlord's business bought.There I've done, with all due ardour,

모모All that duty order'd me;Each one ask'd me for the larder,

모모And there was no scarcity.

모모CHORUS.

모모Surely we for wine may languish!

모모Let the bumper then go round!For all sighs and groans of anguish

모모Thou to-day in joy hast drown'd.

모모LEADER.

모모Each should thus make proclamation

모모Of what he did well to-day!That's the match whose conflagration

모모Should inflame our tuneful lay.Let it be our precept ever

모모To admit no waverer here!For to act the good endeavour,

모모None but rascals meek appear.

모모CHORUS.

모모Surely we for wine may languish!

모모Let the bumper then go round!For all sighs and groans of anguish

모모We have now in rapture drown'd.

모모TRIO.

모모Let each merry minstrel enter,

모모He's right welcome to our hall!'Tis but with the self캆ormentor

모모That we are not liberal;

모모For we fear that his caprices,

모모That his eye-brows dark and sad,That his grief that never ceases

모모Hide an empty heart, or bad.

모모CHORUS.

모모No one now for wine shall languish!

모모Here no minstrel shall be found,Who all sighs and groans of anguish,

모모Has not first in rapture drown'd!

모모1810.-----ERGO BIBAMUS!

모모FOR a praiseworthy object we're now gather'd here,

모모So, brethren, sing: ERGO BIBAMUS!Tho' talk may be hush'd, yet the glasses ring clear,

모모Remember then: ERGO BIBAMUS!In truth 'tis an old, 'tis an excellent word,With its sound so befitting each bosom is stirr'd,And an echo the festal hall filling is heard,

모모A glorious ERGO BIBAMUS!

모모I saw mine own love in her beauty so rare,

모모And bethought me of: ERGO BIBAMUS;So I gently approach'd, and she let me stand there,

모모While I help'd myself, thinking: BIBAMUS!And when she's appeased, and will clasp you and kiss,Or when those embraces and kisses ye miss,Take refuge, till sound is some worthier bliss,

모모In the comforting ERGO BIBAMUS!

모모I am call'd by my fate far away from each friend;

모모Ye loved ones, then: ERGO BIBAMUS!With wallet light-laden from hence I must wend.

모모So double our ERGO BIBAMUS!Whate'er to his treasures the niggard may add,Yet regard for the joyous will ever be had,For gladness lends over its charms to the glad,

모모So, brethren, sing; ERGO BIBAMUS!

모모And what shall we say of to-day as it flies?

모모I thought but of: ERGO BIBAMUS'Tis one of those truly that seldom arise,

모모So again and again sing: BIBAMUS!For joy through a wide-open portal it guides,Bright glitter the clouds, as the curtain divides,An a form, a divine one, to greet us in glides,

모모While we thunder our: ERGO BIBAMUS!

모모1810.-----EPIPHANIAS.

모모THE three holy kings with their star's bright ray,--They eat and they drink, but had rather not pay;They like to eat and drink away,They eat and drink, but had rather not pay.

모모The three holy kings have all come here,In number not four, but three they appear;And if a fourth join'd the other three,Increased by one their number would be.

모모The first am I,--the fair and the white,I ought to be seen when the sun shines bright!But, alas! with all my spices and myrrh,No girl now likes me,--I please not her.

모모The next am I,--the brown and the long,Known well to women, known well to song.Instead of spices, 'tis gold I bear,And so I'm welcome everywhere.

모모The last am I,--the black and small,And fain would be right merry withal.I like to eat and to drink full measure,I eat and drink, and give thanks with pleasure.

모모The three holy kings are friendly and mild,They seek the Mother, and seek the Child;The pious Joseph is sitting by,The ox and the ass on their litter lie.

모모We're bringing gold, we're bringing myrrh,The women incense always prefer;And if we have wine of a worthy growth,We three to drink like six are not loth.

모모As here we see fair lads and lasses,But not a sign of oxen or asses,We know that we have gone astrayAnd so go further on our way.-----

모모BALLADS.

모모-----Poet's art is ever ableTo endow with truth mere fable.----MIGNON.[This universally known poem is also to be found in WilhelmMeister.]

모모KNOW'ST thou the land where the fair citron blows,Where the bright orange midst the foliage glows,Where soft winds greet us from the azure skies,Where silent myrtles, stately laurels rise,Know'st thou it well?

모모'Tis there, 'tis there,That I with thee, beloved one, would repair.

모모Know'st thou the house? On columns rests its pile,Its halls are gleaming, and its chambers smile,And marble statues stand and gaze on me:"Poor child! what sorrow hath befallen thee?"Know'st thou it well?

모모'Tis there, 'tis there,That I with thee, protector, would repair!

모모Know'st thou the mountain, and its cloudy bridge?The mule can scarcely find the misty ridge;In caverns dwells the dragon's olden brood,The frowning crag obstructs the raging flood.Know'st thou it well?

모모'Tis there, 'tis there,Our path lies--Father--thither, oh repair!

모모1795.*-----THE MINSTREL.

모모[This fine poem is introduced in the second book of WilhelmMeister.]

모모"WHAT tuneful strains salute mine ear

모모Without the castle walls?Oh, let the song re-echo here,

모모Within our festal halls!"Thus spake the king, the page out-hied;The boy return'd; the monarch cried:

모모"Admit the old man yonder!"

모모"All hail, ye noble lords to-night!

모모All hail, ye beauteous dames!Star placed by star! What heavenly sight!

모모Whoe'er can tell their names?Within this glittering hall sublime,Be closed, mine eyes! 'tis not the time

모모For me to feast my wonder."

모모The minstrel straightway closed his eyes,

모모And woke a thrilling tone;The knights look'd on in knightly guise,

모모Fair looks tow'rd earth were thrown.The monarch, ravish'd by the strain,Bade them bring forth a golden chain,

모모To be his numbers' guerdon.

모모"The golden chain give not to me,

모모But give the chain to thoseIn whose bold face we shiver'd see

모모The lances of our foes.Or give it to thy chancellor there;With other burdens he may bear

모모This one more golden burden.

모모"I sing, like birds of blithesome note,

모모That in the branches dwell;The song that rises from the throat

모모Repays the minstrel well.One boon I'd crave, if not too bold--One bumper in a cup of gold

모모Be as my guerdon given."

모모The bowl he raised, the bowl he quaff'd:

모모"Oh drink, with solace fraught!Oh, house thrice-blest, where such a draught

모모A trifling gift is thought!When Fortune smiles, remember me,And as I thank you heartily,

모모As warmly thank ye Heaven!"

모모1795.*-----BALLAD

모모OF THE BANISHED AND RETURNING COUNT.

모모[Goethe began to write an opera called Lowenstuhl, founded uponthe old tradition which forms the subject of this Ballad, but henever carried out his design.]

모모OH, enter old minstrel, thou time-honour'd one!We children are here in the hall all alone,

모모The portals we straightway will bar.Our mother is praying, our father is gone

모모To the forest, on wolves to make war.Oh sing us a ballad, the tale then repeat,

모모'Till brother and I learn it right;We long have been hoping a minstrel to meet,

모모For children hear tales with delight.

모모"At midnight, when darkness its fearful veil weaves,His lofty and stately old castle he leaves,

모모But first he has buried his wealth.What figure is that in his arms one perceives,

모모As the Count quits the gateway by stealth?O'er what is his mantle so hastily thrown?

모모What bears he along in his flight?A daughter it is, and she gently sleeps on"--

모모The children they hear with delight.

모모"The morning soon glimmers. the world is so wide,In valleys and forests a home is supplied,

모모The bard in each village is cheer'd.Thus lives he and wanders, while years onward glide,

모모And longer still waxes his beard;But the maiden so fair in his arms grows amain,

모모'Neath her star all-protecting and bright,Secured in the mantle from wind and from rain--"

모모The children they hear with delight.

모모"And year upon year with swift footstep now steals,The mantle it fades, many rents it reveals,

모모The maiden no more it can hold.The father he sees her, what rapture he feels!

모모His joy cannot now be controll'd.How worthy she seems of the race whence she springs,

모모How noble and fair to the sight!What wealth to her dearly-loved father she brings!"--

모모The children they hear with delight.

모모"Then comes there a princely knight galloping by,She stretches her hand out, as soon as he's nigh,

모모But alms he refuses to give.He seizes her hand, with a smile in his eye:

모모'Thou art mine!' he exclaims, 'while I live!''When thou know'st,' cries the old man, 'the treasure that'sthere,

모모A princess thou'lt make her of right;Betroth'd be she now, on this spot green and fair--'"

모모The children they hear with delight.

모모"So she's bless'd by the priest on the hallowed place,And she goes with a smiling but sorrowful face,

모모From her father she fain would not part.The old man still wanders with ne'er-changing pace,

모모He covers with joy his sad heart.So I think of my daughter, as years pass away,

모모And my grandchildren far from my sight;I bless them by night, and I bless them by day"--

모모The children they hear with delight.

모모He blesses the children: a knocking they hear,The father it is! They spring forward in fear,

모모The old man they cannot conceal--"Thou beggar, wouldst lure, then, my children so dear?

모모Straight seize him, ye vassals of steel!To the dungeon most deep, with the fool-hardy knave!"

모모The mother from far hears the fight;She hastens with flatt'ring entreaty to crave--

모모The children they hear with delight.

모모The vassals they suffer the Bard to stand there,And mother and children implore him to spare,

모모The proud prince would stifle his ire,'Till driven to fury at hearing their prayer,

모모His smouldering anger takes fire:"Thou pitiful race! Oh, thou beggarly crew!

모모Eclipsing my star, once so bright!Ye'll bring me destruction, ye sorely shall rue!"

모모The children they hear with affright.

모모The old man still stands there with dignified mien,The vassals of steel quake before him, I ween,

모모The Count's fury increases in power;"My wedded existence a curse long has been,

모모And these are the fruits from that flower!'Tis ever denied, and the saying is true,

모모That to wed with the base-born is right;The beggar has borne me a beggarly crew,--"

모모The children they hear with affright.

모모"If the husband, the father, thus treats you with scorn,If the holiest bonds by him rashly are torn,

모모Then come to your father--to me!The beggar may gladden life's pathway forlorn,

모모Though aged and weak he may be.This castle is mine! thou hast made it thy prey,

모모Thy people 'twas put me to flight;The tokens I bear will confirm what I say"--

모모The children they hear with delight.

모모"The king who erst govern'd returneth again,And restores to the Faithful the goods that were ta'en,

모모I'll unseal all my treasures the while;The laws shall be gentle, and peaceful the reign"--

모모The old man thus cries with a smile--"Take courage, my son! all hath turned out for good,

모모And each hath a star that is bright,Those the princess hath borne thee are princely in blood,"--

모모The children thy hear with delight.

모모1816.-----THE VIOLET.

모모UPON the mead a violet stood,Retiring, and of modest mood,

모모In truth, a violet fair.Then came a youthful shepherdess,And roam'd with sprightly joyousness,And blithely woo'd

모모With carols sweet the air

모모"Ah!" thought the violet, "had I beenFor but the smallest moment e'en

모모Nature's most beauteous flower,'Till gather'd by my love, and press'd,When weary, 'gainst her gentle breast,For e'en, for e'en

모모One quarter of an hour!"

모모Alas! alas! the maid drew nigh,The violet failed to meet her eye,

모모She crush'd the violet sweet.It sank and died, yet murmur'd not:"And if I die, oh, happy lot,For her I die,

모모And at her very feet!"

모모1775.*-----THE FAITHLESS BOY.

모모THERE was a wooer blithe and gay,

모모A son of France was he,--Who in his arms for many a day,

모모As though his bride were she,A poor young maiden had caress'd,And fondly kiss'd, and fondly press'd,

모모And then at length deserted.

모모When this was told the nut-brown maid,

모모Her senses straightway fled;She laugh'd and wept, and vow'd and pray'd,

모모And presently was dead.The hour her soul its farewell took,The boy was sad, with terror shook,

모모Then sprang upon his charger.

모모He drove his spurs into his side,

모모And scour'd the country round;But wheresoever he might ride,

모모No rest for him was found.For seven long days and nights he rode,It storm'd, the waters overflow'd,

모모It bluster'd, lighten'd, thunder'd.

모모On rode he through the tempest's din,

모모Till he a building spied;In search of shelter crept he in,

모모When he his steed had tied.And as he groped his doubtful way,The ground began to rock and sway,--

모모He fell a hundred fathoms.

모모When he recover'd from the blow,

모모He saw three lights pass by;He sought in their pursuit to go,

모모The lights appear'd to fly.They led his footsteps all astray,Up, down, through many a narrow way

모모Through ruin'd desert cellars.

모모When lo! he stood within a hall,

모모With hollow eyes. and grinning all;They bade him taste the fare.

모모A hundred guests sat there.He saw his sweetheart 'midst the throng,Wrapp'd up in grave-clothes white and long;

모모She turn'd, and----*

모모1774.(* This ballad is introduced in Act II. of Claudine of VillaBella, where it is suddenly broken off, as it is here.)-----THE ERL-KING.

모모WHO rides there so late through the night dark and drear?The father it is, with his infant so dear;He holdeth the boy tightly clasp'd in his arm,He holdeth him safely, he keepeth him warm.

모모"My son, wherefore seek'st thou thy face thus to hide?""Look, father, the Erl-King is close by our side!Dost see not the Erl-King, with crown and with train?""My son, 'tis the mist rising over the plain."

모모"Oh, come, thou dear infant! oh come thou with me!Full many a game I will play there with thee;On my strand, lovely flowers their blossoms unfold,My mother shall grace thee with garments of gold."

모모"My father, my father, and dost thou not hearThe words that the Erl-King now breathes in mine ear?""Be calm, dearest child, 'tis thy fancy deceives;'Tis the sad wind that sighs through the withering leaves."

모모"Wilt go, then, dear infant, wilt go with me there?My daughters shall tend thee with sisterly careMy daughters by night their glad festival keep,They'll dance thee, and rock thee, and sing thee to sleep."

모모"My father, my father, and dost thou not see,How the Erl-King his daughters has brought here for me?""My darling, my darling, I see it aright,'Tis the aged grey willows deceiving thy sight."

모모"I love thee, I'm charm'd by thy beauty, dear boy!And if thou'rt unwilling, then force I'll employ.""My father, my father, he seizes me fast,Full sorely the Erl-King has hurt me at last."

모모The father now gallops, with terror half wild,He grasps in his arms the poor shuddering child;He reaches his courtyard with toil and with dread,--The child in his arms finds he motionless, dead.

모모1782.*-----JOHANNA SEBUS.

모모[To the memory of an excellent and beautiful girl of 17,belonging to the village of Brienen, who perished on the 13th ofJanuary, 1809, whilst giving help on the occasion of the breakingup of the ice on the Rhine, and the bursting of the dam ofCleverham.]

모모THE DAM BREAKS DOWN, THE ICE-PLAIN GROWLS,THE FLOODS ARISE, THE WATER HOWLS.

모모"I'll bear thee, mother, across the swell,

모모'Tis not yet high, I can wade right well."

모모"Remember us too! in what danger are we!

모모Thy fellow-lodger, and children three!

모모The trembling woman!--Thou'rt going away!"

모모She bears the mother across the spray.

모모"Quick! haste to the mound, and awhile there wait,

모모I'll soon return, and all will be straight.

모모The mound's close by, and safe from the wet;

모모But take my goat too, my darling pet!"

모모THE DAM DISSOLVES, THE ICE-PLAIN GROWLS,THE FLOODS DASH ON, THE WATER HOWLS.

모모She places the mother safe on the shore;

모모Fair Susan then turns tow'rd the flood once more.

모모"Oh whither? Oh whither? The breadth fast grows,

모모Both here and there the water o'erflows.

모모Wilt venture, thou rash one, the billows to brave?""THEY SHALL, AND THEY MUST BE PRESERVED FROM THE WAVE!"

모모THE DAM DISAPPEARS, THE WATER GROWLS,LIKE OCEAN BILLOWS IT HEAVES AND HOWLS.

모모Fair Susan returns by the way she had tried,

모모The waves roar around, but she turns not aside;

모모She reaches the mound, and the neighbour straight,

모모But for her and the children, alas, too late!

모모THE DAM DISAPPEAR'D,--LIKE A SEA IT GROWLS,ROUND THE HILLOCK IN CIRCLING EDDIES IT HOWLS.

모모The foaming abyss gapes wide, and whirls round,

모모The women and children are borne to the ground;

모모The horn of the goat by one is seized fast,

모모But, ah, they all must perish at last!

모모Fair Susan still stands-there, untouch'd by the wave;

모모The youngest, the noblest, oh, who now will save?

모모Fair Susan still stands there, as bright as a star,

모모But, alas! all hope, all assistance is far.

모모The foaming waters around her roar,

모모To save her, no bark pushes off from the shore.

모모Her gaze once again she lifts up to Heaven,

모모Then gently away by the flood she is driven.

모모NO DAM, NO PLAIN! TO MARK THE PLACESOME STRAGGLING TREES ARE THE ONLY TRACE.

모모The rushing water the wilderness covers,

모모Yet Susan's image still o'er it hovers.--

모모The water sinks, the plains re-appear.

모모Fair Susan's lamented with many a tear,--

모모May he who refuses her story to tell,

모모Be neglected in life and in death as well!

모모1809.-----THE FISHERMAN.

모모THE waters rush'd, the waters rose,

모모A fisherman sat by,While on his line in calm repose

모모He cast his patient eye.And as he sat, and hearken'd there,

모모The flood was cleft in twain,And, lo! a dripping mermaid fair

모모Sprang from the troubled main.

모모She sang to him, and spake the while:

모모"Why lurest thou my brood,With human wit and human guile

모모From out their native flood?Oh, couldst thou know how gladly dart

모모The fish across the sea,Thou wouldst descend, e'en as thou art,

모모And truly happy be!

모모"Do not the sun and moon with grace

모모Their forms in ocean lave?Shines not with twofold charms their face,

모모When rising from the wave?The deep, deep heavens, then lure thee not,--

모모The moist yet radiant blue,--Not thine own form,--to tempt thy lot

모모'Midst this eternal dew?"

모모The waters rush'd, the waters rose,

모모Wetting his naked feet;As if his true love's words were those,

모모His heart with longing beat.She sang to him, to him spake she,

모모His doom was fix'd, I ween;Half drew she him, and half sank he,

모모And ne'er again was seen.

모모1779.*-----THE KING OF THULE.*

모모(* This ballad is also introduced in Faust, where it is sung byMargaret.)

모모IN Thule lived a monarch,

모모Still faithful to the grave,To whom his dying mistress

모모A golden goblet gave.

모모Beyond all price he deem'd it,

모모He quaff'd it at each feast;And, when he drain'd that goblet,

모모His tears to flow ne'er ceas'd.

모모And when he felt death near him,

모모His cities o'er he told,And to his heir left all things,

모모But not that cup of gold.

모모A regal banquet held he

모모In his ancestral ball,In yonder sea-wash'd castle,

모모'Mongst his great nobles all.

모모There stood the aged reveller,

모모And drank his last life's-glow,--Then hurl'd the holy goblet

모모Into the flood below.

모모He saw it falling, filling,

모모And sinking 'neath the main,His eyes then closed for ever,

모모He never drank again.

모모1774.-----

모모THE BEAUTEOUS FLOWER.

모모SONG OF THE IMPRISONED COUNT.

모모COUNT.

모모I KNOW a flower of beauty rare,

모모Ah, how I hold it dear!To seek it I would fain repair,

모모Were I not prison'd here.My sorrow sore oppresses me,For when I was at liberty,

모모I had it close beside me.

모모Though from this castle's walls so steep

모모I cast mine eyes around,And gaze oft from the lofty keep,

모모The flower can not be found.Whoe'er would bring it to my sight,Whether a vassal he, or knight,

모모My dearest friend I'd deem him.

모모THE ROSE.

모모I blossom fair,--thy tale of woes

모모I hear from 'neath thy grate.Thou doubtless meanest me, the rose.

모모Poor knight of high estate!Thou hast in truth a lofty mind;The queen of flowers is then enshrin'd,

모모I doubt not, in thy bosom.

모모COUNT.

모모Thy red, in dress of green array'd,

모모As worth all praise I hold;And so thou'rt treasured by each maid

모모Like precious stones or gold.Thy wreath adorns the fairest faceBut still thou'rt not the flower whose grace

모모I honour here in silence.

모모THE LILY.

모모The rose is wont with pride to swell,

모모And ever seeks to rise;But gentle sweethearts love full well

모모The lily's charms to prize,The heart that fills a bosom true,That is, like me, unsullied too,

모모My merit values duly.

모모COUNT.

모모In truth, I hope myself unstain'd,

모모And free from grievous crime;Yet I am here a prisoner chain'd,

모모And pass in grief my time,To me thou art an image sureOf many a maiden, mild and pure,

모모And yet I know a dearer.

모모THE PINK.

모모That must be me, the pink, who scent

모모The warder's garden here;Or wherefore is he so intent

모모My charms with care to rear?My petals stand in beauteous ring,Sweet incense all around I fling,

모모And boast a thousand colours.

모모COUNT.

모모The pink in truth we should not slight,

모모It is the gardener's prideIt now must stand exposed to light,

모모Now in the shade abide.Yet what can make the Count's heart glowIs no mere pomp of outward show;

모모It is a silent flower.

모모THE VIOLET.

모모Here stand I, modestly half hid,

모모And fain would silence keep;Yet since to speak I now am bid,

모모I'll break my silence deep.If, worthy Knight, I am that flower,It grieves me that I have not power

모모To breathe forth all my sweetness.

모모COUNT.

모모The violet's charms I prize indeed,

모모So modest 'tis, and fair,And smells so sweet; yet more I need

모모To ease my heavy care.The truth I'll whisper in thine ear:Upon these rocky heights so drear,

모모I cannot find the loved one.

모모The truest maiden 'neath the sky

모모Roams near the stream below,And breathes forth many a gentle sigh,

모모Till I from hence can go.And when she plucks a flow'ret blue,And says "Forget-me-not!"--I, too,

모모Though far away, can feel it.

모모Ay, distance only swells love's might,

모모When fondly love a pair;Though prison'd in the dungeon's night,

모모In life I linger thereAnd when my heart is breaking nigh,"Forget-me-not!" is all I cry,

모모And straightway life returneth.

모모1798.-----SIR CURT'S WEDDING-JOURNEY.

모모WITH a bridegroom's joyous bearing,

모모Mounts Sir Curt his noble beast,To his mistress' home repairing,

모모There to hold his wedding feast;When a threatening foe advances

모모From a desert, rocky spot;For the fray they couch their lances,

모모Not delaying, speaking not.

모모Long the doubtful fight continues,

모모Victory then for Curt declares;Conqueror, though with wearied sinews,

모모Forward on his road he fares.When he sees, though strange it may be,

모모Something 'midst the foliage move;'Tis a mother, with her baby,

모모Stealing softly through the grove!

모모And upon the spot she beckons--

모모"Wherefore, love, this speed so wild?Of the wealth thy storehouse reckons,

모모Hast thou nought to give thy child!"Flames of rapture now dart through him,

모모And he longs for nothing more,While the mother seemeth to him

모모Lovely as the maid of yore.

모모But he hears his servants blowing,

모모And bethinks him of his bride;And ere long, while onward going,

모모Chances past a fair to ride;In the booths he forthwith buys him

모모For his mistress many a pledge;But, alas! some Jews surprise him,

모모And long-standing debts allege.

모모And the courts of justice duly

모모Send the knight to prison straight.Oh accursed story, truly!

모모For a hero, what a fate!Can my patience such things weather?

모모Great is my perplexity.Women, debts, and foes together,--

모모Ah, no knight escapes scot free!

모모1803.*-----WEDDING SONG.

모모THE tale of the Count our glad song shall record

모모Who had in this castle his dwelling,Where now ye are feasting the new-married lord,

모모His grandson of whom we are telling.The Count as Crusader had blazon'd his fame,Through many a triumph exalted his name,And when on his steed to his dwelling he came,

모모His castle still rear'd its proud head,But servants and wealth had all fled.

모모'Tis true that thou, Count, hast return'd to thy home,

모모But matters are faring there ill.The winds through the chambers at liberty roam,

모모And blow through the windows at willWhat's best to be done in a cold autumn night?Full many I've pass'd in more piteous plight;The morn ever settles the matter aright.

모모Then quick, while the moon shines so clear,

모모To bed on the straw, without fear,

모모And whilst in a soft pleasing slumber he lay,

모모A motion he feels 'neath his bed.The rat, an he likes it, may rattle away!

모모Ay, had he but crumbs there outspread!But lo! there appears a diminutive wight,A dwarf 'tis, yet graceful, and bearing a light,With orator-gestures that notice invite,

모모At the feet of the Count on the floor

모모Who sleeps not, though weary full sore.

모모"We've long been accustom'd to hold here our feast,

모모Since thou from thy castle first went;And as we believed thou wert far in the East,

모모To revel e'en now we were bent.And if thou'lt allow it, and seek not to chide,We dwarfs will all banquet with pleasure and pride,To honour the wealthy, the beautiful bride

모모Says the Count with a smile, half-asleep;--

모모"Ye're welcome your quarters to keep!"

모모Three knights then advance, riding all in a group,

모모Who under the bed were conceal'd;And then is a singing and noise-making troop

모모Of strange little figures reveal'd;And waggon on waggon with all kinds of things--The clatter they cause through the ear loudly rings--The like ne'er was seen save in castles of kings;

모모At length, in a chariot of gold,

모모The bride and the guests too, behold!

모모Then all at full gallop make haste to advance,

모모Each chooses his place in the hall;With whirling and waltzing, and light joyous dance,

모모They begin with their sweethearts the ball.The fife and the fiddle all merrily sound,Thy twine, and they glide, and with nimbleness bound,Thy whisper, and chatter, and, chatter around;

모모The Count on the scene casts his eye,

모모And seems in a fever to lie.

모모They hustle, and bustle, and rattle away

모모On table, on bench, and on stool;Then all who had joined in the festival gay

모모With their partners attempt to grow cool.The hams and the sausages nimbly they bear,And meat, fish, and poultry in plenty are there,Surrounded with wine of the vintage most rare:

모모And when they have revell'd full long,

모모They vanish at last with a song.

모모*      *      *      *      *      *

모모And if we're to sing all that further occurr'd,

모모Pray cease ye to bluster and prate;For what he so gladly in small saw and heard

모모He enjoy'd and he practis'd in great.For trumpets, and singing, and shouts without endOn the bridal-train, chariots and horsemen attend,They come and appear, and they bow and they bend,

모모In merry and countless array.

모모Thus was it, thus is it to-day.

모모1802.-----THE TREASURE-DIGGER

모모ALL my weary days I pass'd

모모Sick at heart and poor in purse.

모모Poverty's the greatest curse,

모모Riches are the highest good!And to end my woes at last,

모모Treasure-seeking forth I sped.

모모"Thou shalt have my soul instead!"

모모Thus I wrote, and with my blood.

모모Ring round ring I forthwith drew,

모모Wondrous flames collected there,

모모Herbs and bones in order fair,

모모Till the charm had work'd aright.Then, to learned precepts true,

모모Dug to find some treasure old,

모모In the place my art foretold

모모Black and stormy was the night.

모모Coming o'er the distant plain,

모모With the glimmer of a star,

모모Soon I saw a light afar,

모모As the hour of midnight knell'd.Preparation was in vain.

모모Sudden all was lighted up

모모With the lustre of a cup

모모That a beauteous boy upheld.

모모Sweetly seem'd his eves to laugh

모모Neath his flow'ry chaplet's load;

모모With the drink that brightly glow'd,

모모He the circle enter'd in.And he kindly bade me quaff:

모모Then methought "This child can ne'er,

모모With his gift so bright and fair,

모모To the arch-fiend be akin."

모모"Pure life's courage drink!" cried he:"This advice to prize then learn,--

모모Never to this place return

모모Trusting in thy spells absurd;Dig no longer fruitlessly.

모모Guests by night, and toil by day!

모모Weeks laborious, feast-days gay!

모모Be thy future magic-word!

모모1797.-----THE RAT-CATCHER.

모모I AM the bard known far and wide,The travell'd rat-catcher beside;A man most needful to this town,So glorious through its old renown.However many rats I see,How many weasels there may be,I cleanse the place from ev'ry one,All needs must helter-skelter run.

모모Sometimes the bard so full of cheerAs a child-catcher will appear,Who e'en the wildest captive brings,Whene'er his golden tales he sings.However proud each boy in heart,However much the maidens start,I bid the chords sweet music make,And all must follow in my wake.

모모Sometimes the skilful bard ye viewIn the form of maiden-catcher too;For he no city enters e'er,Without effecting wonders there.However coy may be each maid,However the women seem afraid,Yet all will love-sick be ere longTo sound of magic lute and song.

모모[Da Capo.]                  1803.*-----

모모THE SPINNER.

모모As I calmly sat and span,

모모Toiling with all zeal,Lo! a young and handsome man

모모Pass'd my spinning-wheel.

모모And he praised,--what harm was there?--

모모Sweet the things he said--Praised my flax-resembling hair,

모모And the even thread.

모모He with this was not content,

모모But must needs do more;And in twain the thread was rent,

모모Though 'twas safe before.

모모And the flax's stonelike weight

모모Needed to be told;But no longer was its state

모모Valued as of old.

모모When I took it to the weaver,

모모Something felt I start,And more quickly, as with fever,

모모Throbb'd my trembling heart.

모모Then I bear the thread at length

모모Through the heat, to bleach;But, alas, I scarce have strength

모모To the pool to reach.

모모What I in my little room

모모Span so fine and slight,--As was likely. I presume--

모모Came at last to light.

모모1800.*-----BEFORE A COURT OF JUSTICE.

모모THE father's name ye ne'er shall be told

모모Of my darling unborn life;"Shame, shame," ye cry, "on the strumpet bold!"

모모Yet I'm an honest wife.

모모To whom I'm wedded, ye ne'er shall be told,

모모Yet he's both loving and fair;He wears on his neck a chain of gold,

모모And a hat of straw doth he wear.

모모If scorn 'tis vain to seek to repel,

모모On me let the scorn be thrown.I know him well, and he knows me well,

모모And to God, too, all is known.

모모Sir Parson and Sir Bailiff, again,

모모I pray you, leave me in peace!My child it is, my child 'twill remain,

모모So let your questionings cease!

모모1815.*-----THE PAGE AND THE MILLER'S DAUGHTER.

모모PAGE.

모모WHERE goest thou? Where?Miller's daughter so fair!

모모Thy name, pray?--

모모MILLER'S DAUGHTER.

모모'Tis Lizzy.

모모PAGE.Where goest thou? Where?With the rake in thy hand?

모모MILLER'S DAUGHTER.Father's meadows and land

모모To visit, I'm busy.

모모PAGE.Dost go there alone?

모모MILLER'S DAUGHTER.By this rake, sir, 'tis shown

모모That we're making the hay;And the pears ripen fastIn the garden at last,

모모So I'll pick them to-day.

모모PAGE.Is't a silent thicket I yonder view?

모모MILLER'S DAUGHTER.Oh, yes! there are two;There's one on each side.

모모PAGE.I'll follow thee soon;When the sun burns at noonWe'll go there, o'urselves from his rays to hide,And then in some glade all-verdant and deep--

모모MILLER'S DAUGHTER.Why, people would say--

모모PAGE.Within mine arms thou gently wilt sleep.

모모MILLER'S DAUGHTER.

모모Your pardon, I pray!Whoever is kiss'd by the miller-maid,Upon the spot must needs be betray'd.

모모'Twould give me distress

모모To cover with whiteYour pretty dark dress.Equal with equal! then all is right!That's the motto in which I delight.I am in love with the miller-boy;He wears nothing that I could destroy.

모모1797.-----THE YOUTH AND THE MILLSTREAM.

모모[This sweet Ballad, and the one entitled The Maid of the Mill'sRepentance, were written on the occasion of a visit paid by Goetheto Switzerland. The Maid of the Mill's Treachery, to which thelatter forms the sequel, was not written till the following year.]

모모YOUTH.

모모SAY, sparkling streamlet, whither thou

모모Art going!With joyous mien thy waters now

모모Are flowing.Why seek the vale so hastily?Attend for once, and answer me!

모모MILLSTREAM.

모모Oh youth, I was a brook indeed;

모모But latelyMy bed they've deepen'd, and my speed

모모Swell'd greatly,That I may haste to yonder mill.And so I'm full and never still.

모모YOUTH.

모모The mill thou seekest in a mood

모모Contented,And know'st not how my youthful blood

모모'S tormented.But doth the miller's daughter fairGaze often on thee kindly there?

모모MILLSTREAM.

모모She opes the shutters soon as light

모모Is gleaming;And comes to bathe her features bright

모모And beaming.So full and snow-white is her breast,--I feel as hot as steam suppress'd.

모모YOUTH.

모모If she in water can inflame

모모Such ardour,Surely, then, flesh and blood to tame

모모Is harder.When once is seen her beauteous face,One ever longs her steps to trace.

모모MILLSTREAM.

모모Over the wheel I, roaring, bound,

모모All-proudly,And ev'ry spoke whirls swiftly round,

모모And loudly.Since I have seen the miller's daughter,With greater vigour flows the water.

모모YOUTH.

모모Like others, then, can grief, poor brook,

모모Oppress thee?"Flow on!"--thus she'll, with smiling look,

모모Address thee.With her sweet loving glance, oh say,Can she thy flowing current stay?

모모MILLSTREAM.

모모'Tis sad, 'tis sad to have to speed

모모From yonder;I wind, and slowly through the mead

모모Would wander;And if the choice remain'd with me,Would hasten back there presently.

모모YOUTH.

모모Farewell, thou who with me dost prove

모모Love's sadness!Perchance some day thou'lt breathe of love

모모And gladness.Go, tell her straight, and often too,The boy's mute hopes and wishes true.

모모1797.-----

모모THE MAID OF THE MILL'S TREACHERY.

모모[This Ballad is introduced in the Wanderjahre, in a tale calledThe Foolish Pilgrim.]

모모WHENCE comes our friend so hastily,

모모When scarce the Eastern sky is grey?Hath he just ceased, though cold it be,

모모In yonder holy spot to pray?The brook appears to hem his path,

모모Would he barefooted o'er it go?Why curse his orisons in wrath,

모모Across those heights beclad with snow?

모모Alas! his warm bed he bath left,

모모Where he had look'd for bliss, I ween;And if his cloak too, had been reft,

모모How fearful his disgrace had been!By yonder villain sorely press'd,

모모His wallet from him has been torn;Our hapless friend has been undress'd,

모모Left well nigh naked as when born.

모모The reason why he came this road,

모모Is that he sought a pair of eyes,Which, at the mill, as brightly glow'd

모모As those that are in Paradise.He will not soon again be there;

모모From out the house he quickly hied,And when he gain'd the open air,

모모Thus bitterly and loudly cried

모모"Within her gaze, so dazzling bright,

모모No word of treachery I could read;She seem'd to see me with delight,

모모Yet plann'd e'en then this cruel deed!Could I, when basking in her smile,

모모Dream of the treason in her breast?She bade kind Cupid stay awhile,

모모And he was there, to make us blest.

모모"To taste of love's sweet ecstasy

모모Throughout the night, that endless seem'd,And for her mother's help to cry

모모Only when morning sunlight beam'd!A dozen of her kith and kin,

모모A very human flood, in-press'dHer cousins came, her aunts peer'd in,

모모And uncles, brothers, and the rest.

모모"Then what a tumult, fierce and loud!

모모Each seem'd a beast of prey to be;The maiden's honour all the crowd,

모모With fearful shout, demand of me.Why should they, madmen-like, begin

모모To fall upon a guiltless youth?For he who such a prize would win,

모모Far nimbler needs must be, in truth.

모모"The way to follow up with skill

모모His freaks, by love betimes is known:He ne'er will leave, within a mill,

모모Sweet flowers for sixteen years alone.--They stole my clothes away,--yes, all!

모모And tried my cloak besides to steal.How strange that any house so small

모모So many rascals could conceal!

모모"Then I sprang up, and raved, and swore,

모모To force a passage through them there.I saw the treacherous maid once more,

모모And she was still, alas, so fairThey all gave way before my wrath,

모모Wild outcries flew about pell-mell;At length I managed to rush forth,

모모With voice of thunder, from that hell.

모모"As maidens of the town we fly,

모모We'll shun you maidens of the village;Leave it to those of quality

모모Their humble worshippers to pillage.Yet if ye are of practised skill,

모모And of all tender ties afraid,Exchange your lovers, if ye will,

모모But never let them be betray'd."

모모Thus sings he in the winter-night,

모모While not a blade of grass was green.I laugh'd to see his piteous plight,

모모For it was well-deserved, I ween.And may this be the fate of all,

모모Who treat by day their true loves ill,And, with foolhardy daring, crawl

모모By night to Cupid's treacherous mill!

모모1798.-----THE MAID OF THE MILL'S REPENTANCE.

모모YOUTH.

모모AWAY, thou swarthy witch! Go forth

모모From out my house, I tell thee!Or else I needs must, in my wrath,

모모Expel thee!What's this thou singest so falsely, forsooth,Of love and a maiden's silent truth?

모모Who'll trust to such a story!

모모GIPSY.

모모I sing of a maid's repentant fears,

모모And long and bitter yearning;Her levity's changed to truth and tears

모모All-burning.She dreads no more the threats of her mother,She dreads far less the blows of her brother,

모모Than the dearly loved-one's hatred.

모모YOUTH.

모모Of selfishness sing and treacherous lies,

모모Of murder and thievish plunder!Such actions false will cause no surprise,

모모Or wonder.When  they share their booty, both clothes and purse,--As bad as you gipsies, and even worse,

모모Such tales find ready credence.

모모GIPSY.

모모"Alas, alas! oh what have I done?

모모Can listening aught avail me?I hear him toward my room hasten on,

모모To hail me.My heart beat high, to myself I said:'O would that thou hadst never betray'd

모모That night of love to thy mother!'"

모모YOUTH.

모모Alas! I foolishly ventured there,

모모For the cheating silence misled me;Ah, sweetest! let me to thee repair,--

모모Nor dread me!When suddenly rose a fearful din,Her mad relations came pouring in.

모모My blood still boils in my body!

모모GIPSY.

모모"Oh when will return an hour like this?

모모I pine in silent sadness;I've thrown away my only true bliss

모모With madness.Alas, poor maid! O pity my youth!My brother was then full cruel in troth

모모To treat the loved one so basely!"

모모THE POET.

모모The swarthy woman then went inside,

모모To the spring in the courtyard yonder;Her eyes from their stain she purified,

모모And,--wonder!--Her face and eyes were radiant and bright,And the maid of the mill was disclosed to the sight

모모Of the startled and angry stripling!

모모THE MAID OF THE MILL.

모모Thou sweetest, fairest, dearly-loved life!

모모Before thine anger I cower;But blows I dread not, nor sharp-edged knife,--

모모This hourOf sorrow and love to thee I'll sing,And myself before thy feet I'll fling,

모모And either live or die there!

모모YOUTH.

모모Affection, say, why buried so deep

모모In my heart hast thou lain hidden?By whom hast thou now to awake from thy sleep

모모Been bidden?Ah love, that thou art immortal I see!Nor knavish cunning nor treachery

모모Can destroy thy life so godlike.

모모THE MAID OF THE MILL.

모모If still with as fond and heartfelt love,

모모As thou once didst swear, I'm cherish'd,Then nought of the rapture we used to prove

모모Is perish'd.So take the woman so dear to thy breast!In her young and innocent charms be blest,

모모For all are thine from henceforward!

모모BOTH.

모모Now, sun, sink to rest! Now, sun, arise!

모모Ye stars, be now shining, now darkling!A star of love now gleams in the skies,

모모All-sparkling!As long as the fountain may spring and run,So long will we two be blended in one,

모모Upon each other's bosoms!

모모1797.-----THE TRAVELLER AND THE FARM~MAIDEN.

모모HE.

모모CANST thou give, oh fair and matchless maiden,

모모'Neath the shadow of the lindens yonder,--

모모Where I'd fain one moment cease to wander,--Food and drink to one so heavy laden?

모모SHE.

모모Wouldst thou find refreshment, traveller weary,

모모Bread, ripe fruit and cream to meet thy wishes,--

모모None but Nature's plain and homely dishes,--Near the spring may soothe thy wanderings dreary.

모모HE.

모모Dreams of old acquaintance now pass through me,

모모Ne'er-forgotten queen of hours of blisses.

모모Likenesses I've often found, but this isOne that quite a marvel seemeth to me!

모모SHE.

모모Travellers often wonder beyond measure,

모모But their wonder soon see cause to smother;

모모Fair and dark are often like each other,Both inspire the mind with equal pleasure.

모모HE.

모모Not now for the first time I surrender

모모To this form, in humble adoration;

모모It was brightest midst the constellationIn the hail adorn'd with festal splendour.

모모SHE.

모모Be thou joyful that 'tis in my power

모모To complete thy strange and merry story!

모모Silks behind her, full of purple glory,Floated, when thou saw'st her in that hour.

모모HE.

모모No, in truth, thou hast not sung it rightly!

모모Spirits may have told thee all about it;

모모Pearls and gems they spoke of, do not doubt it,--By her gaze eclipsed,--it gleam'd so brightly!

모모SHE.

모모This one thing I certainly collected:

모모That the fair one--(say nought, I entreat thee!)

모모Fondly hoping once again to meet thee,Many a castle in the air erected.

모모HE.

모모By each wind I ceaselessly was driven,

모모Seeking gold and honour, too, to capture!

모모When my wand'rings end, then oh, what rapture,If to find that form again 'tis given!

모모SHE.

모모'Tis the daughter of the race now banish'd

모모That thou seest, not her likeness only;

모모Helen and her brother, glad though lonely,Till this farm of their estate now vanish'd.

모모HE.

모모But the owner surely is not wanting

모모Of these plains, with ev'ry beauty teeming?

모모Verdant fields, broad meads, and pastures gleaming,Gushing springs, all heav'nly and enchanting.

모모SHE.

모모Thou must hunt the world through, wouldst thou find him!--

모모We have wealth enough in our possession,

모모And intend to purchase the succession,When the good man leaves the world behind him.

모모HE.

모모I have learnt the owner's own condition,

모모And, fair maiden, thou indeed canst buy it;

모모But the cost is great, I won't deny it,--Helen is the price,--with thy permission!

모모SHE.

모모Did then fate and rank keep us asunder,

모모And must Love take this road, and no other?

모모Yonder comes my dear and trusty brother;What will he say to it all, I wonder?

모모1803.*-----EFFECTS AT A DISTANCE.

모모THE queen in the lofty hall takes her place,

모모The tapers around her are flaming;She speaks to the page: "With a nimble pace

모모Go, fetch me my purse for gaming.

모모'Tis lying, I'll pledge,

모모On my table's edge."Each nerve the nimble boy straineth,And the end of the castle soon gaineth.

모모The fairest of maidens was sipping sherbet

모모Beside the queen that minute;Near her mouth broke the cup,--and she got so wet!

모모The very devil seem'd in it

모모What fearful distress

모모'Tis spoilt, her gay dress.She hastens, and ev'ry nerve straineth,And the end of the castle soon gaineth.

모모The boy was returning, and quickly came,

모모And met the sorrowing maiden;None knew of the fact,--and yet with Love's flame,

모모Those two had their hearts full laden.

모모And, oh the bliss

모모Of a moment like this!Each falls on the breast of the other,With kisses that well nigh might smother.

모모They tear themselves asunder at last,

모모To her chamber she hastens quickly,To reach the queen the page hies him fast,

모모Midst the swords and the fans crowded thickly.

모모The queen spied amain

모모On his waistcoat a stain;For nought was inscrutable to her,Like Sheba's queen--Solomon's wooer.

모모To her chief attendant she forthwith cried

모모"We lately together contended,And thou didst assert, with obstinate pride,

모모That the spirit through space never wended,--

모모That traces alone

모모By the present were shown,--That afar nought was fashion'd--not evenBy the stars that illumine you heaven.

모모"Now see! while a goblet beside me they drain'd,

모모They spilt all the drink in the chalice;And straightway the boy had his waistcoat stain'd

모모At the furthermost end of the palace.--

모모Let them newly be clad!

모모And since I am gladThat it served as a proof so decided,The cost will by me be provided."

모모1808.-----THE WALKING BELL

모모A CHILD refused to go betimes

모모To church like other people;He roam'd abroad, when rang the chimes

모모On Sundays from the steeple.

모모His mother said: "Loud rings the bell,

모모Its voice ne'er think of scorning;Unless thou wilt behave thee well,

모모'Twill fetch thee without warning."

모모The child then thought: "High over head

모모The bell is safe suspended--"So to the fields he straightway sped

모모As if 'twas school-time ended.

모모The bell now ceas'd as bell to ring,

모모Roused by the mother's twaddle;But soon ensued a dreadful thing!--

모모The bell begins to waddle.

모모It waddles fast, though strange it seem;

모모The child, with trembling wonder,Runs off, and flies, as in a dream;

모모The bell would draw him under.

모모He finds the proper time at last,

모모And straightway nimbly rushesTo church, to chapel, hastening fast

모모Through pastures, plains, and bushes.

모모Each Sunday and each feast as well,

모모His late disaster heeds he;The moment that he bears the bell,

모모No other summons needs he.

모모1813.-----FAITHFUL ECKART,

모모"OH, would we were further! Oh, would we were home,The phantoms of night tow'rd us hastily come,

모모The band of the Sorceress sisters.They hitherward speed, and on finding us here,They'll drink, though with toil we have fetch'd it, the beer,

모모And leave us the pitchers all empty."

모모Thus speaking, the children with fear take to flight,When sudden an old man appears in their sight:

모모"Be quiet, child! children, be quiet!From hunting they come, and their thirst they would still,So leave them to swallow as much as they will,

모모And the Evil Ones then will be gracious."

모모As said, so 'twas done! and the phantoms draw near,And shadowlike seem they, and grey they appear,

모모~Yet blithely they sip and they revelThe beer has all vanish'd, the pitchers are void;With cries and with shouts the wild hunters, o'erjoy'd,

모모Speed onward o'er vale and o'er mountain.

모모The children in terror fly nimbly tow'rd home,And with them the kind one is careful to come:

모모"My darlings, oh, be not so mournful!--"They'll blame us and beat us, until we are dead."--"No, no! ye will find that all goes well," he said;

모모"Be silent as mice, then, and listen!

모모"And he by whose counsels thus wisely ye're taught,Is he who with children loves ever to sport.

모모The trusty and faithful old Eckart.Ye have heard of the wonder for many a day,But ne'er had a proof of the marvellous lay,--

모모Your hands hold a proof most convincing."

모모They arrive at their home, and their pitchers they placeBy the side of their parents, with fear on their face,

모모Awaiting a beating and scolding.But see what they're tasting: the choicest of beer!Though three times and four times they quaff the good cheer

모모The pitchers remain still unemptied.

모모The marvel it lasts till the dawning of day;All people who hear of it doubtless will say:

모모"What happen'd at length to the pitchers?"In secret the children they smile, as they wait;At last, though, they stammer, and stutter, and prate,

모모And straightway the pitchers were empty.

모모And if, children, with kindness address'd ye may be,Whether father, or master, or alderman he,

모모Obey him, and follow his bidding!And if 'tis unpleasant to bridle the tongue,Yet talking is bad, silence good for the young--

모모And then will the beer fill your pitchers!

모모1813.-----THE DANCE OF DEATH.

모모THE warder looks down at the mid hour of night,

모모On the tombs that lie scatter'd below:The moon fills the place with her silvery light,

모모And the churchyard like day seems to glow.When see! first one grave, then another opes wide,And women and men stepping forth are descried,

모모In cerements snow-white and trailing.

모모In haste for the sport soon their ankles they twitch,

모모And whirl round in dances so gay;The young and the old, and the poor, and the rich,

모모But the cerements stand in their way;And as modesty cannot avail them aught here,They shake themselves all, and the shrouds soon appear

모모Scatter'd over the tombs in confusion.

모모Now waggles the leg, and now wriggles the thigh,

모모As the troop with strange gestures advance,And a rattle and clatter anon rises high,

모모As of one beating time to the dance.The sight to the warder seems wondrously queer,When the villainous Tempter speaks thus in his ear:

모모"Seize one of the shrouds that lie yonder!"

모모Quick as thought it was done! and for safety he fled

모모Behind the church-door with all speed;The moon still continues her clear light to shed

모모On the dance that they fearfully lead.But the dancers at length disappear one by one,And their shrouds, ere they vanish, they carefully don,

모모And under the turf all is quiet.

모모But one of them stumbles and shuffles there still,

모모And gropes at the graves in despair;Yet 'tis by no comrade he's treated so ill

모모The shroud he soon scents in the air.So he rattles the door--for the warder 'tis wellThat 'tis bless'd, and so able the foe to repel,

모모All cover'd with crosses in metal.

모모The shroud he must have, and no rest will allow,

모모There remains for reflection no time;On the ornaments Gothic the wight seizes now,

모모And from point on to point hastes to climb.Alas for the warder! his doom is decreed!Like a long-legged spider, with ne'er-changing speed,

모모Advances the dreaded pursuer.

모모The warder he quakes, and the warder turns pale,

모모The shroud to restore fain had sought;When the end,--now can nothing to save him avail,--

모모In a tooth formed of iron is caught.With vanishing lustre the moon's race is run,When the bell thunders loudly a powerful One,

모모And the skeleton fails, crush'd to atoms.

모모1813.-----THE PUPIL IN MAGIC.

모모I AM now,--what joy to hear it!--

모모Of the old magician rid;And henceforth shall ev'ry spirit

모모Do whate'er by me is bid;

모모I have watch'd with rigour

모모All he used to do,

모모And will now with vigour

모모Work my wonders too.

모모Wander, wander

모모Onward lightly,

모모So that rightly

모모Flow the torrent,

모모And with teeming waters yonder

모모In the bath discharge its current!

모모And now come, thou well-worn broom,

모모And thy wretched form bestir;Thou hast ever served as groom,

모모So fulfil my pleasure, sir!

모모On two legs now stand,

모모With a head on top;

모모Waterpail in hand,

모모Haste, and do not stop!

모모Wander, wander

모모Onward lightly,

모모So that rightly

모모Flow the torrent,

모모And with teeming waters yonder

모모In the bath discharge its current!

모모See! he's running to the shore,

모모And has now attain'd the pool,And with lightning speed once more

모모Comes here, with his bucket full!

모모Back he then repairs;

모모See how swells the tide!

모모How each pail he bears

모모Straightway is supplied!

모모Stop, for, lo!

모모All the measure

모모Of thy treasure

모모Now is right!--

모모Ah, I see it! woe, oh woe!

모모I forget the word of might.

모모Ah, the word whose sound can straight

모모Make him what he was before!Ah, he runs with nimble gait!

모모Would thou wert a broom once more!

모모Streams renew'd for ever

모모Quickly bringeth he;

모모River after river

모모Rusheth on poor me!

모모Now no longer

모모Can I bear him;

모모I will snare him,

모모Knavish sprite!

모모Ah, my terror waxes stronger!

모모What a look! what fearful sight

모모Oh, thou villain child of hell!

모모Shall the house through thee be drown'dFloods I see that wildly swell,

모모O'er the threshold gaining ground.

모모Wilt thou not obey,

모모Oh, thou broom accurs'd?

모모Be thou still I pray,

모모As thou wert at first!

모모Will enough

모모Never please thee?

모모I will seize thee,

모모Hold thee fast,

모모And thy nimble wood so tough,

모모With my sharp axe split at last.

모모See, once more he hastens back!

모모Now, oh Cobold, thou shalt catch it!I will rush upon his track;

모모Crashing on him falls my hatchet.

모모Bravely done, indeed!

모모See, he's cleft in twain!

모모Now from care I'm freed,

모모And can breathe again.

모모Woe, oh woe!

모모Both the parts,

모모Quick as darts,

모모Stand on end,

모모Servants of my dreaded foe!

모모Oh, ye gods protection send!

모모And they run! and wetter still

모모Grow the steps and grows the hail.Lord and master hear me call!

모모Ever seems the flood to fill,

모모Ah, he's coming! see,

모모Great is my dismay!

모모Spirits raised by me

모모Vainly would I lay!

모모"To the side

모모Of the room

모모Hasten, broom,

모모As of old!

모모Spirits I have ne'er untied

모모Save to act as they are told."

모모1797.-----THE BRIDE OF CORINTH.

모모[First published in Schiller's Horen, in connection with afriendly contest in the art of ballad-writing between the twogreat poets, to which many of their finest works are owing.]

모모ONCE a stranger youth to Corinth came,

모모Who in Athens lived, but hoped that heFrom a certain townsman there might claim,

모모As his father's friend, kind courtesy.

모모Son and daughter, they

모모Had been wont to say

모모Should thereafter bride and bridegroom be.

모모But can he that boon so highly prized,

모모Save tis dearly bought, now hope to get?They are Christians and have been baptized,

모모He and all of his are heathens yet.

모모For a newborn creed,

모모Like some loathsome weed,

모모Love and truth to root out oft will threat.

모모Father, daughter, all had gone to rest,

모모And the mother only watches late;She receives with courtesy the guest,
