모모And conducts him to the room of state.

모모Wine and food are brought,

모모Ere by him besought;

모모Bidding him good night. she leaves him straight.

모모But he feels no relish now, in truth,

모모For the dainties so profusely spread;Meat and drink forgets the wearied youth,

모모And, still dress'd, he lays him on the bed.

모모Scarce are closed his eyes,

모모When a form in-hies

모모Through the open door with silent tread.

모모By his glimmering lamp discerns he now

모모How, in veil and garment white array'd,With a black and gold band round her brow,

모모Glides into the room a bashful maid.

모모But she, at his sight,

모모Lifts her hand so white,

모모And appears as though full sore afraid.

모모"Am I," cries she, "such a stranger here,

모모That the guest's approach they could not name?Ah, they keep me in my cloister drear,

모모Well nigh feel I vanquish'd by my shame.

모모On thy soft couch now

모모Slumber calmly thou!

모모I'll return as swiftly as I came."

모모"Stay, thou fairest maiden!" cries the boy,

모모Starting from his couch with eager haste:"Here are Ceres', Bacchus' gifts of joy;

모모Amor bringest thou, with beauty grac'd!

모모Thou art pale with fear!

모모Loved one let us here

모모Prove the raptures the Immortals taste."

모모"Draw not nigh, O Youth! afar remain!

모모Rapture now can never smile on me;For the fatal step, alas! is ta'en,

모모Through my mother's sick-bed phantasy.

모모Cured, she made this oath:

모모'Youth and nature both

모모Shall henceforth to Heav'n devoted be.'

모모"From the house, so silent now, are driven

모모All the gods who reign'd supreme of yore;One Invisible now rules in heaven,

모모On the cross a Saviour they adore.

모모Victims slay they here,

모모Neither lamb nor steer,But the altars reek with human gore."

모모And he lists, and ev'ry word he weighs,

모모While his eager soul drinks in each sound:"Can it be that now before my gaze

모모Stands my loved one on this silent ground?

모모Pledge to me thy troth!

모모Through our father's oath:

모모With Heav'ns blessing will our love be crown'd."

모모"Kindly youth, I never can be thine!

모모'Tis my sister they intend for thee.When I in the silent cloister pine,

모모Ah, within her arms remember me!

모모Thee alone I love,

모모While love's pangs I prove;

모모Soon the earth will veil my misery."

모모"No! for by this glowing flame I swear,

모모Hymen hath himself propitious shown:Let us to my fathers house repair,

모모And thoult find that joy is not yet flown,

모모Sweetest, here then stay,

모모And without delay

모모Hold we now our wedding feast alone!"

모모Then exchange they tokens of their truth;

모모She gives him a golden chain to wear,And a silver chalice would the youth

모모Give her in return of beauty rare.

모모"That is not for me;

모모Yet I beg of thee,One lock only give me of thy hair."

모모Now the ghostly hour of midnight knell'd,

모모And she seem'd right joyous at the sign;To her pallid lips the cup she held,

모모But she drank of nought but blood-red wine.

모모For to taste the bread

모모There before them spread,

모모Nought he spoke could make the maid incline.

모모To the youth the goblet then she brought,--

모모He too quaff'd with eager joy the bowl.Love to crown the silent feast he sought,

모모Ah! full love-sick was the stripling's soul.

모모From his prayer she shrinks,

모모Till at length he sinks

모모On the bed and weeps without control.

모모And she comes, and lays her near the boy:

모모"How I grieve to see thee sorrowing so!If thou think'st to clasp my form with joy,

모모Thou must learn this secret sad to know;

모모Yes! the maid, whom thou

모모Call'st thy loved one now,

모모Is as cold as ice, though white as snow."

모모Then he clasps her madly in his arm,

모모While love's youthful might pervades his frame:"Thou might'st hope, when with me, to grow warm,

모모E'en if from the grave thy spirit came!

모모Breath for breath, and kiss!

모모Overflow of bliss!

모모Dost not thou, like me, feel passion's flame?"

모모Love still closer rivets now their lips,

모모Tears they mingle with their rapture blest,From his mouth the flame she wildly sips,

모모Each is with the other's thought possess'd.

모모His hot ardour's flood

모모Warms her chilly blood,

모모But no heart is beating in her breast.

모모In her care to see that nought went wrong,

모모Now the mother happen'd to draw near;At the door long hearkens she, full long,

모모Wond'ring at the sounds that greet her ear.

모모Tones of joy and sadness,

모모And love's blissful madness,

모모As of bride and bridegroom they appear,

모모From the door she will not now remove

모모'Till she gains full certainty of this;And with anger hears she vows of love,

모모Soft caressing words of mutual bliss.

모모"Hush! the cock's loud strain!

모모But thoult come again,

모모When the night returns!"--then kiss on kiss.

모모Then her wrath the mother cannot hold,

모모But unfastens straight the lock with ease"In this house are girls become so bold,

모모As to seek e'en strangers' lusts to please?"

모모By her lamp's clear glow

모모Looks she in,--and oh!

모모Sight of horror!--'tis her child she sees.

모모Fain the youth would, in his first alarm,

모모With the veil that o'er her had been spread,With the carpet, shield his love from harm;

모모But she casts them from her, void of dread,

모모And with spirit's strength,

모모In its spectre length,

모모Lifts her figure slowly from the bed.

모모"Mother! mother!"--Thus her wan lips say:

모모"May not I one night of rapture share?From the warm couch am I chased away?

모모Do I waken only to despair?

모모It contents not thee

모모To have driven me

모모An untimely shroud of death to wear?

모모"But from out my coffin's prison-bounds

모모By a wond'rous fate I'm forced to rove,While the blessings and the chaunting sounds

모모That your priests delight in, useless prove.

모모Water, salt, are vain

모모Fervent youth to chain,

모모Ah, e'en Earth can never cool down love!

모모"When that infant vow of love was spoken,

모모Venus' radiant temple smiled on both.Mother! thou that promise since hast broken,

모모Fetter'd by a strange, deceitful oath.

모모Gods, though, hearken ne'er,

모모Should a mother swear

모모To deny her daughter's plighted troth.

모모From my grave to wander I am forc'd,

모모Still to seek The Good's long-sever'd link,Still to love the bridegroom I have lost,

모모And the life-blood of his heart to drink;

모모When his race is run,

모모I must hasten on,

모모And the young must 'neath my vengeance sink,

모모"Beauteous youth! no longer mayst thou live;

모모Here must shrivel up thy form so fair;Did not I to thee a token give,

모모Taking in return this lock of hair?

모모View it to thy sorrow!

모모Grey thoult be to-morrow,

모모Only to grow brown again when there.

모모"Mother, to this final prayer give ear!

모모Let a funeral pile be straightway dress'd;Open then my cell so sad and drear,

모모That the flames may give the lovers rest!

모모When ascends the fire

모모From the glowing pyre,

모모To the gods of old we'll hasten, blest."

모모1797.-----THE GOD AND THE BAYADERE.

모모AN INDIAN LEGEND.

모모[This very fine Ballad was also first given in the Horen.](MAHADEVA is one of the numerous names of Seeva, the destroyer,--the great god of the Brahmins.)

모모MAHADEVA,* Lord of earth

모모For the sixth time comes below,

모모As a man of mortal birth,--

모모Like him, feeling joy and woe.

모모Hither loves he to repair,

모모And his power behind to leave;

모모If to punish or to spare,

모모Men as man he'd fain perceive.And when he the town as a trav'ller hath seen,Observing the mighty, regarding the mean,He quits it, to go on his journey, at eve.

모모He was leaving now the place,

모모When an outcast met his eyes,--

모모Fair in form, with painted face,--

모모Where some straggling dwellings rise.

모모"Maiden, hail!"--"Thanks! welcome here!

모모Stay!--I'll join thee in the road.'

모모"Who art thou?"--"A Bayadere,

모모And this house is love's abode."The cymbal she hastens to play for the dance,Well skill'd in its mazes the sight to entrance,Then by her with grace is the nosegay bestow'd.

모모Then she draws him, as in play,

모모O'er the threshold eagerly:

모모"Beauteous stranger, light as day

모모Thou shalt soon this cottage see.

모모I'll refresh thee, if thou'rt tired,

모모And will bathe thy weary feet;

모모Take whate'er by thee's desired,

모모Toying, rest, or rapture sweet."--She busily seeks his feign'd suff'rings to ease;Then smiles the Immortal; with pleasure he seesThat with kindness a heart so corrupted can beat.

모모And he makes her act the part

모모Of a slave; he's straight obey'd.

모모What at first had been but art,

모모Soon is nature in the maid.

모모By degrees the fruit we find,

모모Where the buds at first obtain;

모모When obedience fills the mind,

모모Love will never far remain.But sharper and sharper the maiden to prove,The Discerner of all things below and above,Feigns pleasure, and horror, and maddening pain.

모모And her painted cheeks he kisses,

모모And his vows her heart enthrall;

모모Feeling love's sharp pangs and blisses,

모모Soon her tears begin to fall.

모모At his feet she now must sink,

모모Not with thoughts of lust or gain,--

모모And her slender members shrink,

모모And devoid of power remain.And so the bright hours with gladness prepareTheir dark, pleasing veil of a texture so fair,And over the couch softly, tranquilly reign.

모모Late she falls asleep, thus bless'd,--

모모Early wakes, her slumbers fled,

모모And she finds the much-loved guest

모모On her bosom lying dead.

모모Screaming falls she on him there,

모모But, alas, too late to save!

모모And his rigid limbs they bear

모모Straightway to their fiery grave.Then hears she the priests and the funeral song,Then madly she runs, and she severs the throng:"Why press tow'rd the pile thus? Why scream thus, and rave?"

모모Then she sinks beside his bier,

모모And her screams through air resound:

모모"I must seek my spouse so dear,

모모E'en if in the grave he's bound.

모모Shall those limbs of grace divine

모모Fall to ashes in my sight?

모모Mine he was! Yes, only mine!

모모Ah, one single blissful night!"The priests chaunt in chorus: "We bear out the old,When long they've been weary, and late they've grown cold:We bear out the young, too, so thoughtless and light.

모모"To thy priests' commands give ear!

모모This one was thy husband ne'er;

모모Live still as a Bayadere,

모모And no duty thou need'st share.

모모To deaths silent realms from life,

모모None but shades attend man's frame,

모모With the husband, none but wife,--

모모That is duty, that is fame.Ye trumpets, your sacred lament haste to raiseOh, welcome, ye gods, the bright lustre of days!Oh, welcome to heaven the youth from the flame!"

모모Thus increased her torments are

모모By the cruel, heartless quire;

모모And with arms outstretching far

모모Leaps she on the glowing pyre.

모모But the youth divine outsprings

모모From the flame with heav'nly grace,

모모And on high his flight he wings,

모모While his arms his love embrace.In the sinner repentant the Godhead feels joy;Immortals delight thus their might to employ.Lost children to raise to a heavenly place.

모모1797.-----THE PARIAH.

모모I.  THE PARIAH S PRAYER.

모모DREADED Brama, lord of might!

모모All proceed from thee alone;Thou art he who judgeth right!

모모Dost thou none but Brahmins own?Do but Rajahs come from thee?

모모None but those of high estate?

모모Didst not thou the ape create,Aye, and even such as we?

모모We are not of noble kind,

모모For with woe our lot is rife;And what others deadly find

모모Is our only source of life.Let this be enough for men,

모모Let them, if they will, despise us;

모모But thou, Brama, thou shouldst prize us,All are equal in thy ken.

모모Now that, Lord, this prayer is said,

모모As thy child acknowledge me;Or let one be born in-stead,

모모Who may link me on to thee!Didst not thou a Bayadere

모모As a goddess heavenward raise?

모모And we too to swell thy praise,Such a miracle would hear.

모모1821.-----II. LEGEND.

모모[The successful manner in which Goethe employs the simplerhymeless trochaic metre in this and in many other Poems willperhaps be remarked by the reader.]

모모WATER-FETCHING goes the nobleBrahmin's wife, so pure and lovely;He is honour'd, void of blemish.And of justice rigid, stern.Daily from the sacred riverBrings she back refreshments precious;--But where is the pail and pitcher?She of neither stands in need.For with pure heart, hands unsullied,She the water lifts, and rolls itTo a wondrous ball of crystalThis she bears with gladsome bosom,Modestly, with graceful motion,To her husband in the house.

모모She to-day at dawn of morningPraying comes to Ganges' waters,Bends her o'er the glassy surface--Sudden, in the waves reflected,Flying swiftly far above her,From the highest heavens descending,She discerns the beauteous formOf a youth divine, createdBy the God's primeval wisdomIn his own eternal breast.

모모When she sees him, straightway feels sheWondrous, new, confused sensationsIn her inmost, deepest being;Fain she'd linger o'er the vision,Then repels it,--it returneth,--And, perplex'd, she bends her flood-wardsWith uncertain hands to draw it;But, alas, she draws no more!For the water's sacred billowsSeem to fly, to hasten from her;She but sees the fearful chasmOf a whirlpool black disclosed.

모모Arms drop down, and footsteps stumble,Can this be the pathway homewards?Shall she fly, or shall she tarry?Can she think, when thought and counsel,When assistance all are lost?So before her spouse appears she--On her looks he--look is judgment--Proudly on the sword he seizes,To the hill of death he drags her,Where delinquents' blood pays forfeit.What resistance could she offer?What excuses could she proffer,Guilty, knowing not her guilt?

모모And with bloody sword returns he,Musing, to his silent dwelling,When his son before him stands:"Whose this blood? Oh, father! father!""The delinquent woman's!"--"Never!For upon the sword it dries not,Like the blood of the delinquent;Fresh it flows, as from the wound.Mother! mother! hither hasten!Unjust never was my father,Tell me what he now hath done."--"Silence! silence! hers the blood is!""Whose, my father?"--"Silence! Silence!""What! oh what! my mother's blood!What her crime? What did she? Answer!Now, the sword! the sword now hold I;Thou thy wife perchance might'st slaughter,But my mother might'st not slay!Through the flames the wife is ableHer beloved spouse to follow,And his dear and only motherThrough the sword her faithful son.""Stay! oh stay!" exclaim'd the father:"Yet 'tis time, so hasten, hasten!Join the head upon the body,With the sword then touch the figure,And, alive she'll follow thee."

모모Hastening, he, with breathless wonder,Sees the bodies of two womenLying crosswise, and their heads too;Oh, what horror! which to choose!Then his mother's head he seizes,--Does not kiss it, deadly pale 'tis,--On the nearest headless bodyPuts it quickly, and then blessesWith the sword the pious work.Then the giant form uprises,--From the dear lips of his mother,Lips all god-like--changeless--blissful,Sound these words with horror fraught:"Son, oh son! what overhast'ning!Yonder is thy mother's body,Near it lies the impious headOf the woman who hath fallenVictim to the judgment-sword!To her body I am graftedBy thy hand for endless ages;Wise in counsel, wild in action,I shall be amongst the gods.E'en the heav'nly boy's own image,Though in eye and brow so lovely,Sinking downwards to the bosomMad and raging lust will stir.

모모"'Twill return again for ever,Ever rising, ever sinking,Now obscured, and now transfigur'd,--So great Brama hath ordain'd.He 'twas sent the beauteous pinions,Radiant face and slender membersOf the only God-begotten,That I might be proved and tempted;For from high descends temptation,When the gods ordain it so.And so I, the Brahmin woman,With my head in Heaven reclining,Must experience, as a Pariah,The debasing power of earth.

모모Son, I send thee to thy father!Comfort him! Let no sad penance,Weak delay, or thought of merit,Hold thee in the desert fastWander on through ev'ry nation,Roam abroad throughout all ages,And proclaim to e'en the meanest,That great Brama hears his cry!

모모"None is in his eyes the meanest--He whose limbs are lame and palsied,He whose soul is wildly riven,Worn with sorrow, hopeless, helpless,Be he Brahmin, be he Pariah,If tow'rd heaven he turns his gaze,Will perceive, will learn to know it:Thousand eyes are glowing yonder,Thousand ears are calmly list'ning,From which nought below is hid.

모모"If I to his throne soar upward,If he sees my fearful figureBy his might transform'd to horror,He for ever will lament it,--May it to your good be found!And I now will kindly warn him,And I now will madly tell himWhatsoe'er my mind conceiveth,What within my bosom heaveth.But my thoughts, my inmost feelings--Those a secret shall remain."

모모1821.-----III.  THE PARIAH'S THANKS.

모모MIGHTY Brama, now I'll bless thee!

모모'Tis from thee that worlds proceed!As my ruler I confess thee,

모모For of all thou takest heed.

모모All thy thousand ears thou keepest

모모Open to each child of earth;We, 'mongst mortals sunk the deepest,

모모Have from thee received new birth.

모모Bear in mind the woman's story,

모모Who, through grief, divine became;Now I'll wait to view His glory,

모모Who omnipotence can claim.

모모1821.-----DEATH-LAMENT OF THE NOBLE WIFE OF ASAN AGA.

모모[From the Morlack.)

모모WHAT is yonder white thing in the forest?Is it snow, or can it swans perchance be?Were it snow, ere this it had been melted,Were it swans, they all away had hastend.Snow, in truth, it is not, swans it is not,'Tis the shining tents of Asan Aga.He within is lying, sorely wounded;To him come his mother and his sister;Bashfully his wife delays to come there.When the torment of his wounds had lessen'd,To his faithful wife he sent this message:"At my court no longer dare to tarry,At my court, or e'en amongst my people."

모모When the woman heard this cruel message,Mute and full of sorrow stood that true one.At the doors she hears the feet of horses,And bethinks that Asan comes--her husband,To the tower she springs, to leap thence headlong,Her two darling daughters follow sadly,And whilst weeping bitter tears, exclaim they:These are not our father Asan's horses;'Tis thy brother Pintorowich coming!"

모모So the wife of Asan turns to meet him,Clasps her arms in anguish round her brother:"See thy sister's sad disgrace, oh brother!How I'm banish'd--mother of five children!"Silently her brother from his wallet,Wrapp'd in deep red-silk, and ready written,Draweth forth the letter of divorcement,To return home to her mother's dwelling,Free to be another's wife thenceforward.

모모When the woman saw that mournful letter,Fervently she kiss'd her two sons' foreheads,And her two girls' cheeks with fervour kiss'd she,But she from the suckling in the cradleCould not tear herself, so deep her sorrow!So she's torn thence by her fiery brother,On his nimble steed he lifts her quickly,And so hastens, with the heart-sad woman,Straightway tow'rd his father's lofty dwelling.

모모Short the time was--seven days had pass'd not,--Yet enough 'twas; many mighty princesSought the woman in her widow's-mourning.Sought the woman,--as their wife they sought her.And the mightiest was Imoski's Cadi,And the woman weeping begg'd her brother:By thy life, my brother, I entreat thee,Let me not another's wife be ever,Lest my heart be broken at the imageOf my poor, my dearly-cherish'd children!"

모모To her prayer her brother would not hearken,Fix'd to wed her to Imoski's Cadi.Yet the good one ceaselessly implored him:"Send, at least a letter, oh, my brother,With this message to Imoski's Cadi:'The young widow sends thee friendly greeting;Earnestly she prays thee, through this letter,That, when thou com'st hither, with thy Suatians,A long veil thou'lt bring me, 'neath whose shadowI may hide, when near the house of Asan,And not see my dearly cherish'd orphans.'"

모모Scarcely had the Cadi read this letter,Than he gather'd all his Suatians round him,And then tow'rd the bride his course directed,And the veil she ask'd for, took he with him.

모모Happily they reach'd the princess' dwelling,From the dwelling happily they led her.But when they approach'd the house of Asan,Lo! the children saw from high their mother,And they shouted: "To thy halls return thou!Eat thy supper with thy darling children!"Mournfully the wife of Asan heard it,Tow'rd the Suatian prince then turn'd she, saying:"Let, I pray, the Suatians and the horsesAt the loved ones' door a short time tarry,That I may give presents to my children."

모모And before the loved ones' door they tarried,And she presents gave to her poor children,To the boys gave gold-embroider'd buskins,To the girls gave long and costly dresses,To the suckling, helpless in the cradle,Gave a garment, to be worn hereafter.

모모This aside saw Father Asan Aga,--Sadly cried he to his darling children:"Hither come, ye dear unhappy infants,For your mother's breast is turn'd to iron,Lock'd for ever, closed to all compassion!"

모모When the wife of Asan heard him speak thus,On the ground, all pale and trembling, fell she,And her spirit fled her sorrowing bosom,When she saw her children flying from her.

모모1775.-----

모모CANTATAS.

모모-----May the bard these numbers praise,That are sung his fame to raise.-----

모모THE Poems composed by Goethe under this title are five innumber, of which three are here given. The other two are entirelypersonal in their allusions, and not of general interest. One ofthem is a Requiem on the Prince de Ligne, who died in 1814, andwhom Goethe calls "the happiest man of the century," and theother was composed in honour of the 70th birthday of his friendZelter the composer, when Goethe was himself more than 79 (1828).The following sweet aria introduced in the latter is, however,worth giving:--

모모THE flowers so carefully rear'd,

모모In a garland for him I oft twin'd:How sweet have they ever appear'd,

모모When wreath'd for a friend dear and kind.Then incense sweet ascended,

모모Then new-horn blossoms rose,With gentle zephyrs blended

모모In tones of soft repose.-----IDYLL.

모모A village Chorus is supposed to be assembled, and about tocommence its festive procession.

모모[Written for the birthday of the Duchess Louisa of Weimar.]

모모CHORUS.

모모THE festal day hail ye

모모With garlands of pleasure,

모모And dances' soft measure,With rapture commingledAnd sweet choral song.

모모DAMON.

모모Oh, how I yearn from out the crowd to flee!What joy a secret glade would give to me!Amid the throng, the turmoil here,Confined the plain, the breezes e'en appear.

모모CHORUS.

모모Now order it truly,That ev'ry one dulyMay roam and may wander,Now here, and now yonder,

모모The meadows along.

모모[The Chorus retreats gradually, and the song becomes fainter andfainter, till it dies away in the distance.]

모모DAMON.

모모In vain ye call, in vain would lure me on;True my heart speaks,--but with itself alone.

모모And if I may view

모모A blessing-fraught land,

모모The heaven's clear blue,

모모And the plain's verdant hue,

모모Alone I'll rejoice,

모모Undisturbed by man's voice.

모모And there I'll pay homage

모모To womanly merit,

모모Observe it in spirit,

모모In spirit pay homage;

모모To echo alone

모모Shall my secret be known.

모모CHORUS.

모모[Faintly mingling with Damon's song in the distance.]

모모To echo--alone--

모모Shall my secret--be known.--

모모MENALCAS.

모모My friend, why meet I here with thee?

모모Thou hast'nest not to join the festal throng?No longer stay, but come with me,

모모And mingle in the dance and song.

모모DAMON.

모모Thou'rt welcome, friend! but suffer me to roam

모모Where these old beeches hide me from man's view:Love seeks in solitude a home,

모모And homage may retreat there too.

모모MENALCAS.

모모Thou seekest here a spurious fame,

모모And hast a mind to-day to grieve me.Love as thy portion thou mayst claim

모모But homage thou must share with all, believe me!

모모When their voices thousands raise,And the dawn of morning praise,

모모Rapture bringing,

모모Blithely singing

모모On before us,Heart and ear in pleasure vie;

모모And when thousands join in chorus,

모모With the feelings brightly glowing,

모모And the wishes overflowing,Forcibly they'll bear thee high.

모모[The Chorus gradually approaches, from the distance.]

모모DAMON.

모모Distant strains are hither wending,

모모And I'm gladden'd by the throng;Yes, they're coming,--yes, descending

모모To the valley from the height,

모모MENALCAS.

모모Let us haste, our footsteps blending

모모With the rhythm of the song!Yes, they come; their course they're bending

모모Tow'rd the wood's green sward so bright.

모모CHORUS.[Gradually becoming louder.]

모모Yes, we hither come, attending

모모With the harmony of song,As the hours their race are ending

모모On this day of blest delight.

모모ALL.

모모Let none revealThe thoughts we feel,The aims we own!Let joy alone

모모Disclose the story!She'll prove it rightAnd her delight

모모Includes the glory,Includes the blissOf days like this!

모모1813.-----RINALDO.*

모모[This Cantata was written for Prince Frederick of Gotha, and setto music by Winter, the Prince singing the part of Rinaldo.--Seethe Annalen.]

모모(* See Tasso's Gerusalemme Liberata, Canto XVI.)

모모CHORUS.

모모To the strand! quick, mount the bark!

모모If no favouring zephyrs blow,

모모Ply the oar and nimbly row,And with zeal your prowess mark!

모모O'er the sea we thus career.

모모RINALDO.

모모Oh, let me linger one short moment here!'Tis heaven's decree, I may not hence away.The rugged cliffs, the wood-encircled bay,Hold me a prisoner, and my flight delay.

모모Ye were so fair, but now that dream is o'er;The charms of earth, the charms of heaven are nought.What keeps me in this spot so terror-fraught?

모모My only joy is fled for evermore.

모모Let me taste those days so sweet,

모모Heav'n-descended, once again!Heart, dear heart! ay, warmly beat!

모모Spirit true, recall those days

모모Freeborn breath thy gentle lays

모모Mingled are with joy and pain.

모모Round the beds, so richly gleaming,

모모Rises up a palace fair;All with rosy fragrance teeming,

모모As in dream thou saw'st it ne'er.

모모And this spacious garden round,

모모Far extend the galleries;Roses blossom near the ground,

모모High in air, too, bloom the trees.

모모Wat'ry flakes and jets are falling.

모모Sweet and silv'ry strains arise;While the turtle-dove is calling,

모모And the nightingale replies.

모모CHORUS.

모모Gently come! feel no alarm,

모모On a noble duty bent;Vanish'd now is ev'ry charm

모모That by magic power was lent.Friendly words and greetings calmOn his wounds will pour soft balm.

모모Fill his mind with sweet content.

모모RINALDO.

모모Hark! the turtle-dove is calling,

모모And the nightingale replies;Wat'ry flakes and jets are falling,

모모Mingling with their melodies.

모모But all of them say:

모모Her only we mean;But all fly away,

모모As soon as she's seen,--The beauteous young maiden,

모모With graces so rife,

모모Then lily and rose

모모In wreaths are entwining;

모모In dancing combining,Each zephyr that blows

모모Its brother is greeting,

모모All flying and meeting,With balsam full laden,

모모When waken'd to life.

모모CHORUS.

모모No! no longer may we wait;Rouse him from his vision straight!Show the adamantine shield!

모모RINALDO.

모모Woe! what form is here reveal'd!

모모CHORUS.

모모'Twill disclose the cheat to thee.

모모RINALDO.

모모Am I doom'd myself to seeThus degraded evermore?

모모CHORUS.

모모Courage take, and all is o'er.

모모RINALDO.

모모Be it so! I'll take fresh heart,From the spot beloved depart,Leave Armida once again,--Come then! here no more remain.

모모CHORUS.

모모Yes, 'tis well! no more remain.

모모SEMI-CHORUS.

모모Away then! let's fly

모모O'er the zephyr-kiss'd ocean!The soul-lighted eye

모모Sees armies in motion,See proud banners wave

모모O'er the dust-sprinkled course.

모모CHORUS.

모모From his forefathers brave

모모Draws the hero new force.

모모RINALDO.

모모With sorrow laden,

모모Within this valley's

모모All-silent alleysThe fairest maiden

모모Again I see.

모모Twice can this be?What! shall I hear it,And not have spiritTo ease her pains?

모모CHORUS.

모모Unworthy chains?

모모RINALDO.

모모And now I've see her,

모모Alas! how changed!With cold demeanour.

모모And looks estranged,With ghostly tread,--All hope is fled,Yes, fled for ever.The lightnings quiver,Each palace falls;The godlike halls,Each joyous hourOf spirit-power,With love's sweet dayAll fade away!

모모CHORUS.

모모Yes, fade away!

모모SEMI-CHORUS.

모모Already are heard

모모The prayers of the pious.

모모Why longer deny us?The favouring zephyr

모모Forbids all delay.

모모CHORUS.

모모Away, then! away!

모모RINALDO.

모모With heart sadly stirr'd,

모모Your command I receive;

모모Ye force me to leave.Unkind is the zephyr,--

모모Oh, wherefore not stay?

모모CHORUS.

모모Away, then! away!

모모1811.-----THE FIRST WALPURGIS-NIGHT.

모모A DRUID.

모모SWEET smiles the May!

모모The forest gay

모모From frost and ice is freed;

모모No snow is found,

모모Glad songs resound

모모Across the verdant mead.

모모Upon the height

모모The snow lies light,

모모Yet thither now we go,There to extol our Father's name,

모모Whom we for ages know.Amid the smoke shall gleam the flame;

모모Thus pure the heart will grow.

모모THE DRUIDS.

모모Amid the smoke shall gleam the flame;Extol we now our Father's name,

모모Whom we for ages know!

모모Up, up, then, let us go!

모모ONE OF THE PEOPLE.

모모Would ye, then, so rashly act?Would ye instant death attract?Know ye not the cruel threats

모모Of the victors we obey?Round about are placed their nets

모모In the sinful heathen's way.Ah! upon the lofty wall

모모Wife and children slaughter they;And we allHasten to a certain fall.

모모CHORUS OF WOMEN.

모모Ay, upon the camp's high wall

모모All our children loved they slay.

모모Ah, what cruel victors they!And we allHasten to a certain fall.

모모A DRUID.

모모Who fears to-day

모모His rites to pay,

모모Deserves his chains to wear.

모모The forest's free!

모모This wood take we,

모모And straight a pile prepare!

모모Yet in the wood

모모To stay 'tis good

모모By day, till all is still,With watchers all around us plac'd

모모Protecting you from ill.With courage fresh, then let us haste

모모Our duties to fulfil.

모모CHORUS OF WATCHERS.

모모Ye valiant watchers, now divideYour numbers through the forest wide,

모모And see that all is still,

모모While they their rites fulfil.

모모A WATCHER.

모모Let us in a cunning wise,Yon dull Christian priests surpriseWith the devil of their talk

모모We'll those very priests confound.Come with prong, and come with fork.

모모Raise a wild and rattling soundThrough the livelong night, and prowl

모모All the rocky passes round.Screechowl, owl,Join in chorus with our howl!

모모CHORUS OF WATCHERS.

모모Come with prong, and come with fork,Like the devil of their talk,And with wildly rattling sound,Prowl the desert rocks around!Screechowl, owl,Join in chorus with our howl!

모모A DRUID.

모모Thus far 'tis right.

모모That we by night

모모Our Father's praises sing;

모모Yet when 'tis day,

모모To Thee we may

모모A heart unsullied bring.

모모'Tis true that now,

모모And often, Thou

모모Fav'rest the foe in fight.As from the smoke is freed the blaze,

모모So let our faith burn bright!And if they crush our golden ways,

모모Who e'er can crush Thy light?

모모A CHRISTIAN WATCHER.

모모Comrades, quick! your aid afford!All the brood of hell's abroad;See how their enchanted forms

모모Through and through with flames are glowing!Dragon-women, men-wolf swarms,

모모On in quick succession going!Let us, let us haste to fly!

모모Wilder yet the sounds are growing,And the archfiend roars on high;From the groundHellish vapours rise around.

모모CHORUS OF CHRISTIAN WATCHERS.

모모Terrible enchanted forms,Dragon-women, men-wolf swarms!Wilder yet the sounds are growing!See, the archfiend comes, all-glowing!From the groundHellish vapours rise around!

모모CHORUS OF DRUIDS.

모모As from the smoke is freed the blaze,

모모So let our faith burn bright!And if they crush our golden ways,

모모Who e'er can crush Thy light?

모모1799.-----

모모ODES.

모모-----

모모THESE are the most singular of all the Poems of Goethe, and tomany will appear so wild and fantastic, as to leave anything buta pleasing impression. Those at the beginning, addressed to hisfriend Behrisch, were written at the age of eighteen, and most ofthe remainder were composed while he was still quite young.Despite, however, the extravagance of some of them, such as theWinter Journey over the Hartz Mountains, and the Wanderer'sStorm-Song, nothing can be finer than the noble one entitledMahomet's Song, and others, such as the Spirit Song' over theWaters, The God-like, and, above all, the magnificent sketch ofPrometheus, which forms part of an unfinished piece bearing thesame name, and called by Goethe a 'Dramatic Fragment.'

모모TO MY FRIEND.

모모[These three Odes are addressed to a certain Behrisch, who wastutor to Count Lindenau, and of whom Goethe gives an odd accountat the end of the Seventh Book of his Autobiography.]

모모FIRST ODE.

모모TRANSPLANT the beauteous tree!Gardener, it gives me pain;A happier resting-placeIts trunk deserved.

모모Yet the strength of its natureTo Earth's exhausting avarice,To Air's destructive inroads,An antidote opposed.

모모See how it in springtimeCoins its pale green leaves!Their orange-fragrancePoisons each flyblow straight.

모모The caterpillar's toothIs blunted by them;With silv'ry hues they gleamIn the bright sunshine,

모모Its twigs the maidenFain would twine inHer bridal-garland;Youths its fruit are seeking.

모모See, the autumn cometh!The caterpillarSighs to the crafty spider,--Sighs that the tree will not fade.

모모Hov'ring thitherFrom out her yew-tree dwelling,The gaudy foe advancesAgainst the kindly tree,

모모And cannot hurt it,But the more artful oneDefiles with nauseous venomIts silver leaves;

모모And sees with triumphHow the maiden shudders,The youth, how mourns he,On passing by.

모모Transplant the beauteous tree!Gardener, it gives me pain;Tree, thank the gardenerWho moves thee hence!

모모1767.-----SECOND ODE.

모모THOU go'st! I murmur--Go! let me murmur.Oh, worthy man,Fly from this land!

모모Deadly marshes,Steaming mists of OctoberHere interweave their currents,Blending for ever.

모모Noisome insectsHere are engender'd;Fatal darknessVeils their malice.

모모The fiery-tongued serpent,Hard by the sedgy bank,Stretches his pamper'd body,Caress'd by the sun's bright beams.

모모Tempt no gentle night-ramblesUnder the moon's cold twilight!Loathsome toads hold their meetingsYonder at every crossway.

모모Injuring not,Fear will they cause thee.Oh, worthy man,Fly from this land!

모모1767.-----THIRD ODE.

모모BE void of feeling!A heart that soon is stirr'd,Is a possession sadUpon this changing earth.

모모Behrisch, let spring's sweet smileNever gladden thy brow!Then winter's gloomy tempestsNever will shadow it o'er.

모모Lean thyself ne'er on a maiden'sSorrow-engendering breast.Ne'er on the arm,Misery-fraught, of a friend.

모모Already envyFrom out his rocky ambushUpon thee turnsThe force of his lynx-like eyes,

모모Stretches his talons,On thee falls,In thy shouldersCunningly plants them.

모모Strong are his skinny arms,As panther-claws;He shaketh thee,And rends thy frame.

모모Death 'tis to part,'Tis threefold deathTo part, not hopingEver to meet again.

모모Thou wouldst rejoice to leaveThis hated land behind,Wert thou not chain'd to meWith friendships flowery chains.

모모Burst them! I'll not repine.No noble friendWould stay his fellow-captive,If means of flight appear.

모모The remembranceOf his dear friend's freedomGives him freedomIn his dungeon.

모모Thou go'st,--I'm left.But e'en alreadyThe last year's winged spokesWhirl round the smoking axle.

모모I number the turnsOf the thundering wheel;The last one I bless.--Each bar then is broken, I'm free then as thou!

모모1767.-----MAHOMET'S SONG.

모모[This song was intended to be introduced in a dramatic poementitled Mahomet, the plan of which was not carried out byGoethe. He mentions that it was to have been sung by Ali towardsthe end of the piece, in honor of his master, Mahomet, shortlybefore his death, and when at the height of his glory, of whichit is typical.]

모모SEE the rock-born stream!Like the gleamOf a star so brightKindly spiritsHigh above the cloudsNourished him while youthfulIn the copse between the cliffs.

모모Young and fresh.From the clouds he dancethDown upon the marble rocks;Then tow'rd heavenLeaps exulting.

모모Through the mountain-passesChaseth he the colour'd pebbles,And, advancing like a chief,Tears his brother streamlets with himIn his course.

모모In the valley down below'Neath his footsteps spring the flowers,And the meadowIn his breath finds life.

모모Yet no shady vale can stay him,Nor can flowers,Round his knees all-softly twiningWith their loving eyes detain him;To the plain his course he taketh,Serpent-winding,

모모Social streamletsJoin his waters. And now moves heO'er the plain in silv'ry glory,And the plain in him exults,And the rivers from the plain,And the streamlets from the mountain,Shout with joy, exclaiming: "Brother,Brother, take thy brethren with thee,With thee to thine aged father,To the everlasting ocean,Who, with arms outstretching far,Waiteth for us;Ah, in vain those arms lie openTo embrace his yearning children;For the thirsty sand consumes usIn the desert waste; the sunbeamsDrink our life-blood; hills around usInto lakes would dam us! Brother,Take thy brethren of the plain,Take thy brethren of the mountainWith thee, to thy father's arms!

모모Let all come, then!--And now swells heLordlier still; yea, e'en a peopleBears his regal flood on high!And in triumph onward rolling,Names to countries gives he,--citiesSpring to light beneath his foot.

모모Ever, ever, on he rushes,Leaves the towers' flame-tipp'd summits,Marble palaces, the offspringOf his fullness, far behind.

모모Cedar-houses bears the AtlasOn his giant shoulders; flutt'ringIn the breeze far, far above himThousand flags are gaily floating,Bearing witness to his might.

모모And so beareth he his brethren,All his treasures, all his children,Wildly shouting, to the bosomOf his long-expectant sire.

모모1774.-----SPIRIT SONG OVER THE WATERS.

모모THE soul of manResembleth water:From heaven it cometh,To heaven it soareth.And then againTo earth descendeth,Changing ever.

모모Down from the loftyRocky wallStreams the bright flood,Then spreadeth gentlyIn cloudy billowsO'er the smooth rock,And welcomed kindly,Veiling, on roams it,Soft murmuring,Tow'rd the abyss.

모모Cliffs projectingOppose its progress,--Angrily foams itDown to the bottom,Step by step.

모모Now, in flat channel,Through the meadowland steals it,And in the polish'd lakeEach constellationJoyously peepeth.

모모Wind is the lovingWooer of waters;Wind blends togetherBillows all-foaming.

모모Spirit of man,Thou art like unto water!Fortune of man,Thou art like unto wind!

모모1789.*-----MY GODDESS.

모모SAY, which ImmortalMerits the highest reward?With none contend I,But I will give itTo the aye-changing,Ever-movingWondrous daughter of Jove.His best-beloved offspring.Sweet Phantasy.

모모For unto herHath he grantedAll the fancies which erstTo none allow'd heSaving himself;Now he takes his pleasureIn the mad one.

모모She may, crowned with roses,With staff twined round with lilies,Roam thro' flow'ry valleys,Rule the butterfly-people,And soft-nourishing dewWith bee-like lipsDrink from the blossom:

모모Or else she mayWith fluttering hairAnd gloomy looksSigh in the windRound rocky cliffs,And thousand-hued.Like morn and even.Ever changing,Like moonbeam's light,To mortals appear.

모모Let us all, then,Adore the Father!The old, the mighty,Who such a beauteousNe'er-fading spouseDeigns to accordTo perishing mortals!

모모To us aloneDoth he unite her,With heavenly bonds,While he commands her,in joy and sorrow,As a true spouseNever to fly us.

모모All the remainingRaces so poorOf life-teeming earth.In children so rich.Wander and feedIn vacant enjoyment,And 'mid the dark sorrowsOf evanescentRestricted life,--Bow'd by the heavyYoke of Necessity.

모모But unto us heHath his most versatile,Most cherished daughterGranted,--what joy!

모모Lovingly greet herAs a beloved one!Give her the woman'sPlace in our home!

모모And oh, may the agedStepmother WisdomHer gentle spiritNe'er seek to harm!

모모Yet know I her sister,The older, sedater,Mine own silent friend;Oh, may she never,Till life's lamp is quench'd,Turn away from me,--That noble inciter,Comforter,--Hope!

모모1781.-----WINTER JOURNEY OVER THE HARTZ MOUNTAINS.

모모[The following explanation is necessary, in order to make thisode in any way intelligible. The Poet is supposed to leave hiscompanions, who are proceeding on a hunting expedition in winter,in order himself to pay a visit to a hypochondriacal friend, andalso to see the mining in the Hartz mountains. The odealternately describes, in a very fragmentary and peculiar manner,the naturally happy disposition of the Poet himself and theunhappiness of his friend; it pictures the wildness of the roadand the dreariness of the prospect, which is relieved at one spotby the distant sight of a town, a very vague allusion to which ismade in the third strophe; it recalls the hunting party on whichhis companions have gone; and after an address to Love, concludesby a contrast between the unexplored recesses of the highest peakof the Hartz and the metalliferous veins of its smallerbrethren.]

모모LIKE the vultureWho on heavy morning cloudsWith gentle wing reposingLooks for his prey,--Hover, my song!

모모For a God hathUnto each prescribedHis destined path,Which the happy oneRuns o'er swiftlyTo his glad goal:He whose heart cruelFate hath contracted,Struggles but vainlyAgainst all the barriersThe brazen thread raises,But which the harsh shearsMust one day sever.

모모Through gloomy thicketsPresseth the wild deer on,And with the sparrowsLong have the wealthySettled themselves in the marsh.

모모Easy 'tis following the chariotThat by Fortune is driven,Like the baggage that movesOver well-mended highwaysAfter the train of a prince.

모모But who stands there apart?In the thicket, lost is his path;Behind him the bushesAre closing together,The grass springs up again,The desert engulphs him.

모모Ah, who'll heal his afflictions,To whom balsam was poison,Who, from love's fullness,Drank in misanthropy only?First despised, and now a despiser,He, in secret, wastethAll that he is worth,In a selfishness vain.If there be, on thy psaltery,Father of Love, but one toneThat to his ear may be pleasing,Oh, then, quicken his heart!Clear his cloud-enveloped eyesOver the thousand fountainsClose by the thirsty oneIn the desert.

모모Thou who createst much joy,For each a measure o'erflowing,Bless the sons of the chaseWhen on the track of the prey,With a wild thirsting for blood,Youthful and joyousAvenging late the injusticeWhich the peasant resistedVainly for years with his staff.

모모But the lonely one veilWithin thy gold clouds!Surround with winter-green,Until the roses bloom again,The humid locks,Oh Love, of thy minstrel!

모모With thy glimmering torchLightest thou himThrough the fords when 'tis night,Over bottomless placesOn desert-like plains;With the thousand colours of morningGladd'nest his bosom;With the fierce-biting stormBearest him proudly on high;Winter torrents rush from the cliffs,--Blend with his psalms;An altar of grateful delightHe finds in the much-dreaded mountain'sSnow-begirded summit,Which foreboding nationsCrown'd with spirit-dances.

모모Thou stand'st with breast inscrutable,Mysteriously disclosed,High o'er the wondering world,And look'st from cloudsUpon its realms and its majesty,Which thou from the veins of thy brethrenNear thee dost water.

모모1777.-----TO FATHER* KRONOS.

모모[written in a post-chaise.]

모모(* In the original, Schwager, which has the twofold meaning ofbrother-in-law and postilion.)

모모HASTEN thee, Kronos!On with clattering trotDownhill goeth thy path;Loathsome dizziness ever,When thou delayest, assails me.Quick, rattle along,Over stock and stone let thy trotInto life straightway lead

모모Now once moreUp the toilsome ascentHasten, panting for breath!Up, then, nor idle be,--Striving and hoping, up, up!

모모Wide, high, glorious the viewGazing round upon life,While from mount unto mountHovers the spirit eterne,Life eternal foreboding.

모모Sideways a roof's pleasant shadeAttracts thee,And a look that promises coolnessOn the maidenly threshold.There refresh thee! And, maiden,Give me this foaming draught also,Give me this health-laden look!

모모Down, now! quicker still, down!See where the sun setsEre he sets, ere old ageSeizeth me in the morass,Ere my toothless jaws mumble,And my useless limbs totter;While drunk with his farewell beamHurl me,--a fiery seaFoaming still in mine eye,--Hurl me, while dazzled and reeling,Down to the gloomy portal of hell.

모모Blow, then, gossip, thy horn,Speed on with echoing trot,So that Orcus may know we are coming;So that our host may with joyWait at the door to receive us.

모모1774.-----THE WANDERER'S STORM-SONG.

모모[Goethe says of this ode, that it is the only one remaining outof several strange hymns and dithyrambs composed by him at aperiod of great unhappiness, when the love-affair between him andFrederica had been broken off by him. He used to sing them whilewandering wildly about the country. This particular one wascaused by his being caught in a tremendous storm on one of theseoccasions. He calls it a half-crazy piece (halkunsinn), and thereader will probably agree with him.]

모모He whom thou ne'er leavest, Genius,Feels no dread within his heartAt the tempest or the rain.He whom thou ne'er leavest, Genius,Will to the rain-clouds,Will to the hailstorm,Sing in replyAs the lark sings,Oh thou on high!

모모Him whom thou ne'er leavest, Genius,Thou wilt raise above the mud-trackWith thy fiery pinions.He will wander,As, with flowery feet,Over Deucalion's dark flood,Python-slaying, light, glorious,Pythius Apollo.

모모Him whom thou ne'er leavest, Genius,Thou wilt place upon thy fleecy pinionWhen he sleepeth on the rock,--Thou wilt shelter with thy guardian wingIn the forest's midnight hour.

모모Him whom thou ne'er leavest, Genius,Thou wilt wrap up warmlyIn the snow-drift;Tow'rd the warmth approach the Muses,Tow'rd the warmth approach the Graces.

모모Ye Muses, hover round me!Ye Graces also!That is water, that is earth,And the son of water and of earthOver which I wander,Like the gods.

모모Ye are pure, like the heart of the water,Ye are pure like the marrow of earth,Hov'ring round me, while I hoverOver water, o'er the earthLike the gods.

모모Shall he, then, return,The small, the dark, the fiery peasant?Shall he, then, return, waitingOnly thy gifts, oh Father Bromius,And brightly gleaming, warmth-spreading fire?Return with joy?And I, whom ye attended,Ye Muses and ye Graces,Whom all awaits that ye,Ye Muses and ye Graces,Of circling bliss in lifeHave glorified--shall IReturn dejected?

모모Father Bromius!Thourt the Genius,Genius of ages,Thou'rt what inward glowTo Pindar was,What to the worldPhoebus Apollo.

모모Woe! Woe Inward warmth,Spirit-warmth,Central-point!Glow, and vie withPhoebus Apollo!Coldly soonHis regal lookOver thee will swiftly glide,--

모모Envy-struckLinger o'er the cedar's strength,Which, to flourish,Waits him not.

모모Why doth my lay name thee the last?Thee, from whom it began,Thee, in whom it endeth,Thee, from whom it flows,Jupiter Pluvius!Tow'rd thee streams my song.And a Castalian springRuns as a fellow-brook,Runs to the idle ones,Mortal, happy ones,Apart from thee,Who cov'rest me around,Jupiter Pluvius!

모모Not by the elm-treeHim didst thou visit,With the pair of dovesHeld in his gentle arm,--With the beauteous garland of roses,--Caressing him, so blest in his flowers,Anacreon,Storm-breathing godhead!Not in the poplar grove,Near the Sybaris' strand,Not on the mountain'sSun-illumined browDidst thou seize him,The flower-singing,Honey-breathing,Sweetly noddingTheocritus.

모모When the wheels were rattling,Wheel on wheel tow'rd the goal,High aroseThe sound of the lashOf youths with victory glowing,In the dust rolling,As from the mountain fallShowers of stones in the vale--Then thy soul was brightly glowing, Pindar--Glowing? Poor heart!

모모There, on the hill,--Heavenly might!But enough glowThither to wend,Where is my cot!

모모1771.-----THE SEA-VOYAGE.

모모MANY a day and night my bark stood ready laden;Waiting fav'ring winds, I sat with true friends round me,Pledging me to patience and to courage,In the haven.

모모And they spoke thus with impatience twofold:"Gladly pray we for thy rapid passage,Gladly for thy happy voyage; fortuneIn the distant world is waiting for thee,In our arms thoult find thy prize, and love too,When returning."

모모And when morning came, arose an uproar,And the sailors' joyous shouts awoke us;All was stirring, all was living, moving,Bent on sailing with the first kind zephyr.

모모And the sails soon in the breeze are swelling,And the sun with fiery love invites us;Fill'd the sails are, clouds on high are floating,On the shore each friend exulting raisesSongs of hope, in giddy joy expectingJoy the voyage through, as on the morn of sailing,And the earliest starry nights so radiant.

모모But by God-sent changing winds ere long he's drivenSideways from the course he had intended,And he feigns as though he would surrender,While he gently striveth to outwit them,

모모To his goal, e'en when thus press'd, still faithful.But from out the damp grey distance rising,Softly now the storm proclaims its advent,Presseth down each bird upon the waters,Presseth down the throbbing hearts of mortals.And it cometh. At its stubborn fury,Wisely ev'ry sail the seaman striketh;With the anguish-laden ball are sportingWind and water.

모모And on yonder shore are gather'd standing,Friends and lovers, trembling for the bold one:"Why, alas, remain'd he here not with us!Ah, the tempest! Cast away by fortune!Must the good one perish in this fashion?Might not he perchance.... Ye great immortals!"

모모Yet he, like a man, stands by his rudder;With the bark are sporting wind and water,Wind and water sport not with his bosom:On the fierce deep looks he, as a master,--In his gods, or shipwreck'd, or safe landed,Trusting ever.

모모1776.-----THE EAGLE AND DOVE.

모모IN search of prey once raised his pinionsAn eaglet;A huntsman's arrow came, and reftHis right wing of all motive power.Headlong he fell into a myrtle grove,For three long days on anguish fed,In torment writhedThroughout three long, three weary nights;And then was cured,Thanks to all-healing Nature'sSoft, omnipresent balm.He crept away from out the copse,And stretch'd his wing--alas!Lost is all power of flight--He scarce can lift himselfFrom off the groundTo catch some mean, unworthy prey,And rests, deep-sorrowing,On the low rock beside the stream.Up to the oak he looks,Looks up to heaven,While in his noble eye there gleams a tear.Then, rustling through the myrtle boughs, behold,There comes a wanton pair of doves,Who settle down, and, nodding, strutO'er the gold sands beside the stream,And gradually approach;Their red-tinged eyes, so full of love,Soon see the inward-sorrowing one.The male, inquisitively social, leapsOn the next bush, and looksUpon him kindly and complacently."Thou sorrowest," murmurs he:"Be of good cheer, my friend!All that is needed for calm happinessHast thou not here?Hast thou not pleasure in the golden boughThat shields thee from the day's fierce glow?Canst thou not raise thy breast to catch,On the soft moss beside the brook,The sun's last rays at even?Here thou mayst wander through the flowers' fresh dew,Pluck from the overflowThe forest-trees provide,Thy choicest food,--mayst quenchThy light thirst at the silvery spring.Oh friend, true happinessLies in contentedness,And that contentednessFinds everywhere enough.""Oh, wise one!" said the eagle, while he sankIn deep and ever deep'ning thought--"Oh Wisdom! like a dove thou speakest!"

모모1774.*-----PROMETHEUS.

모모COVER thy spacious heavens, Zeus,With clouds of mist,And, like the boy who lopsThe thistles' heads,Disport with oaks and mountain-peaks,Yet thou must leaveMy earth still standing;My cottage too, which was not raised by thee;Leave me my hearth,Whose kindly glowBy thee is envied.

모모I know nought poorerUnder the sun, than ye gods!Ye nourish painfully,With sacrificesAnd votive prayers,Your majesty:Ye would e'en starve,If children and beggarsWere not trusting fools.

모모While yet a childAnd ignorant of life,I turned my wandering gazeUp tow'rd the sun, as if with himThere were an ear to hear my wailings,A heart, like mine,To feel compassion for distress.

모모Who help'd meAgainst the Titans' insolence?Who rescued me from certain death,From slavery?Didst thou not do all this thyself,My sacred glowing heart?And glowedst, young and good,Deceived with grateful thanksTo yonder slumbering one?

모모I honour thee! and why?Hast thou e'er lighten'd the sorrowsOf the heavy laden?Hast thou e'er dried up the tearsOf the anguish-stricken?Was I not fashion'd to be a manBy omnipotent Time,And by eternal Fate,Masters of me and thee?

모모Didst thou e'er fancyThat life I should learn to hate,And fly to deserts,Because not allMy blossoming dreams grew ripe?

모모Here sit I, forming mortalsAfter my image;A race resembling me,To suffer, to weep,To enjoy, to be glad,And thee to scorn,As I!

모모1773.-----GANYMEDE.

모모How, in the light of morning,Round me thou glowest,Spring, thou beloved one!With thousand-varying loving blissThe sacred emotionsBorn of thy warmth eternalPress 'gainst my bosom,Thou endlessly fair one!Could I but hold thee clasp'dWithin mine arms!

모모Ah! upon thy bosomLay I, pining,And then thy flowers, thy grass,Were pressing against my heart.Thou coolest the burningThirst of my bosom,Beauteous morning breeze!The nightingale then calls meSweetly from out of the misty vale.I come, I come!Whither? Ah, whither?

모모Up, up, lies my course.While downward the cloudsAre hovering, the cloudsAre bending to meet yearning love.For me,Within thine armsUpwards!Embraced and embracing!Upwards into thy bosom,Oh Father all-loving!

모모1789.*-----THE BOUNDARIES OF HUMANITY.

모모WHEN the primevalAll-holy FatherSows with a tranquil handFrom clouds, as they roll,Bliss-spreading lightningsOver the earth,Then do I kiss the lastHem of his garment,While by a childlike aweFiil'd is my breast.

모모For with immortalsNe'er may a mortalMeasure himself.If he soar upwardsAnd if he touchWith his forehead the stars,Nowhere will rest thenHis insecure feet,And with him sportTempest and cloud.

모모Though with firm sinewyLimbs he may standOn the enduringWell-grounded earth,All he is everAble to do,Is to resembleThe oak or the vine.

모모Wherein do godsDiffer from mortals?In that the formerSee endless billowsHeaving before them;Us doth the billowLift up and swallow,So that we perish.

모모Small is the ringEnclosing our life,And whole generationsLink themselves firmlyOn to existence'sChain never-ending.

모모1789. *-----THE GODLIKE.

모모NOBLE be man,Helpful and good!For that aloneDistinguisheth himFrom all the beingsUnto us known.

모모Hail to the beings,Unknown and glorious,Whom we forebode!From his exampleLearn we to know them!

모모For unfeelingNature is ever:On bad and on goodThe sun alike shineth;And on the wicked,As on the best,The moon and stars gleam.

모모Tempest and torrent,Thunder and hail,Roar on their path,Seizing the while,As they haste onward,One after another.

모모Even so, fortuneGropes 'mid the throng--Innocent boyhood'sCurly head seizing,--Seizing the hoaryHead of the sinner.

모모After laws mighty,Brazen, eternal,Must all we mortalsFinish the circuitOf our existence.

모모Man, and man onlyCan do the impossible;He 'tis distinguisheth,Chooseth and judgeth;He to the momentEndurance can lend.

모모He and he onlyThe good can reward,The bad can he punish,Can heal and can save;All that wanders and straysCan usefully blend.And we pay homageTo the immortalsAs though they were men,And did in the great,What the best, in the small,Does or might do.

모모Be the man that is noble,Both helpful and good.Unweariedly formingThe right and the useful,A type of those beingsOur mind hath foreshadow'd!

모모1782.-----

모모MISCELLANEOUS POEMS.

모모-----in the wares before you spread,Types of all things may be read.-----THE GERMAN PARNASSUS.

모모'NEATH the shadow

모모Of these bushes,On the meadow

모모Where the cooling water gushes.Phoebus gave me, when a boy,All life's fullness to enjoy.So, in silence, as the GodBade them with his sov'reign nod,Sacred Muses train'd my daysTo his praise.--With the bright and silv'ry floodOf Parnassus stirr'd my blood,And the seal so pure and chasteBy them on my lips was placed.

모모With her modest pinions, see,Philomel encircles me!In these bushes, in yon grove,

모모Calls she to her sister-throng,

모모And their heavenly choral songTeaches me to dream of love.

모모Fullness waxes in my breastOf emotions social, blest;Friendship's nurtured칿ove awakes,--And the silence Phoebus breaksOf his mountains, of his vales,Sweetly blow the balmy gales;All for whom he shows affection,Who are worthy his protection,Gladly follow his direction.

모모This one comes with joyous bearing

모모And with open, radiant gaze;That a sterner look is wearing,This one, scarcely cured, with daring

모모Wakes the strength of former days;For the sweet, destructive flamePierced his marrow and his frame.That which Amor stole beforePhoebus only can restore,Peace, and joy, and harmony,Aspirations pure and free.

모모Brethren, rise ye!Numbers prize ye!Deeds of worth resemble they.

모모Who can better than the bardGuide a friend when gone astray?

모모If his duty he regard,More he'll do, than others may.

모모Yes! afar I hear them sing!Yes! I hear them touch the string,And with mighty godlike stroke

모모Right and duty they inspire,And evoke,

모모As they sing, and wake the lyre,Tendencies of noblest worth,To each type of strength give birth.

모모Phantasies of sweetest powerFlowerRound about on ev'ry bough,Bending nowLike the magic wood of old,'Neath the fruit that gleams like gold.

모모What we feel and what we view

모모In the land of highest bliss,--

모모This dear soil, a sun like this,--Lures the best of women too.And the Muses' breathings blestRouse the maiden's gentle breast,Tune the throat to minstrelsy,And with cheeks of beauteous dye,Bid it sing a worthy song,Sit the sister-band among;And their strains grow softer still,As they vie with earnest will.

모모One amongst the band betimes

모모Goes to wanderBy the beeches, 'neath the limes,

모모Yonder seeking, finding yonderThat which in the morning-groveShe had lost through roguish Love,All her breast's first aspirations,And her heart's calm meditations,To the shady wood so fair

모모Gently stealing,Takes she that which man can ne'er

모모Duly merit,--each soft feeling,--Disregards the noontide rayAnd the dew at close of day,?

모모In the plain her path she loses.Ne'er disturb her on her way!

모모Seek her silently, ye Muses

모모Shouts I hear, wherein the soundOf the waterfall is drown'd.From the grove loud clamours rise,Strange the tumult, strange the cries.See I rightly? Can it be?To the very sanctuary,Lo, an impious troop in-hies!

모모O'er the landStreams the band;Hot desire,Drunken-fireIn their gazeWildly plays,--Makes their hairBristle there.And the troop,With fell swoop,Women, men,Coming then,Ply their blowsAnd expose,Void of shame,All the frame.Iron shot,Fierce and hot,Strike with fearOn the ear;All they slayOn their way.O'er the landPours the band;All take flightAt their sight.

모모Ah, o'er ev'ry plant they rush!Ah, their cruel footsteps crushAll the flowers that fill their path!Who will dare to stem their wrath?

모모Brethren, let us venture all!

모모Virtue in your pure cheek glows.Phoebus will attend our call

모모When he sees our heavy woes;And that we may have arightWeapons suited to the fight,He the mountain shaketh now--From its browRattling downStone on stoneThrough the thicket spread appear.Brethren, seize them! Wherefore fear?Now the villain crew assail,As though with a storm of hail,And expel the strangers wildFrom these regions soft and mildWhere the sun has ever smil'd!

모모What strange wonder do I see?Can it be?All my limbs of power are reft.And all strength my hand has left.Can it he?None are strangers that I see!And our brethren 'tis who goOn before, the way to show!Oh, the reckless impious ones!How they, with their jarring tones,Beat the time, as on they hie!Quick, my brethren!--let us fly!

모모To the rash ones, yet a word!Ay, my voice shall now be heard,As a peal of thunder, strong!

모모Words as poets' arms were made,--

모모When the god will he obey'd,Follow fast his darts ere long.

모모Was it possible that yeThus your godlike dignityShould forget? The Thyrsus rude

모모Must a heavy burden feel

모모To the hand but wont to stealO'er the lyre in gentle mood.From the sparkling waterfalls,From the brook that purling calls,Shall Silenus' loathsome beastBe allow'd at will to feast?Aganippe's * wave he sipsWith profane and spreading lips,--With ungainly feet stamps madly,Till the waters flow on sadly.

모모Fain I'd think myself deluded

모모In the sadd'ning sounds I hear;From the holy glades secluded

모모Hateful tones assail the ear.Laughter wild (exchange how mournful!)

모모Takes the place of love's sweet dream;Women-haters and the scornful

모모In exulting chorus scream.Nightingale and turtle dove

모모Fly their nests so warm and chaste,And, inflamed with sensual love,

모모Holds the Faun the Nymph embrac'd.Here a garment's torn away,

모모Scoffs succeed their sated bliss,While the god, with angry ray,

모모Looks upon each impious kiss.

모모Vapour, smoke, as from a fire,

모모And advancing clouds I view;Chords not only grace the lyre,

모모For the bow its chords bath too.Even the adorer's heart

모모Dreads the wild advancing hand,For the flames that round them dart

모모Show the fierce destroyer's hand.

모모Oh neglect not what I say,

모모For I speak it lovingly!From our boundaries haste away,

모모From the god's dread anger fly!Cleanse once more the holy place,

모모Turn the savage train aside!Earth contains upon its face

모모Many a spot unsanctified;Here we only prize the good.

모모Stars unsullied round us burn.

모모If ye, in repentant mood,

모모From your wanderings would return,--If ye fail to find the bliss

모모That ye found with us of yore,--Or when lawless mirth like this

모모Gives your hearts delight no more,--Then return in pilgrim guise,

모모Gladly up the mountain go,While your strains repentant rise,

모모And our brethren's advent show.

모모Let a new-born wreath entwine

모모Solemnly your temples round;Rapture glows in hearts divine

모모When a long-lost sinner's found.Swifter e'en than Lathe's flood

모모Round Death's silent house can play,Ev'ry error of the good

모모Will love's chalice wash away.All will haste your steps to meet,

모모As ye come in majesty,--Men your blessing will entreat;--

모모Ours ye thus will doubly be!

모모1798.(* Aganippe--A spring in Boeotia, which arose out of MountHelicon, and was sacred to Apollo and the Muses.)-----LILY'S MENAGERIE.

모모[Goethe describes this much-admired Poem, which he wrote inhonour of his love Lily, as being "designed to change hissurrender of her into despair, by drolly-fretful images."]

모모THERE'S no menagerie, I vow,

모모Excels my Lily's at this minute;

모모She keeps the strangest creatures in it,And catches them, she knows not how.

모모Oh, how they hop, and run, and rave,And their clipp'd pinions wildly wave,--Poor princes, who must all endureThe pangs of love that nought can cure.

모모What is the fairy's name?--Is't Lily?--Ask not me!Give thanks to Heaven if she's unknown to thee.

모모Oh what a cackling, what a shrieking,

모모When near the door she takes her stand,

모모With her food-basket in her hand!Oh what a croaking, what a squeaking!Alive all the trees and the bushes appear,While to her feet whole troops draw near;The very fish within, the water clearSplash with impatience and their heads protrude;And then she throws around the foodWith such a look!--the very gods delighting(To say nought of beasts). There begins, then, a biting,A picking, a pecking, a sipping,And each o'er the legs of another is tripping,And pushing, and pressing, and flapping,And chasing, and fuming, and snapping,And all for one small piece of bread,To which, though dry, her fair hands give a taste,As though it in ambrosia had been plac'd.

모모And then her look! the tone

모모With which she calls: Pipi! Pipi!Would draw Jove's eagle from his throne;Yes, Venus' turtle doves, I wean,And the vain peacock e'en,Would come, I swear,Soon as that tone had reach'd them through the air.

모모E'en from a forest dark had she

모모Enticed a bear, unlick'd, ill-bred,

모모And, by her wiles alluring, ledTo join the gentle company,Until as tame as they was he:(Up to a certain point, be't understood!)How fair, and, ah, how goodShe seem'd to be! I would have drain'd my bloodTo water e'en her flow'rets sweet.

모모"Thou sayest: I! Who? How? And where?"--Well, to be plain, good Sirs--I am the bear;

모모In a net-apron, caught, alas!

모모Chain'd by a silk-thread at her feet.

모모But how this wonder came to passI'll tell some day, if ye are curious;Just now, my temper's much too furious.

모모Ah, when I'm in the corner plac'd,

모모And hear afar the creatures snapping,

모모And see the flipping and the flapping,

모모I turn around

모모With growling sound,

모모And backward run a step in haste,

모모And look around

모모With growling sound.

모모Then run again a step in haste,And to my former post go round.

모모But suddenly my anger grows,A mighty spirit fills my nose,My inward feelings all revolt.A creature such as thou! a dolt!Pipi, a squirrel able nuts to crack!I bristle up my shaggy backUnused a slave to be.I'm laughed at by each trim and upstart treeTo scorn. The bowling-green I fly,

모모With neatly-mown and well-kept grass:

모모The box makes faces as I pass,--Into the darkest thicket hasten I,Hoping to 'scape from the ring,Over the palings to spring!Vainly I leap and climb;

모모I feel a leaden spell.

모모That pinions me as well,And when I'm fully wearied out in time,I lay me down beside some mock-cascade,

모모And roll myself half dead, and foam, and cry,

모모And, ah! no Oreads hear my sigh,Excepting those of china made!

모모But, ah, with sudden power

모모In all my members blissful feelings reign!'Tis she who singeth yonder in her bower!

모모I hear that darling, darling voice again.The air is warm, and teems with fragrance clear,Sings she perchance for me alone to hear?

모모I haste, and trample down the shrubs amain;The trees make way, the bushes all retreat,And so--the beast is lying at her feet.

모모She looks at him: "The monster's droll enough!

모모He's, for a bear, too mild,

모모Yet, for a dog, too wild,So shaggy, clumsy, rough!"Upon his back she gently strokes her foot;

모모He thinks himself in Paradise.What feelings through his seven senses shoot!

모모But she looks on with careless eyes.I lick her soles, and kiss her shoes,

모모As gently as a bear well may;Softly I rise, and with a clever ruse

모모Leap on her knee.--On a propitious dayShe suffers it; my ears then tickles she,

모모And hits me a hard blow in wanton play;I growl with new-born ecstasy;Then speaks she in a sweet vain jest, I wot"Allons lout doux! eh! la menotte!Et faites serviteurComme un joli seigneur."Thus she proceeds with sport and glee;

모모Hope fills the oft-deluded beast;Yet if one moment he would lazy be,

모모Her fondness all at once hath ceas'd.

모모She doth a flask of balsam-fire possess,

모모Sweeter than honey bees can make,

모모One drop of which she'll on her finger take,When soften'd by his love and faithfulness,

모모Wherewith her monster's raging thirst to slake;Then leaves me to myself, and flies at last,And I, unbound, yet prison'd fastBy magic, follow in her train,Seek for her, tremble, fly again.The hapless creature thus tormenteth she,

모모Regardless of his pleasure or his woe;Ha! oft half-open'd does she leave the door for me,

모모And sideways looks to learn if I will fly or no.And I--Oh gods! your hands aloneCan end the spell that's o'er me thrown;Free me, and gratitude my heart will fill;

모모And yet from heaven ye send me down no aid--

모모Not quite in vain doth life my limbs pervade:I feel it! Strength is left me still.

모모1775.-----TO CHARLOTTE.

모모'MIDST the noise of merriment and glee,

모모'Midst full many a sorrow, many a care,Charlotte, I remember, we remember thee,

모모How, at evening's hour so fair,Thou a kindly hand didst reach us,

모모When thou, in some happy place

모모Where more fair is Nature s face,

모모Many a lightly-hidden traceOf a spirit loved didst teach us.

모모Well 'tis that thy worth I rightly knew,--

모모That I, in the hour when first we met,

모모While the first impression fill'd me yet,Call'd thee then a girl both good and true.

모모Rear'd in silence, calmly, knowing nought,

모모On the world we suddenly are thrown;Hundred thousand billows round us sport;

모모All things charm us--many please alone,Many grieve us, and as hour on hour is stealing,

모모To and fro our restless natures sway;First we feel, and then we find each feeling

모모By the changeful world-stream borne away.

모모Well I know, we oft within us find

모모Many a hope and many a smart.Charlotte, who can know our mind?

모모Charlotte, who can know our heart?Ah! 'twould fain be understood, 'twould fain o'erflow

모모In some creature's fellow-feelings blest,And, with trust, in twofold measure know

모모All the grief and joy in Nature's breast.

모모Then thine eye is oft around thee cast,

모모But in vain, for all seems closed for ever.Thus the fairest part of life is madly pass'd

모모Free from storm, but resting never:To thy sorrow thou'rt to-day repell'd

모모By what yesterday obey'd thee.Can that world by thee be worthy held

모모Which so oft betray'd thee?

모모Which, 'mid all thy pleasures and thy pains,

모모Lived in selfish, unconcern'd repose?See, the soul its secret cells regains,

모모And the heart--makes haste to close.Thus found I thee, and gladly went to meet thee;

모모"She's worthy of all love!" I cried,And pray'd that Heaven with purest bliss might greet thee,

모모Which in thy friend it richly hath supplied.

모모1776.*-----LOVE'S DISTRESSES.

모모WHO will hear me? Whom shall I lament to?Who would pity me that heard my sorrows?Ah, the lip that erst so many rapturesUsed to taste, and used to give responsive,Now is cloven, and it pains me sorely;And it is not thus severely woundedBy my mistress having caught me fiercely,And then gently bitten me, intendingTo secure her friend more firmly to her:No, my tender lip is crack'd thus, onlyBy the winds, o'er rime and frost proceeding,Pointed, sharp, unloving, having met me.Now the noble grape's bright juice commingledWith the bee's sweet juice, upon the fireOf my hearth, shall ease me of my torment.Ah, what use will all this be, if with itLove adds not a drop of his own balsam?

모모1789.*-----THE MUSAGETES.

모모IN the deepest nights of WinterTo the Muses kind oft cried I:"Not a ray of morn is gleaming,Not a sign of daylight breaking;Bring, then, at the fitting moment,Bring the lamp's soft glimm'ring lustre,'Stead of Phoebus and Aurora,To enliven my still labours!"Yet they left me in my slumbers,Dull and unrefreshing, lying,And to each late-waken'd morningFollow'd days devoid of profit.

모모When at length return'd the spring-time,To the nightingales thus spake I:"Darling nightingales, oh, beat yeEarly, early at my window,--Wake me from the heavy slumberThat chains down the youth so strongly!"Yet the love-o'erflowing songstersTheir sweet melodies protractedThrough the night before my window,Kept awake my loving spirit,Rousing new and tender yearningsIn my newly-waken'd bosom.And the night thus fleeted o'er me,And Aurora found me sleeping,--Ay, the sun could scarce arouse me.

모모Now at length is come the Summer,And the early fly so busyDraws me from my pleasing slumbersAt the first-born morning-glimmer.Mercilessly then returns she,Though the half-aroused one oftenScares her from him with impatience,And she lures her shameless sisters,So that from my weary eyelidsKindly sleep ere long is driven.From my couch then boldly spring I,And I seek the darling Muses,in the beechen-grove I find them,Full of pieasure to receive me;And to the tormenting insectsOwe I many a golden hour.Thus be ye, unwelcome beings,Highly valued by the poet,As the flies my numbers tell of.

모모1798.-----MORNING LAMENT.

모모OH thou cruel deadly-lovely maiden,Tell me what great sin have I committed,That thou keep'st me to the rack thus fasten'd,That thou hast thy solemn promise broken?

모모'Twas but yestere'en that thou with fondnessPress'd my hand, and these sweet accents murmured:"Yes, I'll come, I'll come when morn approacheth,Come, my friend, full surely to thy chamber."

모모On the latch I left my doors, unfasten'd,Having first with care tried all the hinges,And rejoic'd right well to find they creak'd not.

모모What a night of expectation pass'd I!For I watch'd, and ev'ry chime I number'd;If perchance I slept a few short moments,Still my heart remain'd awake forever,And awoke me from my gentle slumbers.

모모Yes, then bless'd I night's o'erhanging darkness,That so calmly cover'd all things round me;I enjoy'd the universal silence,While I listen'd ever in the silence,If perchance the slightest sounds were stirring.

모모"Had she only thoughts, my thoughts resembling,Had she only feelings, like my feelings,She would not await the dawn of morning.But, ere this, would surely have been with me."

모모Skipp'd a kitten on the floor above me,Scratch'd a mouse a panel in the corner,Was there in the house the slightest motion,Ever hoped I that I heard thy footstep,Ever thought I that I heard thee coming.And so lay I long, and ever longer,And already was the daylight dawning,And both here and there were signs of movement.

모모"Is it yon door? Were it my door only!"In my bed I lean'd upon my elbow,Looking tow'rd the door, now half-apparent,If perchance it might not be in motion.Both the wings upon the latch continued,On the quiet hinges calmly hanging.

모모And the day grew bright and brighter ever;And I heard my neighbour's door unbolted,As he went to earn his daily wages,And ere long I heard the waggons rumbling,And the city gates were also open'd,While the market-place, in ev'ry corner,Teem'd with life and bustle and confusion.

모모In the house was going now and comingUp and down the stairs, and doors were creakingBackwards now, now forwards,--footsteps clatter'dYet, as though it were a thing all-living,From my cherish'd hope I could not tear me.

모모When at length the sun, in hated splendour.Fell upon my walls, upon my windows,Up I sprang, and hasten'd to the garden,There to blend my breath, so hot and yearning,With the cool refreshing morning breezes,And, it might be, even there to meet thee:But I cannot find thee in the arbour,Or the avenue of lofty lindens.

모모1789.*-----THE VISIT.

모모FAIN had I to-day surprised my mistress,But soon found I that her door was fasten'd.Yet I had the key safe in my pocket,And the darling door I open'd softly!In the parlour found I not the maiden,Found the maiden not within her closet,Then her chamber-door I gently open'd,When I found her wrapp'd in pleasing slumbers,Fully dress'd, and lying on the sofa.

모모While at work had slumber stolen o'er her;For her knitting and her needle found IResting in her folded bands so tender;And I placed myself beside her softly,And held counsel, whether I should wake her.

모모Then I looked upon the beauteous quietThat on her sweet eyelids was reposingOn her lips was silent truth depicted,On her cheeks had loveliness its dwelling,And the pureness of a heart unsulliedIn her bosom evermore was heaving.All her limbs were gracefully reclining,Set at rest by sweet and godlike balsam.Gladly sat I, and the contemplationHeld the strong desire I felt to wake herFirmer and firmer down, with mystic fetters.

모모"Oh, thou love," methought, "I see that slumber,Slumber that betrayeth each false feature,Cannot injure thee, can nought discoverThat could serve to harm thy friend's soft feelings.

모모"Now thy beauteous eyes are firmly closed,That, when open, form mine only rapture.And thy sweet lips are devoid of motion,Motionless for speaking or for kissing;Loosen'd are the soft and magic fettersOf thine arms, so wont to twine around me,And the hand, the ravishing companionOf thy sweet caresses, lies unmoving.Were my thoughts of thee but based on error,Were the love I bear thee self-deception,I must now have found it out, since AmorIs, without his bandage, placed beside me."

모모Long I sat thus, full of heartfelt pleasureAt my love, and at her matchless merit;She had so delighted me while slumbering,That I could not venture to awake her.

모모Then I on the little table near herSoftly placed two oranges, two roses;Gently, gently stole I from her chamber.When her eyes the darling one shall open,She will straightway spy these colourd presents,And the friendly gift will view with wonder,For the door will still remain unopen'd.

모모If perchance I see to-night the angel,How will she rejoice,--reward me doublyFor this sacrifice of fond affection!

모모1765.-----THE MAGIC NET.

모모Do I see a contest yonder?See I miracles or pastimes?Beauteous urchins, five in number,'Gainst five sisters fair contending,--Measured is the time they're beating--At a bright enchantress' bidding.Glitt'ring spears by some are wielded,Threads are others nimbly twining,

모모So that in their snares, the weaponsOne would think, must needs be captured,Soon, in truth, the spears are prison'd;Yet they, in the gentle war-dance,One by one escape their fettersIn the row of loops so tender,That make haste to seize a free oneSoon as they release a captive.

모모So with contests, strivings, triumphs,Flying now, and now returning,Is an artful net soon woven,In its whiteness like the snow-flakes,That, from light amid the darkness,Draw their streaky lines so varied,As e'en colours scarce can draw them.

모모Who shall now receive that garmentFar beyond all others wish'd-for?Whom our much-loved mistress favourAs her own acknowledged servant?I am blest by kindly Fortune'sTokens true, in silence pray'd for!And I feel myself held captive,To her service now devoted.

모모Yet, e'en while I, thus enraptured,Thus adorn'd, am proudly wand'ring,See! yon wantons are entwining,Void of strife, with secret ardour,Other nets, each fine and finer,Threads of twilight interweaving,Moonbeams sweet, night-violets' balsam.

모모Ere the net is noticed by us,Is a happier one imprison'd,Whom we, one and all, togetherGreet with envy and with blessings.

모모1803.-----THE GOBLET.

모모ONCE I held a well-carved brimming goblet,--In my two hands tightly clasp'd I held it,Eagerly the sweet wine sipp'd I from it,Seeking there to drown all care and sorrow.

모모Amor enter'd in, and found me sitting,And he gently smiled in modest fashion,Smiled as though the foolish one he pitied.

모모"Friend, I know a far more beauteous vessel,One wherein to sink thy spirit wholly;Say, what wilt thou give me, if I grant it,And with other nectar fill it for thee?"

모모Oh, how kindly hath he kept his promise!For to me, who long had yearn'd, he grantedThee, my Lida, fill'd with soft affection.

모모When I clasp mine arms around thee fondly,When I drink in love's long-hoarded balsamFrom thy darling lips so true, so faithful,Fill'd with bliss thus speak I to my spirit"No! a vessel such as this, save AmorNever god hath fashion'd or been lord of!Such a form was ne'er produced by VulcanWith his cunning, reason-gifted hammers!On the leaf-crown'd mountains may LyaeusBid his Fauns, the oldest and the wisest,Pass the choicest clusters through the winepress,And himself watch o'er the fermentation:Such a draught no toil can e'er procure him!"

모모1781.-----TO THE GRASSHOPPER.

모모AFTER ANACREON.

모모[The strong resemblance of this fine poem to Cowley's Ode bearingthe same name, and beginning "Happy insect! what can be," will beat once seen.]

모모HAPPY art thou, darling insect,Who, upon the trees' tall branches,By a modest draught inspired,Singing, like a monarch livest!Thou possessest as thy portionAll that on the plains thou seest,All that by the hours is brought thee'Mongst the husbandmen thou livest,As a friend, uninjured by them,Thou whom mortals love to honour,Herald sweet of sweet Spring's advent!Yes, thou'rt loved by all the Muses,

모모Phoebus' self, too, needs must love thee;They their silver voices gave thee,Age can never steal upon thee.Wise and gentle friend of poets,Born a creature fleshless, bloodless,Though Earth's daughter, free from suff'ring,To the gods e'en almost equal.

모모1781.-----FROM 'THE SORROWS OF YOUNG WERTHER.'
