모모[Prefixed to the second edition.]

모모EV'RY youth for love's sweet portion sighs,

모모Ev'ry maiden sighs to win man's love;Why, alas! should bitter pain arise

모모From the noblest passion that we prove?

모모Thou, kind soul, bewailest, lov'st him well,

모모From disgrace his memory's saved by thee;Lo, his spirit signs from out its cell:

모모BE A MAN, NOR SEEK TO FOLLOW ME.

모모1775.-----TRILOGY OF PASSION.

모모I. TO WERTHER.

모모[This poem, written at the age of seventy-five, was appended toan edition of 'Werther,' published at that time.]

모모ONCE more, then, much-wept shadow, thou dost dare

모모Boldly to face the day's clear light,To meet me on fresh blooming meadows fair,

모모And dost not tremble at my sight.Those happy times appear return'd once more.

모모When on one field we quaff'd refreshing dew,And, when the day's unwelcome toils were o'er,

모모The farewell sunbeams bless'd our ravish'd view;Fate bade thee go,--to linger here was mine,--Going the first, the smaller loss was thine.

모모The life of man appears a glorious fate:The day how lovely, and the night how great!And we 'mid Paradise-like raptures plac'd,The sun's bright glory scarce have learn'd to taste.

모모When strange contending feelings dimly cover,Now us, and now the forms that round us hover;One's feelings by no other are supplied,'Tis dark without, if all is bright inside;An outward brightness veils my sadden'd mood,When Fortune smiles,--how seldom understood!Now think we that we know her, and with mightA woman's beauteous form instils delight;The youth, as glad as in his infancy,The spring-time treads, as though the spring were heRavish'd, amazed, he asks, how this is done?He looks around, the world appears his own.With careless speed he wanders on through space,Nor walls, nor palaces can check his race;As some gay flight of birds round tree-tops plays,So 'tis with him who round his mistress strays;He seeks from AEther, which he'd leave behind him,The faithful look that fondly serves to bind him.

모모Yet first too early warn'd, and then too late,He feels his flight restrain'd, is captur'd straightTo meet again is sweet, to part is sad,Again to meet again is still more glad,And years in one short moment are enshrin'd;But, oh, the harsh farewell is hid behind!

모모Thou smilest, friend, with fitting thoughts inspired;By a dread parting was thy fame acquired,Thy mournful destiny we sorrow'd o'er,For weal and woe thou left'st us evermore,And then again the passions' wavering forceDrew us along in labyrinthine course;And we, consumed by constant misery,At length must part--and parting is to die!How moving is it, when the minstrel sings,To 'scape the death that separation brings!Oh grant, some god, to one who suffers so,To tell, half-guilty, his sad tale of woe

모모1824

모모II. ELEGY.

모모When man had ceased to utter his lament,

모모A god then let me tell my tale of sorrow.

모모WHAT hope of once more meeting is there nowIn the still-closed blossoms of this day?Both heaven and hell thrown open seest thou;What wav'ring thoughts within the bosom playNo longer doubt! Descending from the sky,She lifts thee in her arms to realms on high.

모모And thus thou into Paradise wert brought,

모모As worthy of a pure and endless life;Nothing was left, no wish, no hope, no thought,

모모Here was the boundary of thine inmost strife:And seeing one so fair, so glorified,The fount of yearning tears was straightway dried.

모모No motion stirr'd the day's revolving wheel,

모모In their own front the minutes seem'd to go;The evening kiss, a true and binding seal,

모모Ne'er changing till the morrow's sunlight glow.The hours resembled sisters as they went.Yet each one from another different.

모모The last hour's kiss, so sadly sweet, effac'd

모모A beauteous network of entwining love.Now on the threshold pause the feet, now haste.

모모As though a flaming cherub bade them move;The unwilling eye the dark road wanders o'er,Backward it looks, but closed it sees the door.

모모And now within itself is closed this breast,

모모As though it ne'er were open, and as though,Vying with ev'ry star, no moments blest

모모Had, in its presence, felt a kindling glow;Sadness, reproach, repentance, weight of care,Hang heavy on it in the sultry air.

모모Is not the world still left? The rocky steeps,

모모Are they with holy shades no longer crown'd?Grows not the harvest ripe? No longer creeps

모모The espalier by the stream,--the copse around?Doth not the wondrous arch of heaven still rise,Now rich in shape, now shapeless to the eyes?

모모As, seraph-like, from out the dark clouds' chorus,

모모With softness woven, graceful, light, and fair,Resembling Her, in the blue aether o'er us,

모모A slender figure hovers in the air,--Thus didst thou see her joyously advance,The fairest of the fairest in the dance.

모모Yet but a moment dost thou boldly dare

모모To clasp an airy form instead of hers;Back to thine heart! thou'lt find it better there,

모모For there in changeful guise her image stirsWhat erst was one, to many turneth fast,In thousand forms, each dearer than the last.

모모As at the door, on meeting lingerd she,

모모And step by step my faithful ardour bless'd,For the last kiss herself entreated me,

모모And on my lips the last last kiss impress'd,--Thus clearly traced, the lov'd one's form we view,With flames engraven on a heart so true,--

모모A heart that, firm as some embattled tower,

모모Itself for her, her in itself reveres,For her rejoices in its lasting power,

모모Conscious alone, when she herself appears;Feels itself freer in so sweet a thrall,And only beats to give her thanks in all.

모모The power of loving, and all yearning sighs

모모For love responsive were effaced and drown'd;While longing hope for joyous enterprise

모모Was form'd, and rapid action straightway found;If love can e'er a loving one inspire,Most lovingly it gave me now its fire;

모모And 'twas through her!--an inward sorrow lay

모모On soul and body, heavily oppress'd;To mournful phantoms was my sight a prey,

모모In the drear void of a sad tortured breast;Now on the well-known threshold Hope hath smil'd,Herself appeareth in the sunlight mild.

모모Unto the peace of God, which, as we read,

모모Blesseth us more than reason e'er bath done,Love's happy peace would I compare indeed,

모모When in the presence of the dearest one.There rests the heart, and there that sweetest thought,The thought of being hers, is check'd by nought.

모모In the pure bosom doth a yearning float,

모모Unto a holier, purer, unknown BeingIts grateful aspiration to devote,

모모The Ever-Nameless then unriddled seeing;We call it: piety!--such blest delightI feel a share in, when before her sight.

모모Before her sight, as 'neath the sun's hot ray,

모모Before her breath, as 'neath the spring's soft wind,In its deep wintry cavern melts away

모모Self-love, so long in icy chains confin'd;No selfishness and no self-will are nigh,For at her advent they were forced to fly.

모모It seems as though she said: "As hours pass by

모모They spread before us life with kindly plan;Small knowledge did the yesterday supply,

모모To know the morrow is conceal'd from man;And if the thought of evening made me start,The sun at setting gladden'd straight my heart.

모모"Act, then, as I, and look, with joyous mind,

모모The moment in the face; nor linger thou!Meet it with speed, so fraught with life, so kind

모모In action, and in love so radiant now;Let all things be where thou art, childlike ever,Thus thoult be all, thus, thou'lt be vanquish'd never."

모모Thou speakest well, methought, for as thy guide

모모The moment's favour did a god assign,And each one feels himself when by thy side,

모모Fate's fav'rite in a moment so divine;I tremble at thy look that bids me go,Why should I care such wisdom vast to know?

모모Now am I far! And what would best befit

모모The present minute? I could scarcely tell;Full many a rich possession offers it,

모모These but offend, and I would fain repel.Yearnings unquenchable still drive me on,All counsel, save unbounded tears, is gone.

모모Flow on, flow on in never-ceasing course,

모모Yet may ye never quench my inward fire!Within my bosom heaves a mighty force,

모모Where death and life contend in combat dire.Medicines may serve the body's pangs to still;Nought but the spirit fails in strength of will,--

모모Fails in conception; wherefore fails it so?

모모A thousand times her image it portrays;Enchanting now, and now compell'd to go,

모모Now indistinct, now clothed in purest rays!How could the smallest comfort here be flowing?The ebb and flood, the coming and the going!

모모*      *      *      *      *      *

모모Leave me here now, my life's companions true!

모모Leave me alone on rock, in moor and heath;But courage! open lies the world to you,

모모The glorious heavens above, the earth beneath;Observe, investigate, with searching eyes,And nature will disclose her mysteries.

모모To me is all, I to myself am lost,

모모Who the immortals' fav'rite erst was thought;They, tempting, sent Pandoras to my cost,

모모So rich in wealth, with danger far more fraught;They urged me to those lips, with rapture crown'd,Deserted me, and hurl'd me to the ground.

모모1823.

모모III.  ATONEMENT.

모모[Composed, when 74 years old, for a Polish lady, who excelled inplaying on the pianoforte.]

모모PASSION brings reason--who can pacify

모모An anguish'd heart whose loss hath been so great?Where are the hours that fled so swiftly by?

모모In vain the fairest thou didst gain from fate;Sad is the soul, confused the enterprise;

모모The glorious world, how on the sense it dies!

모모In million tones entwined for evermore,

모모Music with angel-pinions hovers there,To pierce man's being to its inmost core,

모모Eternal beauty has its fruit to bear;The eye grows moist, in yearnings blest reveresThe godlike worth of music as of tears.

모모And so the lighten'd heart soon learns to see

모모That it still lives, and beats, and ought to beat,Off'ring itself with joy and willingly,

모모In grateful payment for a gift so sweet.And then was felt,--oh may it constant prove!--The twofold bliss of music and of love.

모모1823.-----

모모THE remembrance of the GoodKeep us ever glad in mood.

모모The remembrance of the FairMakes a mortal rapture share.

모모The remembrance of one's LoveBlest Is, if it constant prove.

모모The remembrance of the OneIs the greatest joy that's known.

모모1828.-----[Written at the age of 77.]

모모WHEN I was still a youthful wight,

모모So full of enjoyment and merry,The painters used to assert, in spite,

모모That my features were small--yes, very;Yet then full many a beauteous childWith true affection upon me smil'd.

모모Now as a greybeard I sit here in state,

모모By street and by lane held in awe, sirs;And may be seen, like old Frederick the Great,

모모On pipebowls, on cups, and on saucers.Yet the beauteous maidens, they keep afar;Oh vision of youth! Oh golden star!

모모1826.-----FOR EVER.

모모THE happiness that man, whilst prison'd here,

모모Is wont with heavenly rapture to compare,--The harmony of Truth, from wavering clear,--

모모Of Friendship that is free from doubting care,--The light which in stray thoughts alone can cheer

모모The wise,--the bard alone in visions fair,--In my best hours I found in her all this,And made mine own, to mine exceeding bliss.

모모1820.*-----FROM AN ALBUM OF 1604.

모모HOPE provides wings to thought, and love to hope.Rise up to Cynthia, love, when night is clearest,And say, that as on high her figure changeth,So, upon earth, my joy decays and grows.And whisper in her ear with modest softness,How doubt oft hung its head, and truth oft wept.And oh ye thoughts, distrustfully inclined,If ye are therefore by the loved one chided,Answer: 'tis true ye change, but alter not,As she remains the same, yet changeth ever.Doubt may invade the heart, but poisons not,For love is sweeter, by suspicion flavour'd.If it with anger overcasts the eye,And heaven's bright purity perversely blackens,Then zephyr-sighs straight scare the clouds away,And, changed to tears, dissolve them into rain.Thought, hope, and love remain there as before,Till Cynthia gleams upon me as of old.

모모1820.*-----LINES ON SEEING SCHILLER'S SKULL.

모모[This curious imitation of the ternary metre of Dante was writtenat the age of 77.]

모모WITHIN a gloomy charnel-house one day

모모I view'd the countless skulls, so strangely mated,And of old times I thought, that now were grey.

모모Close pack'd they stand, that once so fiercely hated,And hardy bones, that to the death contended,

모모Are lying cross'd,--to lie for ever, fated.What held those crooked shoulder-blades suspended?

모모No one now asks; and limbs with vigour fired,The hand, the foot--their use in life is ended.

모모Vainly ye sought the tomb for rest when tired;Peace in the grave may not be yours; ye're driven

모모Back into daylight by a force inspired;But none can love the wither'd husk, though even

모모A glorious noble kernel it contained.To me, an adept, was the writing given

모모Which not to all its holy sense explained,When 'mid the crowd, their icy shadows flinging,

모모I saw a form, that glorious still remained.And even there, where mould and damp were clinging,

모모Gave me a blest, a rapture-fraught emotion,As though from death a living fount were springing.

모모What mystic joy I felt! What rapt devotion!That form, how pregnant with a godlike trace!

모모A look, how did it whirl me tow'rd that oceanWhose rolling billows mightier shapes embrace!

모모Mysterious vessel! Oracle how dear!Even to grasp thee is my hand too base,

모모Except to steal thee from thy prison hereWith pious purpose, and devoutly go

모모Back to the air, free thoughts, and sunlight clear.What greater gain in life can man e'er know

모모Than when God-Nature will to him explainHow into Spirit steadfastness may flow,

모모How steadfast, too, the Spirit-Born remain.

모모1826.-----ROYAL PRAYER.

모모HA, I am the lord of earth! The noble,

모모Who're in my service, love me.Ha, I am the lord of earth! The noble,

모모O'er whom my sway extendeth, love I.Oh, grant me, God in Heaven, that I may ne'er

모모Dispense with loftiness and love!

모모1815.*-----HUMAN FEELINGS.

모모AH, ye gods! ye great immortalsIn the spacious heavens above us!Would ye on this earth but give usSteadfast minds and dauntless courageWe, oh kindly ones, would leave youAll your spacious heavens above us!

모모1815.*-----ON THE DIVAN.

모모HE who knows himself and others

모모Here will also see,That the East and West, like brothers,

모모Parted ne'er shall be.

모모Thoughtfully to float for ever

모모'Tween two worlds, be man's endeavour!So between the East and West

모모To revolve, be my behest!

모모1833.*-----EXPLANATION  OF AN ANCIENT WOODCUT, REPRESENTINGHANS SACHS' POETICAL MISSION.

모모[I feel considerable hesitation in venturing to offer thisversion of a poem which Carlyle describes to be 'a beautifulpiece (a very Hans Sacks beatified, both in character and style),which we wish there was any possibility of translating.' Thereader will be aware that Hans Sachs was the celebrated Minstrel-Cobbler of Nuremberg, who Wrote 208 plays, 1700 comic tales, andbetween 4000 and 5000 lyric poems. He flourished throughoutalmost the whole of the 16th century.]

모모EARLY within his workshop here,On Sundays stands our master dear;His dirty apron he puts away,And wears a cleanly doublet to-day;Lets wax'd thread, hammer, and pincers rest,And lays his awl within his chest;The seventh day he takes reposeFrom many pulls and many blows.

모모Soon as the spring-sun meets his view,Repose begets him labour anew;He feels that he holds within his brainA little world, that broods there amain,And that begins to act and to live,Which he to others would gladly give.

모모He had a skilful eye and true,And was full kind and loving too.For contemplation, clear and pure,--For making all his own again, sure;He had a tongue that charm'd when 'twas heard,And graceful and light flow'd ev'ry word;Which made the Muses in him rejoice,The Master-singer of their choice.

모모And now a maiden enter'd there,With swelling breast, and body fair;With footing firm she took her place,And moved with stately, noble grace;She did not walk in wanton mood,Nor look around with glances lewd.

모모She held a measure in her hand,Her girdle was a golden band,A wreath of corn was on her head,Her eye the day's bright lustre shed;Her name is honest Industry,Else, Justice, Magnanimity.

모모She enter'd with a kindly greeting;He felt no wonder at the meeting,For, kind and fair as she might be,He long had known her, fancied he.

모모"I have selected thee," she said,"From all who earth's wild mazes tread,That thou shouldst have clear-sighted sense,And nought that's wrong shouldst e'er commence.When others run in strange confusion,Thy gaze shall see through each illusionWhen others dolefully complain,Thy cause with jesting thou shalt gain,Honour and right shalt value duly,In everything act simply, truly,--Virtue and godliness proclaim,And call all evil by its name,Nought soften down, attempt no quibble,Nought polish up, nought vainly scribble.The world shall stand before thee, then,As seen by Albert Durer's ken,In manliness and changeless life,In inward strength, with firmness rife.Fair Nature's Genius by the handShall lead thee on through every land,Teach thee each different life to scan,Show thee the wondrous ways of man,His shifts, confusions, thrustings, and drubbings,Pushings, tearings, pressings, and rubbings;The varying madness of the crew,The anthill's ravings bring to view;But thou shalt see all this express'd,As though 'twere in a magic chest.Write these things down for folks on earth,In hopes they may to wit give birth."--Then she a window open'd wide,And show'd a motley crowd outside,All kinds of beings 'neath the sky,As in his writings one may spy.

모모Our master dear was, after this,On Nature thinking, full of bliss,When tow'rd him, from the other sideHe saw an aged woman glide;The name she bears, Historia,Mythologia, Fabula;With footstep tottering and unstableShe dragg'd a large and wooden carved-table,Where, with wide sleeves and human mien,The Lord was catechizing seen;Adam, Eve, Eden, the Serpent's seduction,Gomorrah and Sodom's awful destruction,The twelve illustrious women, too,That mirror of honour brought to view;All kinds of bloodthirstiness, murder, and sin,The twelve wicked tyrants also were in,And all kinds of goodly doctrine and law;Saint Peter with his scourge you saw,With the world's ways dissatisfied,And by our Lord with power supplied.Her train and dress, behind and before,And e'en the seams, were painted o'erWith tales of worldly virtue and crime.--Our master view'd all this for a time;The sight right gladly he survey'd,So useful for him in his trade,Whence he was able to procureExample good and precept sure,Recounting all with truthful care,As though he had been present there.His spirit seem'd from earth to fly,He ne'er had turned away his eye,Did he not just behind him hearA rattle of bells approaching near.And now a fool doth catch his eye,With goat and ape's leap drawing nighA merry interlude preparingWith fooleries and jests unsparing.Behind him, in a line drawn out,He dragg'd all fools, the lean and stout,The great and little, the empty and full,All too witty, and all too dull,A lash he flourish'd overhead,As though a dance of apes he led,Abusing them with bitterness,As though his wrath would ne'er grow less.

모모While on this sight our master gazed,His head was growing well-nigh crazed:What words for all could he e'er find,Could such a medley be combined?Could he continue with delightFor evermore to sing and write?When lo, from out a cloud's dark bedIn at the upper window spedThe Muse, in all her majesty,As fair as our loved maids we see.With clearness she around him threwHer truth, that ever stronger grew.

모모"I, to ordain thee come," she spake:"So prosper, and my blessing take!The holy fire that slumb'ring liesWithin thee, in bright flames shall rise;Yet that thine ever-restless lifeMay still with kindly strength be rife,I, for thine inward spirit's calm.Have granted nourishment and balm,That rapture may thy soul imbue,Like some fair blossom bathed in dew."--Behind his house then secretlyOutside the doorway pointed she,Where, in a shady garden-nook,A beauteous maid with downcast lookWas sitting where a stream was flowing,With elder bushes near it growing,She sat beneath an apple tree,And nought around her seem'd to see.Her lap was full of roses fair,Which in a wreath she twined with care.And, with them, leaves and blossoms blended:For whom was that sweet wreath intended?Thus sat she, modest and retired,Her bosom throbb'd, with hope inspired;Such deep forebodings fill'd her mind,No room for wishing could she find,And with the thoughts that o'er it flew,Perchance a sigh was mingled too.

모모"But why should sorrow cloud thy brow?That, dearest love, which fills thee nowIs fraught with joy and ecstasy.Prepared in one alone for thee,That he within thine eye may findSolace when fortune proves unkind,And be newborn through many a kiss,That he receives with inward bliss;When'er he clasps thee to his breast.May he from all his toils find restWhen he in thy dear arms shall sink,May he new life and vigour drink:Fresh joys of youth shalt thou obtain,In merry jest rejoice again.With raillery and roguish spite,Thou now shalt tease him, now delight.Thus Love will nevermore grow old,Thus will the minstrel ne'er be cold!"

모모While he thus lives, in secret bless'd,Above him in the clouds doth restAn oak-wreath, verdant and sublime,Placed on his brow in after-time;While they are banish'd to the slough,Who their great master disavow.

모모1776.-----

모모SONNETS.

모모-----Lovingly I'll sing of love;Ever comes she from above.-----THE FRIENDLY MEETING.

모모IN spreading mantle to my chin conceald,

모모I trod the rocky path, so steep and grey,

모모Then to the wintry plain I bent my wayUneasily, to flight my bosom steel'd.

모모But sudden was the newborn day reveal'd:

모모A maiden came, in heavenly bright array,

모모Like the fair creatures of the poet's layIn realms of song. My yearning heart was heal'd.

모모Yet turn'd I thence, till she had onward pass'd,

모모While closer still the folds to draw I tried,

모모As though with heat self-kindled to grow warm;

모모But follow'd her. She stood. The die was cast!

모모No more within my mantle could I hide;

모모I threw it off,--she lay within mine arm.

모모1807-8.-----IN A WORD.

모모THUS to be chain'd for ever, can I bear?

모모A very torment that, in truth, would be.

모모This very day my new resolve shall see.--I'll not go near the lately-worshipp'd Fair.

모모Yet what excuse, my heart, can I prepare

모모In such a case, for not consulting thee?

모모But courage! while our sorrows utter weIn tones where love, grief, gladness have a share.

모모But see! the minstrel's bidding to obey,

모모Its melody pours forth the sounding lyre,

모모Yearning a sacrifice of love to bring.

모모Scarce wouldst thou think it--ready is the lay;

모모Well, but what then? Methought in the first fire

모모We to her presence flew, that lay to sing.

모모1807?.-----THE MAIDEN SPEAKS.

모모How grave thou loookest, loved one! wherefore so?

모모Thy marble image seems a type of thee;

모모Like it, no sign of life thou giv'st to me;Compared with thee, the stone appears to glow.

모모Behind his shield in ambush lurks the foe,

모모The friend's brow all-unruffled we should see.

모모I seek thee, but thou seek'st away to flee;Fix'd as this sculptured figure, learn to grow!

모모Tell me, to which should I the preference pay?

모모Must I from both with coldness meet alone?

모모The one is lifeless, thou with life art blest.

모모In short, no longer to throw words away,

모모I'll fondy kiss and kiss and kiss this stone,

모모Till thou dost tear me hence with envious breast.

모모1807.-----GROWTH.

모모O'ER field and plain, in childhood's artless days,

모모Thou sprang'st with me, on many a spring-morn fair.

모모"For such a daughter, with what pleasing care,Would I, as father, happy dwellings raise!"

모모And when thou on the world didst cast thy gaze,

모모Thy joy was then in household toils to share.

모모"Why did I trust her, why she trust me e'er?For such a sister, how I Heaven should praise!"

모모Nothing can now the beauteous growth retard;

모모Love's glowing flame within my breast is fann'd.

모모Shall I embrace her form, my grief to end?

모모Thee as a queen must I, alas, regard:

모모So high above me placed thou seem'st to stand;

모모Before a passing look I meekly bend.

모모1807?.-----FOOD IN TRAVEL.

모모IF to her eyes' bright lustre I were blind,

모모No longer would they serve my life to gild.

모모The will of destiny must be fulfilid,--This knowing, I withdrew with sadden'd mind.

모모No further happiness I now could find:

모모The former longings of my heart were still'd;

모모I sought her looks alone, whereon to buildMy joy in life,--all else was left behind.

모모Wine's genial glow, the festal banquet gay,

모모Ease, sleep, and friends, all wonted pleasures glad

모모I spurn'd, till little there remain'd to prove.

모모Now calmly through the world I wend my way:

모모That which I crave may everywhere be had,

모모With me I bring the one thing needful--love.

모모1807?.-----DEPARTURE.

모모WITH many a thousand kiss not yet content,

모모At length with One kiss I was forced to go;

모모After that bitter parting's depth of woe,I deem'd the shore from which my steps I bent,

모모Its hills, streams, dwellings, mountains, as I went,

모모A pledge of joy, till daylight ceased to glow;

모모Then on my sight did blissful visions growIn the dim-lighted, distant firmament,

모모And when at length the sea confined my gaze,

모모My ardent longing fill'd my heart once more;

모모What I had lost, unwillingly I sought.

모모Then Heaven appear'd to shed its kindly rays:

모모Methought that all I had possess'd of yore

모모Remain'd still mine--that I was reft of nought.

모모1807?.-----THE LOVING ONE WRITES.

모모THE look that thy sweet eyes on mine impress

모모The pledge thy lips to mine convey,--the kiss,--

모모He who, like me, hath knowledge sure of this,Can he in aught beside find happiness?

모모Removed from thee, friend-sever'd, in distress,

모모These thoughts I vainly struggle to dismiss:

모모They still return to that one hour of bliss,The only one; then tears my grief confess.

모모But unawares the tear makes haste to dry:

모모He loves, methinks, e'en to these glades so still,--

모모And shalt not thou to distant lands extend?

모모Receive the murmurs of his loving sigh;

모모My only joy on earth is in thy will,

모모Thy kindly will tow'rd me; a token send!

모모1807?.-----THE LOVING ONE ONCE MORE.

모모WHY do I o'er my paper once more bend?

모모Ask not too closely, dearest one, I pray

모모For, to speak truth, I've nothing now to say;Yet to thy hands at length 'twill come, dear friend.

모모Since I can come not with it, what I send

모모My undivided heart shall now convey,

모모With all its joys, hopes, pleasures, pains, to-day:All this hath no beginning, hath no end.

모모Henceforward I may ne'er to thee confide

모모How, far as thought, wish, fancy, will, can reach,

모모My faithful heart with thine is surely blended.

모모Thus stood I once enraptured by thy side,

모모Gazed on thee, and said nought. What need of speech?

모모My very being in itself was ended.

모모1807?.-----SHE CANNOT END.

모모WHEN unto thee I sent the page all white,

모모Instead of first thereon inscribing aught,

모모The space thou doubtless filledst up in sport.And sent it me, to make my joy grow bright.

모모As soon as the blue cover met my sight,

모모As well becomes a woman, quick as thought

모모I tore it open, leaving hidden nought,And read the well-known words of pure delight:

모모MY ONLY BEING! DEAREST HEART! SWEET CHILD!

모모How kindly thou my yearning then didst still

모모With gentle words, enthralling me to thee.

모모In truth methought I read thy whispers mild

모모Wherewith thou lovingly my soul didst fill,

모모E'en to myself for aye ennobling me.

모모1807?.-----NEMESIS.

모모WHEN through the nations stalks contagion wild,

모모We from them cautiously should steal away.

모모E'en I have oft with ling'ring and delayShunn'd many an influence, not to be defil'd.

모모And e'en though Amor oft my hours beguil'd,

모모At length with him preferr'd I not to play,

모모And so, too, with the wretched sons of clay,When four and three-lined verses they compil'd.

모모But punishment pursues the scoffer straight,

모모As if by serpent-torch of furies led

모모From bill to vale, from land to sea to fly.

모모I hear the genie's laughter at my fate;

모모Yet do I find all power of thinking fled

모모In sonnet-rage and love's fierce ecstasy.

모모1807-8.-----THE CHRISTMAS-BOX.

모모THIS box, mine own sweet darling, thou wilt find

모모With many a varied sweetmeat's form supplied;

모모The fruits are they of holy Christmas tide,But baked indeed, for children's use design'd.

모모I'd fain, in speeches sweet with skill combin'd,

모모Poetic sweetmeats for the feast provide;

모모But why in such frivolities confide?Perish the thought, with flattery to blind!

모모One sweet thing there is still, that from within,

모모Within us speaks,--that may be felt afar;

모모This may be wafted o'er to thee alone.

모모If thou a recollection fond canst win,

모모As if with pleasure gleam'd each well-known star,

모모The smallest gift thou never wilt disown.

모모1807.-----THE WARNING.

모모WHEN sounds the trumpet at the Judgment Day,

모모And when forever all things earthly die,

모모We must a full and true account supplyOf ev'ry useless word we dropp'd in play.

모모But what effect will all the words convey

모모Wherein with eager zeal and lovingly,

모모That I might win thy favour, labour'd I,If on thine ear alone they die away?

모모Therefore, sweet love, thy conscience bear in mind,

모모Remember well how long thou hast delay'd,

모모So that the world such sufferings may not know.

모모If I must reckon, and excuses find

모모For all things useless I to thee have said,

모모To a full year the Judgment Day will grow

모모1807?.-----THE EPOCHS.

모모ON Petrarch's heart, all other days before,

모모In flaming letters written, was impress d

모모GOOD FRIDAY. And on mine, be it confess'd,Is this year's ADVENT, as it passeth o'er.

모모I do not now begin,--I still adore

모모Her whom I early cherish'd in my breast;,

모모Then once again with prudence dispossess'd,And to whose heart I'm driven back once more.

모모The love of Petrarch, that all-glorious love,

모모Was unrequited, and, alas, full sad;

모모One long Good Friday 'twas, one heartache drear

모모But may my mistress' Advent ever prove,

모모With its palm-jubilee, so sweet and glad,

모모One endless Mayday, through the livelong year!

모모1807.-----THE DOUBTERS AND THE LOVERS.

모모THE DOUBTERS.

모모YE love, and sonnets write! Fate's strange behest!

모모The heart, its hidden meaning to declare,

모모Must seek for rhymes, uniting pair with pair:Learn, children, that the will is weak, at best.

모모Scarcely with freedom the o'erflowing breast

모모As yet can speak, and well may it beware;

모모Tempestuous passions sweep each chord that's there,Then once more sink to night and gentle rest.

모모Why vex yourselves and us, the heavy stone

모모Up the steep path but step by step to roll?

모모It falls again, and ye ne'er cease to strive.

모모THE LOVERS.

모모But we are on the proper road alone!

모모If gladly is to thaw the frozen soul,

모모The fire of love must aye be kept alive.

모모1807?.-----CHARADE.

모모Two words there 'are, both short, of beauty rare,

모모Whose sounds our lips so often love to frame,

모모But which with clearness never can proclaimThe things whose own peculiar stamp they bear.

모모'Tis well in days of age and youth so fair,

모모One on the other boldly to inflame;

모모And if those words together link'd we name,A blissful rapture we discover there.

모모But now to give them pleasure do I seek,

모모And in myself my happiness would find;

모모I hope in silence, but I hope for this:

모모Gently, as loved one's names, those words to speak

모모To see them both within one image shrin'd,

모모Both in one being to embrace with bliss.

모모1807.-----

모모EPIGRAMS.

모모-----In these numbers be express'dMeaning deep, 'neath merry jest.-----

모모TO ORIGINALS.

모모A FELLOW says: "I own no school or college;No master lives whom I acknowledge;And pray don't entertain the thoughtThat from the dead I e'er learnt aught."This, if I rightly understand,Means: "I'm a blockhead at first hand."

모모1815.-----THE SOLDIER'S CONSOLATION.

모모No! in truth there's here no lack:White the bread, the maidens black!To another town, next night:Black the bread, the maidens white!

모모1815.*-----GENIAL IMPULSE.

모모THUS roll I, never taking ease,My tub, like Saint Diogenes,Now serious am, now seek to please;Now love and hate in turn one sees;The motives now are those, now these;Now nothings, now realities.Thus roll I, never taking ease,My tub, like Saint Diogenes.

모모1810.-----NEITHER THIS NOR THAT.

모모IF thou to be a slave shouldst will,Thou'lt get no pity, but fare ill;And if a master thou wouldst be,The world will view it angrily;And if in statu quo thou stay,That thou art but a fool, they'll say.

모모1815.*-----THE WAY TO BEHAVE.

모모THOUGH tempers are bad and peevish folks swear,Remember to ruffle thy brows, friend, ne'er;And let not the fancies of women so fairE'er serve thy pleasure in life to impair.

모모1815.*-----THE BEST.

모모WHEN head and heart are busy, say,

모모What better can be found?Who neither loves nor goes astray,

모모Were better under ground.

모모1815.*-----AS BROAD AS IT'S LONG.

모모MODEST men must needs endure,

모모And the bold must humbly bow;Thus thy fate's the same, be sure,

모모Whether bold or modest thou.

모모1815.*-----THE RULE OF LIFE.

모모IF thou wouldst live unruffled by care,Let not the past torment thee e'er;As little as possible be thou annoy'd,And let the present be ever enjoy'd;Ne'er let thy breast with hate be supplied,And to God the future confide.

모모1815.*-----THE SAME, EXPANDED.

모모IF thou wouldst live unruffled by care,Let not the past torment thee e'er;If any loss thou hast to rue,Act as though thou wert born anew;Inquire the meaning of each day,What each day means itself will say;In thine own actions take thy pleasure,What others do, thou'lt duly treasure;Ne'er let thy breast with hate be supplied,And to God the future confide.

모모-----

모모IF wealth is gone--then something is gone!

모모Quick, make up thy mind,

모모And fresh wealth find.If honour is gone--then much is gone!

모모Seek glory to find,

모모And people then will alter their mind.If courage is gone--then all is gone!'Twere better that thou hadst never been born.

모모-----

모모HE who with life makes sport,

모모Can prosper never;Who rules himself in nought,

모모Is a slave ever.

모모MAY each honest effort be

모모Crown'd with lasting constancy.

모모-----

모모EACH road to the proper endRuns straight on, without a bend.

모모1825.-----CALM AT SEA.

모모SILENCE deep rules o'er the waters,

모모Calmly slumb'ring lies the main,While the sailor views with trouble

모모Nought but one vast level plain.

모모Not a zephyr is in motion!

모모Silence fearful as the grave!In the mighty waste of ocean

모모Sunk to rest is ev'ry wave.

모모1795.-----THE PROSPEROUS VOYAGE.

모모THE mist is fast clearing.And radiant is heaven,Whilst AEolus loosensOur anguish-fraught bond.The zephyrs are sighing,Alert is the sailor.Quick! nimbly be plying!The billows are riven,The distance approaches;I see land beyond!

모모1795.-----COURAGE.

모모CARELESSLY over the plain away,Where by the boldest man no pathCut before thee thou canst discern,Make for thyself a path!

모모Silence, loved one, my heart!Cracking, let it not break!Breaking, break not with thee!

모모1776.*-----MY ONLY PROPERTY.

모모I FEEL that I'm possess'd of nought,Saving the free unfetterd thought

모모Which from my bosom seeks to flow,And each propitious passing hourThat suffers me in all its power

모모A loving fate with truth to know.

모모1814.-----ADMONITION.

모모WHEREFORE ever ramble on?

모모For the Good is lying near,Fortune learn to seize alone,

모모For that Fortune's ever here.

모모1789.-----OLD AGE.

모모OLD age is courteous--no one more:For time after time he knocks at the door,But nobody says, "Walk in, sir, pray!"Yet turns he not from the door away,But lifts the latch, and enters with speed.And then they cry "A cool one, indeed!"

모모1814.-----EPITAPH.

모모As a boy, reserved and naughty;As a youth, a coxcomb and haughty;As a man, for action inclined;As a greybeard, fickle in mind.--Upon thy grave will people read:This was a very man, indeed!

모모1815.*-----RULES FOR MONARCHS.

모모IF men are never their thoughts to employ,Take care to provide them a life full of joy;But if to some profit and use thou wouldst bend them,Take care to shear them, and then defend them.

모모1815.*-----PAULO POST FUTURI.

모모WEEP ye not, ye children dear,

모모That as yet ye are unborn:For each sorrow and each tear

모모Makes the father's heart to mourn.

모모Patient be a short time to it,

모모Unproduced, and known to none;If your father cannot do it,

모모By your mother 'twill be done.

모모1784.-----THE FOOL'S EPILOGUE.

모모MANY good works I've done and ended,Ye take the praise--I'm not offended;For in the world, I've always thoughtEach thing its true position hath sought.When praised for foolish deeds am I,I set off laughing heartily;When blamed for doing something good,I take it in an easy mood.If some one stronger gives me hard blows,That it's a jest, I feign to suppose:But if 'tis one that's but my own like,I know the way such folks to strike.When Fortune smiles, I merry grow,And sing in dulci jubilo;When sinks her wheel, and tumbles me o'er,I think 'tis sure to rise once more.

모모In the sunshine of summer I ne'er lament,Because the winter it cannot prevent;And when the white snow-flakes fall around,I don my skates, and am off with a bound.Though I dissemble as I will,The sun for me will ne'er stand still;The old and wonted course is run,Until the whole of life is done;Each day the servant like the lord,In turns comes home, and goes abroad;If proud or humble the line they take,They all must eat, drink, sleep, and wake.So nothing ever vexes me;Act like the fool, and wise ye'll be!

모모1804.-----

모모PARABLES.

모모-----Joy from that in type we borrow,Which in life gives only sorrow.-----JOY.

모모A DRAGON-FLY with beauteous wingIs hov'ring o'er a silv'ry spring;I watch its motions with delight,--Now dark its colours seem, now bright;Chameleon-like appear, now blue,Now red, and now of greenish hue.Would it would come still nearer me,That I its tints might better see

모모It hovers, flutters, resting ne'er!

모모But hush! it settles on the mead.I have it safe now, I declare!

모모And when its form I closely view,

모모'Tis of a sad and dingy blue--Such, Joy-Dissector, is thy case indeed

모모1767-9.-----EXPLANATION OF AN ANTIQUE GEM,

모모A YOUNG fig-tree its form lifts high

모모Within a beauteous garden;And see, a goat is sitting by.

모모As if he were its warden.

모모But oh, Quirites, how one errs!

모모The tree is guarded badly;For round the other side there whirrs

모모And hums a beetle madly.

모모The hero with his well-mail'd coat

모모Nibbles the branches tall so;A mighty longing feels the goat

모모Gently to climb up also.

모모And so, my friends, ere long ye see

모모The tree all leafless standing;It looks a type of misery,

모모Help of the gods demanding.

모모Then listen, ye ingenuous youth,

모모Who hold wise saws respected:From he-goat and from beetles-tooth

모모A tree should be protected!

모모1815.-----CAT-PIE.

모모WHILE he is mark'd by vision clear

모모Who fathoms Nature's treasures,The man may follow, void of fear,

모모Who her proportions measures.

모모Though for one mortal, it is true,

모모These trades may both be fitted,Yet, that the things themselves are two

모모Must always be admitted.

모모Once on a time there lived a cook

모모Whose skill was past disputing,Who in his head a fancy took

모모To try his luck at shooting.

모모So, gun in hand, he sought a spot

모모Where stores of game were breeding,And there ere long a cat he shot

모모That on young birds was feeding.

모모This cat he fancied was a hare,

모모Forming a judgment hasty,So served it up for people's fare,

모모Well-spiced and in a pasty.

모모Yet many a guest with wrath was fill'd

모모(All who had noses tender):The cat that's by the sportsman kill'd

모모No cook a hare can render.

모모1810.-----LEGEND.

모모THERE lived in the desert a holy man

모모To whom a goat-footed Faun one dayPaid a visit, and thus began

모모To his surprise: "I entreat thee to prayThat grace to me and my friends may be given,That we may be able to mount to Heaven,For great is our thirst for heav'nly bliss."The holy man made answer to this:"Much danger is lurking in thy petition,Nor will it be easy to gain admission;Thou dost not come with an angel's salute;For I see thou wearest a cloven foot."The wild man paused, and then answer'd he:"What doth my goat's foot matter to thee?Full many I've known into heaven to passStraight and with ease, with the head of an ass!"

모모1815.*-----AUTHORS.

모모OVER the meadows, and down the stream,

모모And through the garden-walks straying,He plucks the flowers that fairest seem;

모모His throbbing heart brooks no delaying.His maiden then comes--oh, what ecstasy!Thy flowers thou giv'st for one glance of her eye!

모모The gard'ner next door o'er the hedge sees the youth:"I'm not such a fool as that, in good truth;My pleasure is ever to cherish each flower,And see that no birds my fruit e'er devour.But when 'tis ripe, your money, good neighbour!'Twas not for nothing I took all this labour!"And such, methinks, are the author-tribe.

모모The one his pleasures around him strews,

모모That his friends, the public, may reap, if they choose;The other would fain make them all subscribe,

모모1776.*-----THE CRITIC.

모모I HAD a fellow as my guest,Not knowing he was such a pest,And gave him just my usual fare;He ate his fill of what was there,

모모And for desert my best things swallow'd,Soon as his meal was o'er, what follow'd?Led by the Deuce, to a neighbour he went,And talk'd of my food to his heart's content:"The soup might surely have had more spice,The meat was ill-brown'd, and the wine wasn't nice."A thousand curses alight on his head!'Tis a critic, I vow! Let the dog be struck dead!

모모1776.*-----THE DILETTANTE AND THE CRITIC.

모모A BOY a pigeon once possess'd,In gay and brilliant plumage dress'd;He loved it well, and in boyish sportIts food to take from his mouth he taught,And in his pigeon he took such pride,That his joy to others he needs must confide.

모모An aged fox near the place chanc'd to dwell,Talkative, clever, and learned as well;The boy his society used to prize,Hearing with pleasure his wonders and lies.

모모"My friend the fox my pigeon must seeHe ran, and stretch'd 'mongst the bushes lay he"Look, fox, at my pigeon, my pigeon so fair!His equal I'm sure thou hast look'd upon ne'er!"

모모"Let's see!"--The boy gave it.--"'Tis really not bad;And yet, it is far from complete, I must add.The feathers, for, instance, how short! 'Tis absurd!"So he set to work straightway to pluck the poor bird.

모모The boy screamed.--"Thou must now stronger pinions supply,Or else 'twill be ugly, unable to fly."--Soon 'twas stripp'd--oh, the villain!--and torn all to pieces.The boy was heart-broken,--and so my tale ceases.

모모*         *         *         *

모모He who sees in the boy shadow'd forth his own case,Should be on his guard 'gainst the fox's whole race.

모모1776.*-----THE WRANGLER.

모모ONE day a shameless and impudent wightWent into a shop full of steel wares bright,Arranged with art upon ev'ry shelf.He fancied they were all meant for himself;And so, while the patient owner stood by,The shining goods needs must handle and try,And valued,--for how should a fool better know?--The bad things high, and the good ones low,And all with an easy self-satisfied face;Then, having bought nothing, he left the place.

모모The tradesman now felt sorely vex'd,So when the fellow went there next,A lock of steel made quite red hot.The other cried upon the spot:"Such wares as these, who'd ever buy?the steel is tarnish'd shamefully,"--Then pull'd it, like a fool about,But soon set up a piteous shout."Pray what's the matter?" the shopman spoke;The other scream'd: "Faith, a very cool joke!"

모모1815.*-----THE YELPERS.

모모OUR rides in all directions bend,

모모For business or for pleasure,Yet yelpings on our steps attend,

모모And barkings without measure.The dog that in our stable dwells,

모모After our heels is striding,And all the while his noisy yells

모모But show that we are riding.

모모1815.*-----THE STORK'S VOCATION.

모모THE stork who worms and frogs devours

모모That in our ponds reside,Why should he dwell on high church-towers,

모모With which he's not allied?

모모Incessantly he chatters there,

모모And gives our ears no rest;But neither old nor young can dare

모모To drive him from his nest.

모모I humbly ask it,--how can he

모모Give of his title proof,Save by his happy tendency

모모To soil the church's roof?-----CELEBRITY.

모모[A satire on his own Sorrows of Werther.]

모모ON bridges small and bridges greatStands Nepomucks in ev'ry state,Of bronze, wood, painted, or of stone,Some small as dolls, some giants grown;Each passer must worship before Nepomuck,Who to die on a bridge chanced to have the ill luck,When once a man with head and earsA saint in people's eyes appears,Or has been sentenced piteouslyBeneath the hangman's hand to die,He's as a noted person prized,In portrait is immortalized.Engravings, woodcuts, are supplied,And through the world spread far and wide.Upon them all is seen his name,And ev'ry one admits his claim;Even the image of the LordIs not with greater zeal ador'd.Strange fancy of the human race!Half sinner frail, half child of graceWe see HERR WERTHER of the storyIn all the pomp of woodcut glory.His worth is first made duly known,By having his sad features shownAt ev'ry fair the country round;In ev'ry alehouse too they're found.His stick is pointed by each dunce"The ball would reach his brain at once!"And each says, o'er his beer and bread:"Thank Heav'n that 'tis not we are dead!"

모모1815.*-----PLAYING AT PRIESTS.

모모WITHIN a town where parityAccording to old form we see,--That is to say, where CatholicAnd Protestant no quarrels pick,And where, as in his father's day,Each worships God in his own way,We Luth'ran children used to dwell,By songs and sermons taught as well.The Catholic clingclang in truthSounded more pleasing to our youth,For all that we encounter'd there,To us seem'd varied, joyous, fair.As children, monkeys, and mankindTo ape each other are inclin'd,We soon, the time to while away,A game at priests resolved to play.Their aprons all our sisters lentFor copes, which gave us great content;And handkerchiefs, embroider'd o'er,Instead of stoles we also wore;Gold paper, whereon beasts were traced,The bishop's brow as mitre graced.

모모Through house and garden thus in stateWe strutted early, strutted late,Repeating with all proper unction,Incessantly each holy function.The best was wanting to the game;

모모We knew that a sonorous ring

모모Was here a most important thing;But Fortune to our rescue came,For on the ground a halter lay;

모모We were delighted, and at once

모모Made it a bellrope for the nonce,And kept it moving all the day;

모모In turns each sister and each brother

모모Acted as sexton to another;All help'd to swell the joyous throng;

모모The whole proceeded swimmingly,

모모And since no actual bell had we,We all in chorus sang, Ding dong!

모모*      *      *      *      *

모모Our guileless child's-sport long was hush'd

모모In memory's tomb, like some old lay;And yet across my mind it rush'd

모모With pristine force the other day.The New-Poetic CatholicsIn ev'ry point its aptness fix!

모모1815.*-----SONGS.

모모SONGS are like painted window-panes!In darkness wrapp'd the church remains,If from the market-place we view it;Thus sees the ignoramus through it.No wonder that he deems it tame,--And all his life 'twill be the same.

모모But let us now inside repair,And greet the holy Chapel there!At once the whole seems clear and bright,Each ornament is bathed in light,And fraught with meaning to the sight.God's children! thus your fortune prize,Be edified, and feast your eyes!

모모1827.*-----POETRY.

모모GOD to his untaught children sent

모모Law, order, knowledge, art, from high,And ev'ry heav'nly favour lent,

모모The world's hard lot to qualify.They knew not how they should behave,

모모For all from Heav'n stark-naked came;But Poetry their garments gave,

모모And then not one had cause for shame.

모모1816.-----A PARABLE.

모모I PICKED a rustic nosegay lately,And bore it homewards, musing greatly;When, heated by my hand, I foundThe heads all drooping tow'rd the ground.I plac'd them in a well-cool'd glass,And what a wonder came to passThe heads soon raised themselves once more.The stalks were blooming as before,And all were in as good a caseAs when they left their native place.

모모*      *      *      *

모모So felt I, when I wond'ring heardMy song to foreign tongues transferr'd.

모모1828.-----SHOULD E'ER THE LOVELESS DAY.

모모SHOULD e'er the loveless day remainObscured by storms of hail and rain,

모모Thy charms thou showest never;I tap at window, tap at door:Come, lov'd one, come! appear once more!

모모Thou art as fair as ever!

모모1827.*-----A PLAN THE MUSES ENTERTAINED.

모모A PLAN the Muses entertain'd

모모Methodically to impart

모모To Psyche the poetic art;Prosaic-pure her soul remain'd.No wondrous sounds escaped her lyre

모모E'en in the fairest Summer night;But Amor came with glance of fire,--

모모The lesson soon was learn'd aright.

모모1827.*-----THE DEATH OF THE FLY.

모모WITH eagerness he drinks the treach'rous potion,

모모Nor stops to rest, by the first taste misled;Sweet is the draught, but soon all power of motion

모모He finds has from his tender members fled;No longer has he strength to plume his wing,No longer strength to raise his head, poor thing!E'en in enjoyment's hour his life he loses,His little foot to bear his weight refuses;So on he sips, and ere his draught is o'er,Death veils his thousand eyes for evermore.

모모1810.-----BY THE RIVER.

모모WHEN by the broad stream thou dost dwell,

모모Oft shallow is its sluggish flood;Then, when thy fields thou tendest well,

모모It o'er them spreads its slime and mud.

모모The ships descend ere daylight wanes,

모모The prudent fisher upward goes;Round reef and rock ice casts its chains,

모모And boys at will the pathway close.

모모To this attend, then, carefully,

모모And what thou wouldst, that execute!Ne'er linger, ne'er o'erhasty be,

모모For time moves on with measured foot.

모모1821.*-----THE FOX AND CRANE.

모모ONCE two persons uninvited

모모Came to join my dinner table;For the nonce they lived united,

모모Fox and crane yclept in fable.

모모Civil greetings pass'd between us

모모Then I pluck'd some pigeons tenderFor the fox of jackal-genius,

모모Adding grapes in full-grown splendour.

모모Long-neck'd flasks I put as dishes

모모For the crane, without delaying,Fill'd with gold and silver fishes,

모모In the limpid water playing.

모모Had ye witness'd Reynard planted

모모At his flat plate, all demurely,Ye with envy must have granted:

모모"Ne'er was such a gourmand, surely!"

모모While the bird with circumspection

모모On one foot, as usual, cradled,From the flasks his fish-refection

모모With his bill and long neck ladled.

모모One the pigeons praised,--the other,

모모As they went, extoll'd the fishes,Each one scoffing at his brother

모모For preferring vulgar dishes.

모모*        *        *

모모If thou wouldst preserve thy credit,

모모When thou askest folks to guzzleAt thy hoard, take care to spread it

모모Suited both for bill and muzzle.

모모1819.-----THE FOX AND HUNTSMAN.

모모HARD 'tis on a fox's traces

모모To arrive, midst forest-glades;Hopeless utterly the chase is,

모모If his flight the huntsman aids.

모모And so 'tis with many a wonder,

모모(Why A B make Ab in fact,)Over which we gape and blunder,

모모And our head and brains distract.

모모1821.*-----THE FROGS.

모모A POOL was once congeal'd with frost;The frogs, in its deep waters lost,

모모No longer dared to croak or spring;But promised, being half asleep,If suffer'd to the air to creep,

모모As very nightingales to sing.

모모A thaw dissolved the ice so strong,--They proudly steer'd themselves along,When landed, squatted on the shore,And croak'd as loudly as before.

모모1821.*-----THE WEDDING.

모모A FEAST was in a village spread,--It was a wedding-day, they said.The parlour of the inn I found,And saw the couples whirling round,Each lass attended by her lad,And all seem'd loving, blithe, and glad;

모모But on my asking for the bride,A fellow with a stare, replied:"'Tis not the place that point to raise!

모모We're only dancing in her honour;We now have danced three nights and days,

모모And not bestowed one thought upon her."

모모*      *      *      *

모모Whoe'er in life employs his eyesSuch cases oft will recognise.

모모1821.*-----BURIAL.

모모To the grave one day from a house they bore

모모A maiden;To the window the citizens went to explore;In splendour they lived, and with wealth as of yore

모모Their banquets were laden.Then thought they: "The maid to the tomb is now borne;We too from our dwellings ere long must be torn,And he that is left our departure to mourn,

모모To our riches will be the successor,

모모For some one must be their possessor.

모모1827.*-----THREATENING SIGNS.

모모IF Venus in the evening skyIs seen in radiant majesty,If rod-like comets, red as blood,Are 'mongst the constellations view'd,Out springs the Ignoramus, yelling:"The star's exactly o'er my dwelling!What woeful prospect, ah, for me!Then calls his neighbour mournfully:"Behold that awful sign of evil,Portending woe to me, poor devil!My mother's asthma ne'er will leave her,My child is sick with wind and fever;I dread the illness of my wife,A week has pass'd, devoid of strife,--And other things have reach'd my ear;The Judgment Day has come, I fear!"

모모His neighbour answered: "Friend, you're right!Matters look very had to-night.Let's go a street or two, though, hence,And gaze upon the stars from thence."--No change appears in either case.Let each remain then in his place,And wisely do the best he can,Patient as any other man.

모모1821.*-----THE BUYERS.

모모To an apple-woman's stall

모모Once some children nimbly ran;Longing much to purchase all,They with joyous haste beganSnatching up the piles there raised,While with eager eyes they gazedOn the rosy fruit so nice;But when they found out the price,Down they threw the whole they'd got,Just as if they were red hot.

모모*      *      *      *      *

모모The man who gratis will his goods supplyWill never find a lack of folks to buy!

모모1820.-----THE MOUNTAIN VILLAGE.

모모"THE mountain village was destroy'd;But see how soon is fill'd the void!Shingles and boards, as by magic arise,The babe in his cradle and swaddling-clothes lies;How blest to trust to God's protection!"

모모Behold a wooden new erection,So that, if sparks and wind but choose,God's self at such a game must lose!

모모1821.*-----SYMBOLS.

모모PALM Sunday at the Vatican

모모They celebrate with palms;With reverence bows each holy man,

모모And chaunts the ancient psalms.Those very psalms are also sung

모모With olive boughs in hand,While holly, mountain wilds among,

모모In place of palms must stand:In fine, one seeks some twig that's green,

모모And takes a willow rod,So that the pious man may e'en

모모In small things praise his God.

모모And if ye have observed it well,

모모To gain what's fit ye're able,If ye in faith can but excel;

모모Such are the myths of fable.

모모1827.*-----THREE PALINODIAS.

모모I.

모모"Incense is hut a tribute for the gods,--To mortals 'tis but poison."

모모THE smoke that from thine altar blows,

모모Can it the gods offend?For I observe thou hold'st thy nose--

모모Pray what does this portend?Mankind deem incense to excel

모모Each other earthly thing,So he that cannot bear its smell,

모모No incense e'er should bring.

모모With unmoved face by thee at least

모모To dolls is homage given;If not obstructed by the priest,

모모The scent mounts up to heaven.

모모1827.*

모모II

모모CONFLICT OF WIT AND BEAUTY.

모모SIR Wit, who is so much esteem'd,

모모And who is worthy of all honour,Saw Beauty his superior deem'd

모모By folks who loved to gaze upon her;At this he was most sorely vex'd.

모모Then came Sir Breath (long known as fit

모모To represent the cause of wit),

모모Beginning, rudely, I admit,To treat the lady with a text.To this she hearken'd not at all,But hasten'd to his principal:"None are so wise, they say, as you,--Is not the world enough for two?

모모If you are obstinate, good-bye!If wise, to love me you will try,For be assured the world can ne'erGive birth to a more handsome pair."

모모1827.*

모모=====

모모FAIR daughters were by Beauty rear'd,

모모Wit had but dull sons for his lot;So for a season it appear'd

모모Beauty was constant, Wit was not.But Wit's a native of the soil,

모모So he return'd, work'd, strove amain,And found--sweet guerdon for his toil!--

모모Beauty to quicken him again.

모모1827.*

모모III.

모모RAIN AND RAINBOW.

모모DURING a heavy storm it chancedThat from his room a cockney glancedAt the fierce tempest as it broke,While to his neighbour thus he spoke:"The thunder has our awe inspired,Our barns by lightning have been fired,--Our sins to punish, I suppose;But in return, to soothe our woes,See how the rain in torrents fell,Making the harvest promise well!But is't a rainbow that I spyExtending o'er the dark-grey sky?With it I'm sure we may dispense,The colour'd cheat! The vain pretence!"Dame Iris straightway thus replied:"Dost dare my beauty to deride?In realms of space God station'd meA type of better worlds to beTo eyes that from life's sorrows roveIn cheerful hope to Heav'n above,And, through the mists that hover hereGod and his precepts blest revere.Do thou, then, grovel like the swine,And to the ground thy snout confine,But suffer the enlighten'd eyeTo feast upon my majesty."

모모1827.*

모모VALEDICTION.

모모I ONCE was fond of fools,

모모And bid them come each day;Then each one brought his tools

모모The carpenter to play;The roof to strip first choosing,

모모Another to supply,The wood as trestles using,

모모To move it by-and-by,While here and there they ran,

모모And knock'd against each other;To fret I soon began,

모모My anger could not smother,So cried, "Get out, ye fools!"

모모At this they were offendedThen each one took his tools,

모모And so our friendship ended.

모모Since that, I've wiser been,

모모And sit beside my door;When one of them is seen,

모모I cry, "Appear no more!""Hence, stupid knave!" I bellow:

모모At this he's angry too:"You impudent old fellow!

모모And pray, sir, who are you?Along the streets we riot,

모모And revel at the fair;But yet we're pretty quiet,

모모And folks revile us ne'er.Don't call us names, then, please!"--At length I meet with ease,

모모For now they leave my door--'Tis better than before!

모모1827.*-----THE COUNTRY SCHOOLMASTER.

모모I.

모모A MASTER of a country schoolJump'd up one day from off his stool,Inspired with firm resolve to tryTo gain the best society;So to the nearest baths he walk'd,And into the saloon he stalk'd.He felt quite. startled at the door,Ne'er having seen the like before.To the first stranger made he nowA very low and graceful bow,But quite forgot to bear in mindThat people also stood behind;His left-hand neighbor's paunch he struckA grievous blow, by great ill luck;Pardon for this he first entreated,And then in haste his bow repeated.His right hand neighbor next he hit,And begg'd him, too, to pardon it;But on his granting his petition,Another was in like condition;These compliments he paid to all,Behind, before, across the hall;At length one who could stand no more,Show'd him impatiently the door.

모모*      *      *      *

모모May many, pond'ring on their crimes,A moral draw from this betimes!

모모II.

모모As he proceeded on his wayHe thought, "I was too weak to-day;To bow I'll ne'er again be seen;For goats will swallow what is green."Across the fields he now must speed,Not over stumps and stones, indeed,But over meads and cornfields sweet,Trampling down all with clumsy feet.A farmer met him by-and-by,And didn't ask him: how? or why?But with his fist saluted him.

모모"I feel new life in every limb!"Our traveller cried in ecstasy."Who art thou who thus gladden'st me?May Heaven such blessings ever send!Ne'er may I want a jovial friend!"

모모1808.*-----THE LEGEND OF THE HORSESHOE.

모모WHAT time our Lord still walk'd the earth,Unknown, despised, of humble birth,And on Him many a youth attended(His words they seldom comprehended),It ever seem'd to Him most meetTo hold His court in open street,As under heaven's broad canopyOne speaks with greater liberty.The teachings of His blessed wordFrom out His holy mouth were heard;Each market to a fane turn'd HeWith parable and simile.

모모One day, as tow'rd a town He roved,In peace of mind with those He loved,Upon the path a something gleam'd;A broken horseshoe 'twas, it seem'd.So to St. Peter thus He spake:"That piece of iron prythee take!"St. Peter's thoughts had gone astray,--He had been musing on his wayRespecting the world's government,A dream that always gives content,For in the head 'tis check'd by nought;This ever was his dearest thought,For him this prize was far too meanHad it a crown and sceptre been!But, surely, 'twasn't worth the troubleFor half a horseshoe to bend double!And so he turn'd away his head,As if he heard not what was said,

모모The Lord, forbearing tow'rd all men,Himself pick'd up the horseshoe then(He ne'er again like this stoop'd down).And when at length they reach'd the town,Before a smithy He remain'd,And there a penny for 't obtain'd.As they the market-place went by,Some beauteous cherries caught His eye:Accordingly He bought as manyAs could be purchased for a penny,And then, as oft His wont had been,Placed them within His sleeve unseen.

모모They went out by another gate,O'er plains and fields proceeding straight,No house or tree was near the spot,The sun was bright, the day was hot;In short, the weather being such,A draught of water was worth much.The Lord walk'd on before them all,And let, unseen, a cherry fall.St. Peter rush'd to seize it hold,As though an apple 'twere of gold;His palate much approv'd the berry;The Lord ere long another cherryOnce more let fall upon the plain;St. Peter forthwith stoop'd again.The Lord kept making him thus bendTo pick up cherries without end.For a long time the thing went on;The Lord then said, in cheerful tone:"Had'st thou but moved when thou wert bid,Thou of this trouble had'st been rid;The man who small things scorns, will next,By things still smaller be perplex'd."

모모1797.-----A SYMBOL.

모모(This fine poem is given by Goethe amongst a small collection ofwhat he calls Loge (Lodge), meaning thereby Masonic pieces.)

모모THE mason's trade                Observe them well,

모모Resembles life,                  And watch them revealing

모모With all its strife,--           How solemn feelingIs like the stir made            And wonderment swell

모모By man on earth's face.         The hearts of the brave.

모모Though weal and woe               The voice of the blest,

모모The future may hide,              And of spirits on high

모모Unterrified                       Seems loudly to cry:We onward go                      "To do what is best,

모모In ne'er changing race.           Unceasing endeavour!

모모A veil of dread                   "In silence eterne

모모Hangs heavier still.              Here chaplets are twin'd,

모모Deep slumbers fill                That each noble mindThe stars over-head,              Its guerdon may earn.--

모모And the foot-trodden grave.       Then hope ye for ever!"

모모1827.*-----

모모ART.

모모-----Artist, fashion! talk not long!Be a breath thine only song!-----THE DROPS OF NECTAR.

모모WHEN Minerva, to give pleasureTo Prometheus, her well-loved one,Brought a brimming bowl of nectarFrom the glorious realms of heavenAs a blessing for his creatures,And to pour into their bosomsImpulses for arts ennobling,She with rapid footstep hasten'd,Fearing Jupiter might see her,And the golden goblet trembled,And there fell a few drops from itOn the verdant plain beneath her.Then the busy bees flew thitherStraightway, eagerly to drink them,And the butterfly came quicklyThat he, too, might find a drop there;Even the misshapen spiderThither crawl'd and suck'd with vigour.

모모To a happy end they tasted,They, and other gentle insects!For with mortals now divide theyArt캆hat noblest gift of all.

모모1789.*-----THE WANDERER.

모모[Published in the Gottingen Musen Almanach, having been written"to express his feelings and caprices" after his separation fromFrederica.]

모모WANDERER.

모모YOUNG woman, may God bless thee,Thee, and the sucking infantUpon thy breast!Let me, 'gainst this rocky wall,Neath the elm-tree's shadow,Lay aside my burden,Near thee take my rest.

모모WOMAN.

모모What vocation leads thee,While the day is burning,Up this dusty path?Bring'st thou goods from out the townRound the country?Smil'st thou, stranger,At my question?

모모WANDERER.

모모From the town no goods I bring.Cool is now the evening;Show to me the fountain'Whence thou drinkest,Woman young and kind!

모모WOMAN.

모모Up the rocky pathway mount;Go thou first! Across the thicketLeads the pathway tow'rd the cottageThat I live in,To the fountainWhence I drink.

모모WANDERER.

모모Signs of man's arranging handSee I 'mid the trees!Not by thee these stones were join'd,Nature, who so freely scatterest!

모모WOMAN.

모모Up, still up!

모모WANDERER.

모모Lo, a mossy architrave is here!I discern thee, fashioning spirit!On the stone thou hast impress'd thy seal.

모모WOMAN.

모모Onward, stranger!

모모WANDERER.

모모Over an inscription am I treading!'Tis effaced!Ye are seen no longer,Words so deeply graven,Who your master's true devotionShould have shown to thousand grandsons!

모모WOMAN.

모모At these stones, whyStart'st thou, stranger?Many stones are lying yonderRound my cottage.

모모WANDERER.

모모Yonder?

모모WOMAN.

모모Through the thicket,Turning to the left,Here!

모모WANDERER.

모모Ye Muses and ye Graces!

모모WOMAN.

모모This, then, is my cottage.

모모WANDERER.

모모'Tis a ruin'd temple! *

모모WOMAN.

모모Just below it, see,Springs the fountainWhence I drink.

모모WANDERER.

모모Thou dost hoverO'er thy grave, all glowing,Genius! while upon theeHath thy master-pieceFallen crumbling,Thou Immortal One!

모모WOMAN.

모모Stay, a cup I'll fetch theeWhence to drink.

모모WANDERER.

모모Ivy circles thy slenderForm so graceful and godlike.How ye rise on highFrom the ruins,Column-pairAnd thou, their lonely sister yonder,--How thou,Dusky moss upon thy sacred head,--Lookest down in mournful majestyOn thy brethren's figuresLying scatter'dAt thy feet!In the shadow of the brambleEarth and rubbish veil them,Lofty grass is waving o'er themIs it thus thou, Nature, prizestThy great masterpiece's masterpiece?Carelessly destroyest thouThine own sanctuary,Sowing thistles there?

모모WOMAN.

모모How the infant sleeps!Wilt thou rest thee in the cottage,Stranger? Wouldst thou ratherIn the open air still linger?Now 'tis cool! take thou the childWhile I go and draw some water.Sleep on, darling! sleep!

모모WANDERER.

모모Sweet is thy repose!How, with heaven-born health imbued,Peacefully he slumbers!Oh thou, born among the ruinsSpread by great antiquity,On thee rest her spirit!He whom it encirclesWill, in godlike consciousness,Ev'ry day enjoy.Full, of germ, unfold,As the smiling springtime'sFairest charm,Outshining all thy fellows!And when the blossom's husk is faded,May the full fruit shoot forthFrom out thy breast,And ripen in the sunshine!

모모WOMAN.

모모God bless him!--Is he sleeping still?To the fresh draught I nought can add,Saving a crust of bread for thee to eat.

모모WANDERER.

모모I thank thee well.How fair the verdure all around!How green!

모모WOMAN.

모모My husband soonWill home returnFrom labour. Tarry, tarry, man,And with us eat our evening meal.

모모WANDERER.

모모Is't here ye dwell?

모모WOMAN.

모모Yonder, within those walls we live.My father 'twas who built the cottageOf tiles and stones from out the ruins.'Tis here we dwell.He gave me to a husbandman,And in our arms expired.--Hast thou been sleeping, dearest heartHow lively, and how full of play!Sweet rogue!

모모WANDERER.

모모Nature, thou ever budding one,Thou formest each for life's enjoyments,And, like a mother, all thy children dear,Blessest with that sweet heritage,--a homeThe swallow builds the cornice round,Unconscious of the beautiesShe plasters up.The caterpillar spins around the bough,To make her brood a winter house;And thou dost patch, between antiquity'sMost glorious relics,For thy mean use,Oh man, a humble cot,--Enjoyest e'en mid tombs!--Farewell, thou happy woman!

모모WOMAN.

모모Thou wilt not stay, then?

모모WANDERER.

모모May God preserve thee,And bless thy boy!

모모WOMAN.

모모A happy journey!

모모WANDERER.

모모Whither conducts the pathAcross yon hill?

모모WOMAN.

모모To Cuma.

모모WANDERER.

모모How far from hence?

모모WOMAN.

모모'Tis full three miles.

모모WANDERER.

모모Farewell!Oh Nature, guide me on my way!The wandering stranger guide,Who o'er the tombsOf holy bygone timesIs passing,To a kind sheltering place,From North winds safe,And where a poplar groveShuts out the noontide ray!And when I comeHome to my cotAt evening,Illumined by the setting sun,Let me embrace a wife like this,Her infant in her arms!

모모1772.* Compare with the beautiful description contained in thesubsequent lines, an account of a ruined temple of Ceres, givenby Chamberlayne in his Pharonnida (published in 1659)

모모".... With mournful majesiyA heap of solitary ruins lie,Half sepulchred in dust, the bankrupt heirTo prodigal antiquity...."-----LOVE AS A LANDSCAPE PAINTER.

모모ON a rocky peak once sat I early,Gazing on the mist with eyes unmoving;Stretch'd out like a pall of greyish texture,All things round, and all above it cover'd.

모모Suddenly a boy appear'd beside me,Saying "Friend, what meanest thou by gazingOn the vacant pall with such composure?Hast thou lost for evermore all pleasureBoth in painting cunningly, and forming?"On the child I gazed, and thought in secret:"Would the boy pretend to be a master?"

모모"Wouldst thou be for ever dull and idle,"Said the boy, "no wisdom thou'lt attain to;See, I'll straightway paint for thee a figure,--How to paint a beauteous figure, show thee."

모모And he then extended his fore-finger,--(Ruddy was it as a youthful rosebud)Tow'rd the broad and far outstretching carpet,And began to draw there with his finger.

모모First on high a radiant sun he painted,Which upon mine eyes with splendour glisten'd,And he made the clouds with golden border,Through the clouds he let the sunbeams enter;Painted then the soft and feathery summitsOf the fresh and quicken'd trees, behind themOne by one with freedom drew the mountains;Underneath he left no lack of water,But the river painted so like Nature,That it seem'd to glitter in the sunbeams,That it seem'd against its banks to murmur.

모모Ah, there blossom'd flowers beside the river,And bright colours gleam'd upon the meadow,Gold, and green, and purple, and enamell'd,All like carbuncles and emeralds seeming!

모모Bright and clear he added then the heavens,And the blue-tinged mountains far and farther,So that I, as though newborn, enrapturedGazed on, now the painter, now the picture.

모모Then spake he: "Although I have convinced theeThat this art I understand full surely,Yet the hardest still is left to show thee."

모모Thereupon he traced, with pointed finger,And with anxious care, upon the forest,At the utmost verge, where the strong sunbeamsFrom the shining ground appear'd reflected,

모모Traced the figure of a lovely maiden,Fair in form, and clad in graceful fashion,Fresh the cheeks beneath her brown locks' ambush,And the cheeks possess'd the selfsame colourAs the finger that had served to paint them.

모모"Oh thou boy!" exclaim'd I then, "what masterIn his school received thee as his pupil,Teaching thee so truthfully and quicklyWisely to begin, and well to finish?"

모모Whilst I still was speaking, lo, a zephyrSoftly rose, and set the tree-tops moving,Curling all the wavelets on the river,And the perfect maiden's veil, too, fill'd it,And to make my wonderment still greater,Soon the maiden set her foot in motion.On she came, approaching tow'rd the stationWhere still sat I with my arch instructor.

모모As now all, yes, all thus moved together,--Flowers, river, trees, the veil,--all moving,--And the gentle foot of that most fair one,Can ye think that on my rock I linger'd,Like a rock, as though fast-chain'd and silent?

모모1788.-----

모모GOD, SOUL, AND WORLD.

모모-----RHYMED DISTICHS.

모모[The Distichs, of which these are given as a specimen, are aboutforty in number.]

모모WHO trusts in God,Fears not His rod.-----THIS truth may be by all believed:Whom God deceives, is well deceived.-----HOW? when? and where?--No answer comes from high;Thou wait'st for the Because, and yet thou ask'st not Why?-----IF the whole is ever to gladden thee,That whole in the smallest thing thou must see.-----WATER its living strength first shows,When obstacles its course oppose.-----TRANSPARENT appears the radiant air,Though steel and stone in its breast it may bear;At length they'll meet with fiery power,And metal and stones on the earth will shower.------WHATE'ER a living flame may surround,No longer is shapeless, or earthly bound.'Tis now invisible, flies from earth,And hastens on high to the place of its birth.

모모1815.*------PROCEMION.

모모IN His blest name, who was His own creation,Who from all time makes making his vocation;The name of Him who makes our faith so bright,Love, confidence, activity, and might;In that One's name, who, named though oft He be,Unknown is ever in Reality:As far as ear can reach, or eyesight dim,Thou findest but the known resembling Him;How high so'er thy fiery spirit hovers,Its simile and type it straight discoversOnward thou'rt drawn, with feelings light and gay,Where'er thou goest, smiling is the way;No more thou numbrest, reckonest no time,Each step is infinite, each step sublime.

모모1816.-----WHAT God would outwardly alone control,And on his finger whirl the mighty Whole?He loves the inner world to move, to viewNature in Him, Himself in Nature too,So that what in Him works, and is, and lives,The measure of His strength, His spirit gives.

모모1816.-----WITHIN us all a universe doth dwell;And hence each people's usage laudable,That ev'ry one the Best that meets his eyesAs God, yea e'en his God, doth recognise;To Him both earth and heaven surrenders he,Fears Him, and loves Him too, if that may be.

모모1816.-----THE METAMORPHOSIS OF PLANTS.

모모THOU art confused, my beloved, at, seeing the thousandfold union

모모Shown in this flowery troop, over the garden dispers'd;any a name dost thou hear assign'd; one after another

모모Falls on thy list'ning ear, with a barbarian sound.None resembleth another, yet all their forms have a likeness;

모모Therefore, a mystical law is by the chorus proclaim'd;Yes, a sacred enigma! Oh, dearest friend, could I only

모모Happily teach thee the word, which may the mystery solve!Closely observe how the plant, by little and little progressing,

모모Step by step guided on, changeth to blossom and fruit!First from the seed it unravels itself, as soon as the silent

모모Fruit-bearing womb of the earth kindly allows Its escape,And to the charms of the light, the holy, the ever-in-motion,

모모Trusteth the delicate leaves, feebly beginning to shoot.Simply slumber'd the force in the seed; a germ of the future,

모모Peacefully lock'd in itself, 'neath the integument lay,Leaf and root, and bud, still void of colour, and shapeless;

모모Thus doth the kernel, while dry, cover that motionless life.Upward then strives it to swell, in gentle moisture confiding,

모모And, from the night where it dwelt, straightway ascendeth to light.Yet still simple remaineth its figure, when first it appeareth;

모모And 'tis a token like this, points out the child 'mid the plants.Soon a shoot, succeeding it, riseth on high, and reneweth,

모모Piling-up node upon node, ever the primitive form;Yet not ever alike: for the following leaf, as thou seest,

모모Ever produceth itself, fashioned in manifold ways.Longer, more indented, in points and in parts more divided,

모모Which. all-deform'd until now, slept in the organ below,So at length it attaineth the noble and destined perfection,

모모Which, in full many a tribe, fills thee with wondering awe.Many ribb'd and tooth'd, on a surface juicy and swelling,

모모Free and unending the shoot seemeth in fullness to be;Yet here Nature restraineth, with powerful hands, the formation,

모모And to a perfecter end, guideth with softness its growth,Less abundantly yielding the sap, contracting the vessels,

모모So that the figure ere long gentler effects doth disclose.Soon and in silence is check'd the growth of the vigorous branches,

모모And the rib of the stalk fuller becometh in form.Leafless, however, and quick the tenderer stem then up-springeth,

모모And a miraculous sight doth the observer enchant.Ranged in a circle, in numbers that now are small, and now countless,

모모Gather the smaller-sized leaves, close by the side of their like.Round the axis compress'd the sheltering calyx unfoldeth,

모모And, as the perfectest type, brilliant-hued coronals forms.Thus doth Nature bloom, in glory still nobler and fuller,

모모Showing, in order arranged, member on member uprear'd.Wonderment fresh dost thou feel, as soon as the stem rears the flower

모모Over the scaffolding frail of the alternating leaves.But this glory is only the new creation's foreteller,

모모Yes, the leaf with its hues feeleth the hand all divine,And on a sudden contracteth itself; the tenderest figures

모모Twofold as yet, hasten on, destined to blend into one.Lovingly now the beauteous pairs are standing together,

모모Gather'd in countless array, there where the altar is raised.Hymen hovereth o'er them, and scents delicious and mighty

모모Stream forth their fragrance so sweet, all things enliv'ning around.Presently, parcell'd out, unnumber'd germs are seen swelling,

모모Sweetly conceald in the womb, where is made perfect the fruit.Here doth Nature close the ring of her forces eternal;

모모Yet doth a new one, at once, cling to the one gone before,So that the chain be prolonged for ever through all generations,

모모And that the whole may have life, e'en as enjoy'd by each part.Now, my beloved one, turn thy gaze on the many-hued thousands

모모Which, confusing no more, gladden the mind as they wave.Every plant unto thee proclaimeth the laws everlasting,

모모Every flowered speaks louder and louder to thee;But if thou here canst decipher the mystic words of the goddess,

모모Everywhere will they be seen, e'en though the features are changed.Creeping insects may linger, the eager butterfly hasten,--

모모Plastic and forming, may man change e'en the figure decreed!Oh, then, bethink thee, as well, how out of the germ of acquaintance,

모모Kindly intercourse sprang, slowly unfolding its leaves;Soon how friendship with might unveil'd itself in our bosoms,

모모And how Amor, at length, brought forth blossom and fruitThink of the manifold ways wherein Nature hath lent to our feelings,

모모Silently giving them birth, either the first or the last!Yes, and rejoice in the present day! For love that is holy

모모Seeketh the noblest of fruits,--that where the thoughts are the same,Where the opinions agree,--that the pair may, in rapt contemplation,

모모Lovingly blend into one,--find the more excellent world.

모모1797.-----

모모PROVERBS.

모모-----'TIS easier far a wreath to bind,Than a good owner fort to find.-----I KILL'D a thousand flies overnight,Yet was waken'd by one, as soon as twas light.-----To the mother I give;For the daughter I live.-----A BREACH is every day,

모모By many a mortal storm'd;Let them fall in the gaps as they may,

모모Yet a heap of dead is ne'er form'd.-----WHAT harm has thy poor mirror done, alas?Look not so ugly, prythee, in the glass!

모모1815.*-----TAME XENIA.

모모THE Epigrams bearing the title of XENIA were written by Goetheand Schiller together, having been first occasioned by someviolent attacks made on them by some insignificant writers. Theyare extremely numerous, but scarcely any of them could betranslated into English. Those here given are merely presented asa specimen.

모모GOD gave to mortals birth,

모모In his own image too;Then came Himself to earth,

모모A mortal kind and true.
