For well did the good youth deserve it,Who, when departing, presented the ring, but never return'd home.All was by him foreseen, when freedom's love of a sudden,And a desire to play his part in the new-found Existence,Drove him to go to Paris, where prison and death were his portion.'Farewell,' said he, 'I go; for all things on earth are in motionAt this moment, and all things appear in a state of disunion.Fundamental laws in the steadiest countries are loosen'd,And possessions are parted from those who used to possess them,Friends are parted from friends, and love is parted from love too.I now leave you here, and whether I ever shall see youHere again,--who can tell? Perchance these words will our last be.Man is a stranger here upon earth, the proverb informs us;Every person has now become more a stranger than ever.Ours the soil is no longer; our treasures are fast flying from us;All the sacred old vessels of gold and silver are melted,All is moving, as though the old-fashion'd world would roll backwardsInto chaos and night, in order anew to be fashion'd.You of my heart have possession, and if we shall ever here-afterMeet again over the wreck of the world, it will be as new creatures,All remodell'd and free and independent of fortune;For what fetters can bind down those who survive such a period!But if we are destined not to escape from these dangers,If we are never again to embrace each other with rapturesO then fondly keep in your thoughts my hovering image,That you may be prepared with like courage for good and ill fortune!If a new home or a new alliance should chance to allure you,Then enjoy with thanks whatever your destiny offers,Purely loving the loving, and grateful to him who thus loves you.But remember always to tread with a circumspect footstep,For the fresh pangs of a second loss will behind you be lurking.Deem each day as sacred; but value not life any higherThan any other possession, for all possessions are fleeting.'Thus he spoke; and the noble youth and I parted for ever:Meanwhile I ev'rything lost, and a thousand times thought of his warning.Once more I think of his words, now that love is sweetly preparingHappiness for me anew, and the brightest of hopes is unfolding.Pardon me, dearest friend, for trembling e'en at the momentWhen I am clasping your arm! For thus, on first landing, the sailorFancies that even the solid ground is shaking beneath him."

모모Thus she spoke, and she placed the rings by the side of each other.But the bridegroom answer'd, with noble and manly emotion"All the firmer, amidst the universal disruption,Be, Dorothea, our union! We'll show ourselves bold and enduring,Firmly hold our own, and firmly retain our possessions.For the man who in wav'ring times is inclined to be wav'ringOnly increases the evil, and spreads it wider and wider;But the man of firm decision the universe fashions.'Tis not becoming the Germans to further this fearful commotion,And in addition to waver uncertainly hither and thither.'This is our own!' we ought to say, and so to maintain it!For the world will ever applaud those resolute nationsWho for God and the Law, their wives, and parents, and childrenStruggle, and fall when contending against the foeman together.You are mine; and now what is mine, is mine more than ever.Not with anxiety will I preserve it, or timidly use it,But with courage and strength. And if the enemy threatenNow or hereafter, I'll hold myself ready, and reach down my weapons.If I know that the house and my parents by you are protected,I shall expose my breast to the enemy, void of all terror;And if all others thought thus, then might against might should be measured,And in the early prospect of peace we should all be rejoicing."

모모1796?.-----WEST-EASTERN DIVAN.-----Who the song would understand,Needs must seek the song's own land.Who the minstrel understand,Needs must seek the minstrel's land.-----

모모THE Poems comprised in this collection are written in thePersian style, and are greatly admired by Oriental scholars, forthe truthfulness with which the Eastern spirit of poetry isreproduced by the Western minstrel. They were chiefly composedbetween the years 1814 and 1819, and first given to the world inthe latter year. Of the twelve books into which they are divided,that of Suleika will probably be considered the best, from themany graceful love-songs which it contains. The following isHanoi's account of the Divan, and may well serve as a substitutefor anything I could say respecting it:--

모모It contains opinions and sentiments on the East, expressed in aseries of rich cantos and stanzas full of sweetness and spirit,and all this as enchanting as a harem emitting the most deliciousand rare perfumes, and blooming with exquisitely-lovely nymphswith eyebrows painted black, eyes piercing as those of theantelope, arms white as alabaster, and of the most graceful andperfectly-formed shapes, while the heart of the reader beats andgrows faint, as did that of the happy Gaspard Debaran, the clown,who, when on the highest step of his ladder, was enabled to peepinto the Seraglio of Constantinople--that recess concealed fromthe inspection of man. Sometimes also the reader may imaginehimself indolently stretched on a carpet of Persian softness,luxuriously smoking the yellow tobacco of Turkistan through along tube of jessamine and amber, while a black slave fans himwith a fan of peacock's feathers, and a little boy presents himwith a cup of genuine Mocha. Goethe has put these enchanting andvoluptuous customs into poetry, and his verses are so perfect, soharmonious, so tasteful, so soft, that it seems really surprisingthat he should ever have been able to have brought the Germanlanguage to this state of suppleness. The charm of the book isinexplicable; it is a votive nosegay sent from the West to theEast, composed of the most precious and curious plants: redroses, hortensias like the breast of a spotless maiden, purpledigitalis like the long finger of a man, fantastically formedranunculi, and in the midst of all, silent and tastefullyconcealed, a tuft of German violets. This nosegay signifies thatthe West is tired of thin and icy-cold spirituality, and seekswarmth in the strong and healthy bosom of the East."

모모Translations are here given of upwards of sixty of the bestPoems embraced in the Divan, the number in the original exceedingtwo hundred.-----I.  MORGAGNI NAME.

모모BOOK OF THE MINSTREL.

모모TALISMANS.

모모GOD is of the east possess'd,God is ruler of the west;North and south alike, each landRests within His gentle hand.-----HE, the only righteous one,Wills that right to each be done.'Mongst His hundred titles, then,Highest praised be this!--Amen.-----ERROR seeketh to deceive me,Thou art able to retrieve me;Both in action and in songKeep my course from going wrong.

모모1819.*-----THE FOUR FAVOURS.

모모THAT Arabs through the realms of space

모모May wander on, light-hearted,Great Allah hath, to all their race,

모모Four favours meet imparted.

모모The turban first--that ornament

모모All regal crowns excelling;A light and ever-shifting tent,

모모Wherein to make our dwelling;

모모A sword, which, more than rocks and walls

모모Doth shield us, brightly glistening;A song that profits and enthrall,

모모For which the maids are list'ning!

모모1814.-----DISCORD.

모모WHEN by the brook his strain

모모Cupid is fluting,And on the neighboring plain

모모Mayors disputing,There turns the ear ere long,

모모Loving and tender,Yet to the noise a song

모모Soon must surrender.Loud then the flute-notes glad

모모Sound 'mid war's thunder;If I grow raving mad,

모모Is it a wonder?Flutes sing and trumpets bray,

모모Waxing yet stronger;If, then, my senses stray,

모모Wonder no longer.

모모1814.-----SONG AND STRUCTURE.

모모LET the Greek his plastic clay

모모Mould in human fashion,While his own creation may

모모Wake his glowing passion;

모모But it is our joy to court

모모Great Euphrates' torrent,Here and there at will to sport

모모In the Wat'ry current.

모모Quench'd I thus my spirit's flame,

모모Songs had soon resounded;Water drawn by bards whose fame

모모Pure is, may be rounded.+

모모1819.*(+ This oriental belief in the power of the pure to roll-up water into acrystal hail is made the foundation of the Interesting Pariah Legend,that will be found elsewhere amongst the Ballads.)-----II.  HAFIS NAME.

모모BOOK OF HAFIS.

모모SPIRIT let us bridegroom call,

모모And the word the bride;Known this wedding is to all

모모Who have Hafis tried.

모모THE UNLIMITED.

모모THAT thou can't never end, doth make thee great,And that thou ne'er beginnest, is thy fate.Thy song is changeful as yon starry frame,End and beginning evermore the same;And what the middle bringeth, but containsWhat was at first, and what at last remains.Thou art of joy the true and minstrel-source,From thee pours wave on wave with ceaseless force.A mouth that's aye prepared to kiss,

모모A breast whence flows a loving song,A throat that finds no draught amiss,

모모An open heart that knows no wrong.

모모And what though all the world should sink!

모모Hafis, with thee, alone with thee

모모Will I contend! joy, misery,

모모The portion of us twain shall be;Like thee to love, like thee to drink,--

모모This be my pride,--this, life to me!

모모Now, Song, with thine own fire be sung,--For thou art older, thou more young!

모모1817.*-----TO HAFIS.

모모HAFIS, straight to equal thee,

모모One would strive in vain;Though a ship with majesty

모모Cleaves the foaming main,Feels its sails swell haughtily

모모As it onward hiesCrush'd by ocean's stern decree,

모모Wrecked it straightway lies.Tow'rd thee, songs, light, graceful, free,

모모Mount with cooling gush;Then their glow consumeth me,

모모As like fire they rush.Yet a thought with ecstasy

모모Hath my courage moved;In the land of melody

모모I have lived and loved.

모모1815.-----III. USCHK NAME.

모모BOOK OF LOVE.

모모THE TYPES.

모모LIST, and in memory bearThese six fond loving pair.Love, when aroused, kept trueRustan and Rad!Strangers approach from farJoseph and Suleika;Love, void of hope, is inFerhad and Schirin.Born for each other areMedschnun and Lily;Loving, though old and grey,Dschemil saw Boteinah.Love's sweet caprice anon,Brown maid + and Solomon!If thou dost mark them well,Stronger thy love will swell.

모모1817.*(+ Brown maid is the Queen of Sheba.)-----ONE PAIR MORE.

모모LOVE is indeed a glorious prize!What fairer guerdon meets our eyes?--Though neither wealth nor power are thine,A very hero thou dost shine.As of the prophet, they will tell,Wamik and Asia's tale as well.--They'll tell not of them,--they'll but giveTheir names, which now are all that live.The deeds they did, the toils they provedNo mortal knows! But that they lovedThis know we. Here's the story trueOf Wamik and of Asia too.

모모1827.*-----LOVE's torments sought a place of rest,

모모Where all might drear and lonely be;They found ere long my desert breast,

모모And nestled in its vacancy.

모모1827.*-----IV. TEFKIR NAME.

모모BOOK OF CONTEMPLATION.

모모FIVE THINGS.

모모WHAT makes time short to me?

모모Activity!What makes it long and spiritless?

모모'Tis idleness!What brings us to debt?

모모To delay and forget!What makes us succeed?

모모Decision with speedHow to fame to ascend?

모모Oneself to defend!

모모1814-----FOR woman due allowance make!

모모Form'd of a crooked rib was she,--

모모By Heaven she could not straightened be.Attempt to bend her, and she'll break;If left alone, more crooked grows madam;What well could be worse, my good friend, Adam?--For woman due allowance make;'Twere grievous, if thy rib should break!

모모1819.*-----FIRDUSI (Speaks).

모모OH world, with what baseness and guilt thou art rife!

모모Thou nurtures, trainest, and illest the while.

모모He only whom Allah doth bless with his smileIs train'd and is nurtured with riches and life.

모모1819.*-----SULEIKA (Speaks).

모모THE mirror tells me, I am fair!

모모Thou sayest, to grow old my fate will be.Nought in God's presence changeth e'er,--

모모Love him, for this one moment, then, in me.

모모1819.*-----V. RENDSCH NAME

모모BOOK OF GLOOM.

모모IT is a fault oneself to praise,

모모And yet 'tis done by each whose deeds are kind;And if there's no deceit in what he says,

모모The good we still as good shall find.

모모Let, then, ye fools, that wise man taste

모모Of joy, who fancies that he s wise,That he, a fool like you, may waste

모모Th' insipid thanks the world supplies.

모모1816.-----VI. HIKMET NAME.

모모BOOK OF PROVERBS.

모모CALL on the present day and night for nought,Save what by yesterday was brought.-----THE sea is flowing ever,The land retains it never.-----BE stirring, man, while yet the day is clear;The night when none can work fast Draweth near.-----WHEN the heavy-laden sigh,Deeming help and hope gone by,Oft, with healing power is heard,Comfort-fraught, a kindly word.-----How vast is mine inheritance, how glorious and sublime!For time mine own possession is, the land I till is time!-----UNWARY saith,--ne'er lived a man more true;The deepest heart, the highest head he knew,--"In ev'ry place and time thou'lt find availingUprightness, judgment, kindliness unfailing."-----THOUGH the bards whom the Orient sun bath bless'dAre greater than we who dwell in the west,Yet in hatred of those whom our equals we find.In this we're not in the least behind.-----

모모WOULD we let our envy burst,

모모Feed its hunger fully first!To keep our proper place,

모모We'll show our bristles more;With hawks men all things chase,

모모Except the savage boar.-----BY those who themselves more bravely have foughtA hero's praise will be joyfully told.The worth of man can only be taughtBy those who have suffer'd both heat and cold.-----"WHEREFORE is truth so far from our eyes,Buried as though in a distant land?"None at the proper moment are wise!

모모Could they properly understand,

모모Truth would appear in her own sweet guise,Beauteous, gentle, and close at hand.-----WHY these inquiries make,

모모Where charity may flow?Cast in the flood thy cake,--

모모Its eater, who will know?-----ONCE when I a spider had kill'd,

모모Then methought: wast right or wrong?

모모That we both to these times should belong,This had God in His goodness willed.-----MOTLEY this congregation is, for, lo!At the communion kneel both friend and foe.-----IF the country I'm to show,Thou must on the housetop go.-----A MAN with households twain

모모Ne'er finds attention meet,A house wherein two women reign

모모Is ne'er kept clean and neat.-----BLESS, thou dread Creator,

모모Bless this humble fane;Man may build them greater,--

모모More they'll not contain.-----LET this house's glory rise,

모모Handed to far ages down,

모모And the son his honour prize.As the father his renown.-----O'ER the Mediterranean sea

모모Proudly hath the Orient sprung;Who loves Hafis and knows him, he

모모Knows what Caldron hath sung.-----IF the ass that bore the Saviour

모모Were to Mecca driven, he

모모Would not alter, but would beStill an ass in his behavior.-----THE flood of passion storms with fruitless strife

모모'Gainst the unvanquished solid land.--

모모It throws poetic pearls upon the strand,And thus is gain'd the prize of life.-----WHEN so many minstrels there are,

모모How it pains me, alas, to know it!Who from the earth drives poetry far?

모모Who but the poet!-----VII.  TIMUR NAME.

모모BOOK OF TIMUR.

모모THE WINTER AND TIMUR.

모모So the winter now closed round themWith resistless fury. ScatteringOver all his breath so icy,He inflamed each wind that blitheTo assail them angrily.Over them he gave dominionTo his frost-unsharpened tempests;Down to Timur's council went he,And with threat'ning voice address'd him:--"Softly, slowly, wretched being!Live, the tyrant of injustice;But shall hearts be scorch'd much longerBy thy flames,--consume before them?If amongst the evil spiritsThou art one,--good! I'm another.Thou a greybeard art--so I am;Land and men we make to stiffen.Thou art Mars! And I Saturnus,--Both are evil-working planets,When united, horror-fraught.Thou dost kill the soul, thou freezesE'en the atmosphere; still colderIs my breath than thine was ever.Thy wild armies vex the faithfulWith a thousand varying torments;Well! God grant that I discoverEven worse, before I perish!And by God, I'll give thee none.Let God hear what now I tell thee!Yes, by God! from Death's cold clutchesNought, O greybeard, shall protect thee,Not the hearth's broad coalfire's ardour,Not December's brightest flame."

모모1814.-----TO SULEIKA.

모모FITTING perfumes to prepare,

모모And to raise thy rapture high,Must a thousand rosebuds fair

모모First in fiery torments die.

모모One small flask's contents to glean,

모모Whose sweet fragrance aye may live,Slender as thy finger e'en,

모모Must a world its treasures give;

모모Yes, a world where life is moving,

모모Which, with impulse full and strong,Could forbode the Bulbul's loving,

모모Sweet, and spirit-stirring song.

모모Since they thus have swell'd our joy,

모모Should such torments grieve us, then?Doth not Timur's rule destroy

모모Myriad souls of living men?

모모1815.*-----VIII. SULEIKA NAME.

모모BOOK OF SULEIKA.

모모ONCE, methought, in the night hours cold,

모모That I saw the moon in my sleep;But as soon as I waken'd, behold

모모Unawares rose the sun from the deep.

모모THAT Suleika's love was so strong

모모For Joseph, need cause no surprise;

모모He was young, youth pleaseth the eyes,--

모모He was fair, they say, beyond measure

모모Fair was she, and so great was their pleasure.But that thou, who awaitedst me long,Youthful glances of fire dost throw me,Soon wilt bless me, thy love now dost show me,This shall my joyous numbers proclaim,Thee I for ever Suleika shall name.

모모1815.-----HATEM.

모모NOT occasion makes the thief;

모모She's the greatest of the whole;For Love's relics, to my grief,

모모From my aching heart she stole.

모모She hath given it to thee,--

모모All the joy my life had known,So that, in my poverty,

모모Life I seek from thee alone.

모모Yet compassion greets me straight

모모In the lustre of thine eye,And I bless my newborn fate,

모모As within thine arms I lie.

모모1815.-----SULEIKA.

모모THE sun appears! A glorious sight!

모모The crescent-moon clings round him now.What could this wondrous pair unite?

모모How to explain this riddle? How?

모모HATEM.

모모May this our joy's foreboder prove!

모모In it I view myself and thee;Thou calmest me thy sun, my love,--

모모Come, my sweet moon, cling thou round me!

모모1815.-----LOVE for love, and moments sweet,

모모Lips returning kiss for kiss,Word for word, and eyes that meet;

모모Breath for breath, and bliss for bliss.Thus at eve, and thus the morrow!

모모Yet thou feeblest, at my lay,Ever some half-hidden sorrow;Could I Joseph's graces borrow,

모모All thy beauty I'd repay!

모모1815.-----HATEM.

모모O, SAY, 'neath what celestial sign

모모The day doth lie,When ne'er again this heart of mine

모모Away will fly?And e'en though fled (what thought divine!)

모모Would near me lie?--On the soft couch, on whose sweet shrine

모모My heart near hers will lie!

모모1816.-----HATEM.

모모HOLD me, locks, securely caught

모모In the circle of her face!Dear brown serpents, I have nought

모모To repay this act of grace,

모모Save a heart whose love ne'er dies,

모모Throbbing with aye-youthful glow;For a raging ETA lies

모모'Neath its veil of mist and snow.

모모Yonder mountain's stately brow

모모Thou, like morning beams, dost shame;Once again feels Hatem now

모모Spring's soft breath and summer's flame.

모모One more bumper! Fill the glass;

모모This last cup I pledge to thee!--By mine ashes if she pass,

모모"He consumed," she'll say, "for me."

모모1815.-----THE LOVING ONE SPEAKS.

모모AND wherefore sends notThe horseman-captainHis heralds hither

모모Each day, unfailing?Yet hath he horses,He writes well.

모모He waiteth Tali,And Neski knows heTo write with beautyOn silken tablets.I'd deem him present,Had I his words.

모모The sick One will not,Will not recoverFrom her sweet sorrow;She, when she hearethThat her true loverGrows well, falls sick.

모모1819.*-----THE LOVING ONE AGAIN.

모모WRITES he in Neski,Faithfully speaks he;Writes he in Tali,Joy to give, seeks he:Writes he in either,Good!--for he loves!

모모1819.*-----THESE tufted branches fair

모모Observe, my loved one, well!And see the fruits they bear

모모In green and prickly shell!

모모They've hung roll'd up, till now,

모모Unconsciously and still;A loosely-waving bough

모모Doth rock them at its will.

모모Yet, ripening from within.

모모The kernel brown swells fast;It seeks the air to win,

모모It seeks the sun at last.

모모With joy it bursts its thrall,

모모The shell must needs give way.'Tis thus my numbers fall

모모Before thy feet, each day.

모모1815.-----SULEIKA.

모모WHAT is by this stir reveal'd?

모모Doth the East glad tidings bring?For my heart's deep wounds are heal'd

모모By his mild and cooling wing.

모모He the dust with sports doth meet,

모모And in gentle cloudlets chase;To the vineleaf's safe retreat

모모Drives the insects' happy race,

모모Cools these burning cheeks of mine,

모모Checks the sun's fierce glow Adam,Kisses, as he flies, the vine,

모모Flaunting over hill and plain.

모모And his whispers soft convey

모모Thousand greetings from my friend;Ere these hills own night's dark sway,

모모Kisses greet me, without end.

모모Thus canst thou still onward go,

모모Serving friend and mourner too!There, where lofty ramparts glow,

모모Soon the loved one shall I view.

모모Ah, what makes the heart's truth known,--

모모Love's sweet breath,--a newborn life,--Learn I from his mouth alone,

모모In his breath alone is rife!

모모1815.-----THE SUBLIME TYPE.

모모THE sun, whom Grecians Helms call,

모모His heavenly path with pride doth tread,And, to subdue the world's wide all,

모모Looks round, beneath him, high o'er head.

모모He sees the fairest goddess pine,

모모Heaven's child, the daughter of the clouds,--For her alone he seems to shine;

모모In trembling grief his form he shrouds,

모모Careless for all the realms of bliss,--

모모Her streaming tears more swiftly flow:For every pearl he gives a kiss,

모모And changeth into joy her woe.

모모She gazeth upward fixedly,

모모And deeply feels his glance of might,While, stamped with his own effigy,

모모Each pearl would range itself aright.

모모Thus wreath'd with bows, with hues thus grac'd,

모모With gladness beams her face so fair,While he, to meet her, maketh haste,

모모And yet, alas! can reach her ne'er.

모모So, by the harsh decree of Fate,

모모Thou modest from me, dearest one;And were I Helms e'en, the Great,

모모What would avail his chariot-throne?

모모1815.-----SULEIKA.

모모ZEPHYR, for thy humid wing,

모모Oh, how much I envy thee!Thou to him canst tidings bring

모모How our parting saddens me!

모모In my breast, a yearning still

모모As thy pinions wave, appears;Flow'rs and eyes, and wood, and hill

모모At thy breath are steeped in tears.

모모Yet thy mild wing gives relief,

모모Soothes the aching eyelid's pain;Ah, I else had died for grief,

모모Him ne'er hoped to see again.

모모To my love, then, quick repair,

모모Whisper softly to his heart;Yet, to give him pain, beware,

모모Nor my bosom's pangs impart.

모모Tell him, but in accents coy,

모모That his love must be my life;Both, with feelings fraught with joy,

모모In his presence will be rife.

모모1815.-----THE REUNION.

모모CAN it be! of stars the star,

모모Do I press thee to my heart?In the night of distance far,

모모What deep gulf, what bitter smart!Yes, 'tis thou, indeed, at last,

모모Of my joys the partner dear!Mindful, though, of sorrows past,

모모I the present needs must fear.

모모When the still-unfashion'd earth

모모Lay on God's eternal breast,He ordain'd its hour of birth,

모모With creative joy possess'd.Then a heavy sigh arose,

모모When He spake the sentence:--"Be!"And the All, with mighty throes,

모모Burst into reality.

모모And when thus was born the light,

모모Darkness near it fear'd to stay,And the elements with might

모모Fled on every side away;Each on some far-distant trace,

모모Each with visions wild employ,Numb, in boundless realm of space,

모모Harmony and feeling-void.

모모Dumb was all, all still and dead,

모모For the first time, God alone!Then He form'd the morning-red,

모모Which soon made its kindness known:It unravelled from the waste,

모모Bright and glowing harmony,And once more with love was grac'd

모모What contended formerly.

모모And with earnest, noble strife,

모모Each its own Peculiar sought;Back to full, unbounded life

모모Sight and feeling soon were brought.Wherefore, if 'tis done, explore

모모How? why give the manner, name?Allah need create no more,

모모We his world ourselves can frame.

모모So, with morning pinions bright,

모모To thy mouth was I impell'd;Stamped with thousand seals by night,

모모Star-clear is the bond fast held.Paragons on earth are we

모모Both of grief and joy sublime,And a second sentence:--"Be!"

모모Parts us not a second time.

모모1815.-----SULEIKA.

모모WITH what inward joy, sweet lay,

모모I thy meaning have descried!Lovingly thou seem'st to say

모모That I'm ever by his side;

모모That he ever thinks of me,

모모That he to the absent givesAll his love's sweet ecstasy,

모모While for him alone she lives.

모모Yes, the mirror which reveals

모모Thee, my loved one, is my breast;This the bosom, where thy seals

모모Endless kisses have impress'd.

모모Numbers sweet, unsullied truth,

모모Chain me down in sympathy!Love's embodied radiant youth,

모모In the garb of poesy!

모모1819.*-----IN thousand forms mayst thou attempt surprise,

모모Yet, all-beloved-one, straight know I thee;Thou mayst with magic veils thy face disguise,

모모And yet, all-present-one, straight know I thee.

모모Upon the cypress' purest, youthful bud,

모모All-beauteous-growing-one, straight know I thee;In the canal's unsullied, living flood,

모모All-captivating-one, well know I thee.

모모When spreads the water-column, rising proud,

모모All-sportive one, how gladly know I thee;When, e'en in forming, is transform'd the cloud,

모모All-figure-changing-one, there know I thee.

모모Veil in the meadow-carpet's flowery charms,

모모All-checkered-starry-fair-one, know I thee;And if a plant extend its thousand arms,

모모O, all-embracing-one, there know I thee.

모모When on the mount is kindled morn's sweet light,

모모Straightway, all-gladdening-one, salute I thee,The arch of heaven o'er head grows pure and bright,--

모모All-heart-expanding-one, then breathe I thee.

모모That which my inward, outward sense proclaims,

모모Thou all-instructing-one, I know through thee;And if I utter Allah's hundred names,

모모A name with each one echoes, meant for thee.

모모1819.*-----IX. SAKE NAME.

모모THE CONVIVIAL BOOK.

모모CAN the Koran from Eternity be?

모모'Tis worth not a thought!Can the Koran a creation, then, be?

모모Of that, I know nought!Yet that the book of all books it must be,

모모I believe, as a Mussulman ought.That from Eternity wine, though, must be,

모모I ever have thought;That 'twas ordain'd, ere the Angels, to be,

모모As a truth may be taught.Drinkers, however these matters may be,

모모Gaze on God's face, fearing nought.

모모1815.-----YE'VE often, for our drunkenness,

모모Blamed us in ev'ry way,And, in abuse of drunkenness,

모모Enough can never say.Men, overcome by drunkenness,

모모Are wont to lie till day;And yet I find my drunkenness

모모All night-time make me stray;For, oh! 'tis Love's sweet drunkenness

모모That maketh me its prey,Which night and day, and day and night,

모모My heart must needs obey,--A heart that, in its drunkenness,

모모Pours forth full many a lay,So that no trifling drunkenness

모모Can dare assert its sway.Love, song, and wine's sweet drunkenness,

모모By night-time and by day,--How god-like is the drunkenness

모모That maketh me its prey!

모모1815.-----X. MATHAL NAME.

모모BOOK OF PARABLES.

모모FROM heaven there fell upon the foaming wave

모모A timid drop; the flood with anger roared,--

모모But God, its modest boldness to reward,Strength to the drop and firm endurance gave.Its form the mussel captive took,

모모And to its lasting glory and renown,

모모The pearl now glistens in our monarch's crown,With gentle gleam and loving look.

모모1819.*-----BULBUL'S song, through night hours cold,

모모Rose to Allah's throne on high;

모모To reward her melody,Giveth he a cage of gold.Such a cage are limbs of men,--

모모Though at first she feels confin'd,

모모Yet when all she brings to mind,Straight the spirit sings again.

모모1819.*-----IN the Koran with strange delightA peacock's feather met my sight:Thou'rt welcome in this holy place,The highest prize on earth's wide face!As in the stars of heaven, in thee,God's greatness in the small we see;For he whose gaze whole worlds bath bless'dHis eye hath even here impress'd,And the light down in beauty dress'd,So that e'en monarchs cannot hopeIn splendour with the bird to cope.Meekly enjoy thy happy lot,And so deserve that holy spot!

모모1815.-----ALL kinds of men, both small and great,A fine-spun web delight to create,And in the middle they take their place,And wield their scissors with wondrous grace.But if a besom should sweep that way:"What a most shameful thing," they say,--"They've crush'd a mighty palace to-day."

모모1815.-----IT IS GOOD.

모모IN Paradise while moonbeams play'd,

모모Jehovah found, in slumber deep,Adam fast sunk; He gently laid

모모Eve near him,--she, too, fell asleep.There lay they now, on earth's fair shrine,God's two most beauteous thoughts divine.--When this He saw, He cried:--'Tis Good!!!And scarce could move from where He stood.

모모No wonder, that our joy's completeWhile eye and eye responsive meet,When this blest thought of rapture moves us--That we're with Him who truly loves us,And if He cries:--Good, let it be!'Tis so for both, it seems to me.Thou'rt clasp'd within these arms of mine,Dearest of all God's thoughts divine!

모모1815.-----XI. PARIS NAME.

모모BOOK OF THE PARSEES.

모모THE BEQUEST OF THE ANCIENT PERSIAN FAITH.

모모BRETHREN, what bequest to you should comeFrom the lowly poor man, going home,Whom ye younger ones with patience tended,Whose last days ye honour'd and defended?

모모When we oft have seen the monarch ride,Gold upon him, gold on ev'ry side;Jewels on him, on his courtiers all,Thickly strewed as hailstones when they fall,

모모Have ye e'er known envy at the sight?And not felt your gaze become more bright,When the sun was, on the wings of morning,Darnawend's unnumber'd peaks adorning,

모모As he, bow-like, rose? How each eye dweltOn the glorious scene! I felt, I felt,Thousand times, as life's days fleeted by,Borne with him, the coming one, on high.

모모God upon His throne then to proclaim,Him, the life-fount's mighty Lord, to name,Worthily to prize that glorious sight,And to wander on beneath His light.

모모When the fiery orb was all defined,There I stood, as though in darkness, blind,Beat my breast, my quicken'd members threwOn the earth, brow-foremost, at the view.

모모Let this holy, great bequest rewardBrotherly good-will and kind regard:SOLEMN DUTY'S DAILY observation.--More than this, it needs no revelation.

모모If its gentle hands a new-born oneMove, then straightway turn it tow'rd the sun,--Soul and body dip in bath of fire!Then each morning's favour 'twill acquire.

모모To the living one, commit the dead,O'er the beast let earth and dust be spread,And, so far as may extend your might,What ye deem impure, conceal from sight.

모모Till your plains to graceful purity,That the sun with joy your labours see;When ye plant, your trees in rows contrive,For he makes the Regular to thrive.

모모E'en the floods that through the channel rushMust not fail in fulness or in gush;And as Senderud, from mountain high,Rises pure, in pureness must it die.

모모Not to weaken water's gentle fall,Carefully cleanse out the channels all;Salamander, snake, and rush, and reed,--All destroy,--each monster and each weed.

모모If thus pure ye earth and water keep,Through the air the sun will gladly peep,Where he, worthily enshrined in space,Worketh life, to life gives holy grace.

모모Ye, by toil on toil so sorely tried,Comfort take, the All is purified;And now man, as priest, may boldly dareFrom the stone God's image to prepare.

모모When the flame burns joyously and bright,Limbs are supple, radiant is the night;On the hearth when fire with ardour glows,Ripe the sap of plants and creatures grows.

모모Dragging wood, with rapture be it done,'Tis the seed of many an earthly sun;Plucking Pambeh, gladly may ye say:--This, as wick, the Holy will convey.

모모If ye meekly, in each burning lamp,See the nobler light's resplendent stamp,Ne'er will Fate prevent you, void of feeling,At God's throne at morningtide from kneeling.

모모This is Being's mighty signet, then,God's pure glass to angels and to men;Each word lisped the Highest's praise to sound,Ring in ring, united there is found.

모모From the shore of Senderud ascendeth,Up to Darnawend its pinions bendeth,As He dawns, with joy to greet His light,You with endless blessings to requite.

모모1819.*-----XII.  CHULD NAME.

모모BOOK OF PARADISE.

모모THE PRIVILEGED MEN.

모모AFTER THE BATTLE OF BADE, BENEATH THE CANOPY OF HEAVEN.

모모[This battle was fought in the second year of the Hegira (A.A.623), between the followers of Mahomet, who numbered threehundred and thirteen, possessing two horses and seventy camels,and the 'idolaters,' or Meccans, whose forces amounted to ninehundred and fifty, including two hundred cavalry. The victoryremained with Mahomet, who lost fourteen men, while seventy ofthe enemy were slain. A great accession of strength ensued inconsequence to the Prophet, who pretended that miracles werewrought in his behalf in the battle, God having sent angels tofight on his side, and having also made his army to appear largerto the enemy than it really was.--See the Koran, chapter viii.,and ABULFEDA'S Life of Mahomet.]

모모MAHOMET (Speaks).

모모LET the foeman sorrow o'er his dead,

모모Ne'er will they return again to light;O'er our brethren let no tear be shed,

모모For they dwell above yon spheres so bright.

모모All the seven planets open throw

모모All their metal doors with mighty shock,And the forms of those we loved below

모모At the gates of Eden boldly knock.

모모There they find, with bliss ne'er dream'd before,

모모Glories that my flight first show'd to eye,When the wondrous steed my person bore

모모In one second through the realms on high.

모모Wisdom's trees, in cypress-order growing,

모모High uphold the golden apples sweet;Trees of life, their spreading shadows throwing,

모모Shade each blossoming plant, each flow'ry seat.

모모Now a balmy zephyr from the East

모모Brings the heavenly maidens to thy view;With the eye thou now dost taste the feast,

모모Soon the sight pervades thee through and through.

모모There they stand, to ask thee thy career:

모모Mighty plans? or dangerous bloody rout?Thou'rt a hero, know they,--for Thourt here,

모모What a hero?--This they'll fathom out.

모모By thy wounds soon clearly this is shown,

모모Wounds that write thy fame's undying story;Wounds the true believer mark alone,

모모When have perish'd joy and earthly glory.

모모To chiosks and arbors thou art brought,

모모Fill'd with checkered marble columns bright;To the noble grape-juice, solace-fraught,

모모They the guest with kindly sips invite.

모모Youth! Thou'rt welcome more than e'er was youth

모모All alike are radiant and serene;When thou tak'st one to thine heart with truth,

모모Of thy band she'll be the friend and queen.

모모So prepare thee for this place of rest,

모모Never can it now be changed again;Maids like these will ever make thee blest,

모모Wines like these will never harm thy brain.

모모1819.-----THE FAVOURED BEASTS.

모모Or beasts there have been chosen four

모모To come to Paradise,And there with saints for evermore

모모They dwell in happy wise.

모모Amongst them all the Ass stands first;

모모He comes with joyous stride,For to the Prophet-City erst

모모Did Jesus on him ride.

모모Half timid next a Wolf doth creep,

모모To whom Mahomet spake"Spoil not the poor man of his sheep,

모모The rich man's thou mayst take."

모모And then the brave and faithful Hound,

모모Who by his master kept,And slept with him the slumbers sound

모모The seven sleepers slept.

모모Abuherrira's Cat, too, here,

모모Purrs round his master blest,For holy must the beast appear

모모The Prophet hath caress'd.

모모1815.-----THE SEVEN SLEEPERS.

모모Six among the courtiers favour'dFly before the Caesar's fury,Who would as a god be worshipp'd,Though in truth no god appearing,For a fly prevents him everFrom enjoying food at table.Though with fans his servants scare it,They the fly can never banish.It torments him, stings, and troubles,And the festal board perplexes,Then returning like the heraldOf the olden crafty Fly-God."What!"--the striplings say together--"Shall a fly a god embarrass?

모모Shall a god drink, eat at table,Like us mortals? No, the Only,Who the sun and moon created,And the glowing stars arch'd o'er us,He is God,--we'll fly!"--The gentle,Lightly shod, and dainty striplingsDid a shepherd meet, and hide them,With himself, within a cavern.

모모And the sheep-dog will not leave them,--Scared away, his foot all-mangled,To his master still he presses,And he joins the hidden party,Joins the favorites of slumber.

모모And the prince, whom they had fled from,Fondly-furious, thinks of vengeance,And, discarding sword and fire,Has them walled-up in the cavern,Walled-up fast with bricks and mortar.

모모But the others slumber ever,And the Angel, their protector,Gives before God's throne this notice"To the right and left alternateHave I ever cared to turn them,That their fair and youthful membersBe not by the mould-damp injured;Clefts within the rocks I open'd,That the sun may, rising, setting,Keep their cheeks in youthful freshness."So they lie there, bless'd by Heaven.And, with forepaws sound and scatheless,Sleeps the dog in gentle slumber.

모모Years come round, and years fly onward,And the youths at length awaken,And the wall, which now had moldered,From its very age has fallen.And Jamblika says,--whose beautyFar exceedeth all the others,--When the fearful shepherd lingers:--"I will run, and food procure you,Life and piece of gold I'll wager!"--Ephebus had many a year nowOwn'd the teaching of the ProphetJesus (Peace be with the Good One!)

모모And he ran, and at the gatewayWere the warders and the others.Yet he to the nearest baker's,Seeking bread, went swiftly onwards.--"Rogue!" thus cried the baker--"hast thou,Youth, a treasure, then, discover'd?Give me,--for the gold betrays thee,--Give me half, to keep thy secret!"--

모모And they quarrel.--To the monarchComes the matter; and the monarchFain would halve it, like the baker.

모모Now the miracle is provenSlowly by a hundred tokens.He can e'en his right establishTo the palace he erected,For a pillar, when pierced open.

모모Leads to wealth he said 'twould lead to.Soon are gather'd there whole races,Their relationship to show him.And as great-grandfather, noblyStands Jamblika's youthful figure.

모모As of ancestors, he hears them,Speaking of his son and grandsons.His great-grandsons stand around him,Like a race of valiant mortals,Him to honour,--him, the youngest.And one token on anotherRises up, the proof completing;The identity is provenOf himself, and of his comrades.

모모Now returns he to the cavern,With him go both king and people.--Neither to the king nor peopleE'er returns that chosen mortal;For the Seven, who for ages--Eight was, with the dog, their number--Had from all the world been sunder'd,Gabriel's mysterious power,To the will of God obedient,Hath to Paradise conducted,--And the cave was closed for ever.

모모1814-15.-----

모모SONGS FROM VARIOUS PLAYS, ETC

모모-----FROM FAUST.

모모I.DEDICATION.

모모YE shadowy forms, again ye're drawing near,

모모So wont of yore to meet my troubled gaze!Were it in vain to seek to keep you here?

모모Loves still my heart that dream of olden days?Oh, come then! and in pristine force appear,

모모Parting the vapor mist that round me plays!My bosom finds its youthful strength again,Feeling the magic breeze that marks your train.

모모Ye bring the forms of happy days of yore,

모모And many a shadow loved attends you too;Like some old lay, whose dream was well nigh o'er,

모모First-love appears again, and friendship true;Upon life's labyrinthine path once more

모모Is heard the sigh, and grief revives anew;The friends are told, who, in their hour of pride,Deceived by fortune, vanish'd from my side.

모모No longer do they hear my plaintive song,

모모The souls to whom I sang in life's young day;Scatter'd for ever now the friendly throng,

모모And mute, alas! each sweet responsive lay.My strains but to the careless crowd belong,

모모Their smiles but sorrow to my heart convey;And all who heard my numbers erst with gladness,If living yet, roam o'er the earth in sadness.

모모Long buried yearnings in my breast arise,

모모Yon calm and solemn spirit-realm to gain;Like the AEONIAN harp's sweet melodies,

모모My murmuring song breathes forth its changeful strain.A trembling seizes me, tears fill mine eyes,

모모And softer grows my rugged heart amain.All I possess far distant seems to be,The vanish'd only seems reality.

모모II.PROLOGUE IN HEAVEN.

모모THE ARCHANGELS' SONG.

모모RAPHAEL.

모모THE sun still chaunts, as in old time,

모모With brother-spheres in choral song,And with his thunder-march sublime

모모Moves his predestined course along.Strength find the angels in his sight,

모모Though he by none may fathomed be;Still glorious is each work of might

모모As when first form'd in majesty.

모모GABRIEL.

모모And swift and swift, in wondrous guise,

모모Revolves the earth in splendour bright,The radiant hues of Paradise

모모Alternating with deepest night.From out the gulf against the rock,

모모In spreading billows foams the ocean,--And cliff and sea with mighty shock,

모모The spheres whirl round in endless motion.

모모MICHAEL.

모모And storms in emulation growl

모모From land to sea, from sea to land,And fashion, as they wildly howl,

모모A circling, wonder-working band.Destructive flames in mad career

모모Precede Thy thunders on their way;Yet, Lord, Thy messengers revere

모모The soft mutations of Thy day.

모모THE THREE.

모모Strength find the angels in Thy sight,

모모Though none may hope to fathom Thee;Still glorious are Thy works of might,

모모As when first form'd in majesty.

모모III.CHORUS OF ANGELS.

모모CHRIST is arisen!

모모Mortal, all hail!Thou, of Earth's prison

모모Dreary and frail,Bursting the veil,

모모Proudly hast risen!

모모CHORUS OF WOMEN.

모모Rich spices and myrrh,

모모To embalm Him we brought;His corpse to inter

모모His true followers sought.In pure cerements shrin'd,

모모'Twas placed in the bierBut, alas! we now find

모모That Christ is not here.

모모CHORUS OF ANGELS.

모모Christ is arisen!

모모Speechless His love.Who to Earth's prison

모모Came from above,Trials to prove.

모모Now is He risen!

모모CHORUS OF YOUTHS.

모모Death's gloomy portal

모모Now hath He rended,--Living, immortal,

모모Heavenward ascended;Freed from His anguish,

모모Sees He God's throne;We still must languish,

모모Earthbound, alone.Now that He's reft us,

모모Heart-sad we pine;Why hast Thou left us,

모모Master divine?

모모CHORUS OF ANGELS.

모모Christ is arisen,

모모Death hath He slain;

모모Burst ye your prison,

모모Rend ye each chain!

모모Songs of praise lead ye,--

모모Love to show, heed ye,--

모모Hungry ones feed ye,--

모모Preaching, on speed ye,--

모모Coming joys plead ye,--Then is the Master near,Then is He here!

모모IV.CHORUS OF SPIRITS.

모모VANISH, dark clouds on high,

모모Offspring of night!Let a more radiant beamThrough the blue ether gleam,

모모Charming the sight!Would the dark clouds on high

모모Melt into air!Stars glimmer tenderly,

모모Planets more fair

모모Shed their soft light.Spirits of heav'nly birth,Fairer than sons of earth,Quivering emotions true

모모Hover above;Yearning affections, too,

모모In their train move.See how the spirit-band,By the soft breezes fann'd,Covers the smiling land,--Covers the leafy grove,Where happy lovers rove,Deep in a dream of love,True love that never dies!Bowers on bowers rise,

모모Soft tendrils twine;While from the press escapes,Born of the juicy grapes,

모모Foaming, the wine;And as the current flowsO'er the bright stones it goes,--Leaving the hilly lands

모모Far, far behind,--Into a sea expands,

모모Loving to windRound the green mountain's base;And the glad-winged race,

모모Rapture sip in,As they the sunny light,And the fair islands bright,

모모Hasten to win,That on the billows playWith sweet deceptive ray,Where in glad choral songShout the exulting throng;Where on the verdant plain

모모Dancers we see,Spreading themselves amain

모모Over the lea.Some boldly climbing are

모모O'er the steep brake,Others are floating far

모모O'er the smooth lake.All for a purpose move,

모모All with life teem,While the sweet stars above

모모Blissfully gleam.

모모V.MARGARET AT HER SPINNING-WHEEL.

모모MY heart is sad,

모모My peace is o'er;I find it never

모모And nevermore.

모모When gone is he,The grave I see;The world's wide allIs turned to gall.

모모Alas, my head

모모Is well-nigh crazed;My feeble mind

모모Is sore amazed.

모모My heart is sad,

모모My peace is o'er;I find it never

모모And nevermore.

모모For him from the window

모모Alone I spy;For him alone

모모From home go I.

모모His lofty step,

모모His noble form,His mouth's sweet smile,

모모His glances warm,

모모His voice so fraught

모모With magic bliss,His hand's soft pressure,

모모And, ah, his kiss!

모모My heart is sad,

모모My peace is o'er;I find it never

모모And nevermore.

모모My bosom yearns

모모For his form so fair;Ah, could I clasp him

모모And hold him there!

모모My kisses sweet

모모Should stop his breath,And 'neath his kisses

모모I'd sink in death!

모모VI.SCENE--A GARDEN,

모모Margaret. Faust.

모모MARGARET.

모모DOST thou believe in God?

모모FAUST.

모모Doth mortal live

모모Who dares to say that he believes in God?Go, bid the priest a truthful answer give,

모모Go, ask the wisest who on earth e'er trod,--Their answer will appear to beGiven alone in mockery.

모모MARGARET.

모모Then thou dost not believe? This sayest thou?

모모FAUST.

모모Sweet love, mistake not what I utter now!Who knows His name?Who dares proclaim:--Him I believe?Who so can feelHis heart to steelTo sari believe Him not?The All-Embracer,The All-Sustained,Holds and sustains He notThee, me, Himself?

모모Hang not the heavens their arch overhead?Lies not the earth beneath us, firm?Gleam not with kindly glancesEternal stars on high?Looks not mine eye deep into thine?And do not all thingsCrowd on thy head and heart,And round thee twine, in mystery eterne,Invisible, yet visible?Fill, then, thy heart, however vast, with this,And when the feeling perfecteth thy bliss,O, call it what thou wilt,Call it joy! heart! love! God!No name for it I know!'Tis feeling all--nought else;Name is but sound and smoke,Obscuring heaven's bright glow.

모모VII.MARGARET, Placing fresh flowers in the flower-pots.

모모O THOU well-tried in grief,

모모Grant to thy child relief,And view with mercy this unhappy one!

모모The sword within thy heart,

모모Speechless with bitter smart,Thou Lookest up towards thy dying son.

모모Thou look'st to God on high,

모모And breathest many a sighO'er his and thy distress, thou holy One!

모모Who e'er can know

모모The depth of woe

모모Piercing my very bone?The sorrows that my bosom fill,Its trembling, its aye-yearning will,

모모Are known to thee, to thee alone!

모모Wherever I may go,

모모With woe, with woe, with woe,My bosom sad is aching!

모모I scarce alone can creep,

모모I weep, I weep, I weep,My very heart is breaking.

모모The flowers at my window

모모My falling tears bedewed,When I, at dawn of morning,

모모For thee these flow'rets strewed.

모모When early to my chamber

모모The cheerful sunbeams stole,I sat upon my pallet,

모모In agony of soul.

모모Help! rescue me from death and misery!

모모Oh, thou well-tried in grief,

모모Grant to thy child relief,And view with mercy my deep agony!

모모FROM FAUST--SECOND PART.

모모I.

모모ARIEL.

모모WHEN in spring the gentle rain

모모Breathes into the flower new birth,When the green and happy plain

모모Smiles upon the sons of earth,Haste to give what help we may,

모모Little elves of wondrous might!Whether good or evil they,

모모Pity for them feels the sprite.

모모II.CHORUS OF SPIRITS.

모모WHEN the moist and balmy gale

모모Round the verdant meadow sighs,Odors sweet in misty veil

모모At the twilight-hour arise.Murmurings soft of calm repose

모모Rock the heart to child-like rest,And the day's bright portals close

모모On the eyes with toil oppress'd.

모모Night already reigns o'er all,

모모Strangely star is link'd to star;Planets mighty, sparkling small,

모모Glitter near and gleam afar.Gleam above in clearer night,

모모Glitter in the glassy sea;Pledging pure and calm delight,

모모Rules the moon in majesty.

모모Now each well-known hour is over,

모모Joy and grief have pass'd away;Feel betimes! thoult then recover:

모모Trust the newborn eye of day.Vales grow verdant, hillocks teem,

모모Shady nooks the bushes yield,And with waving, silvery gleam,

모모Rocks the harvest in the field.

모모Wouldst thou wish for wish obtain,

모모Look upon yon glittering ray!Lightly on thee lies the chain,

모모Cast the shell of sleep away!Tarry not, but be thou bold,

모모When the many loiter still;All with ease may be controll'd

모모By the man of daring will.

모모III.ARIEL.

모모HARK! the storm of hours draws near,Loudly to the spirit-earSigns of coming day appear.Rocky gates are wildly crashing,Phoebus' wheels are onward dashing;

모모(A wonderful noise proclaims the approach of the sun.)

모모Light doth mighty sounds beget!Pealing loud as rolling thunder,Eye and ear it fills with wonder,

모모Though itself unconscious yet.Downward steals it,'mongst the flowersSeeking deeper, stiller bowers,'Mongst the foliage, 'neath the rock;Thou'lt be deafened by the shock!-----FROM FAUST--SECOND PART.

모모SCENE THE LAST.

모모ANGELS.[Hovering in the higher regions of air, and hearing the immortalpart of Faust.]

모모THE spirit-region's noble limb

모모Hath 'scaled the Archfiend's power;For we have strength to rescue him

모모Who labours ev'ry hour.And if he feels within his breast

모모A ray of love from heaven.He's met by all the squadron blest

모모With welcome gladly given.

모모THE YOUNGER ANGELS.

모모Yonder roses, from the holyHands of penitents so lowly,Help'd to render us victorious,And to do the deed all-glorious;For they gain'd us this soul-treasure.

모모Evil ones those roses banish'd,

모모Devils, when we met them, vanish'd.Spirits felt love's pangs with pleasure,Where hell's torments used to dwell;E'en the hoary king of hellFelt sharp torments through him run.Shout for joy! the prize is won.

모모THE MORE PERFECT ANGELS.

모모Strains of mortality

모모Long have oppress'd us;Pure could they ever be,

모모If of asbestos.If mighty spirit-strength

모모Elements everKnew how to seize at length,

모모Angels could neverLink'd twofold natures move,

모모Where single-hearted;By nought but deathless love

모모Can they be parted.

모모THE YOUNGER ANGELS.

모모See where a spirit-race

모모Bursts on the sight!Dimly their forms I trace

모모Round the far height.Each cloud becometh clear,While the bright troops appear

모모Of the blest boys,

모모From the Earth's burden free,In a glad company

모모Drinking in joys,Born of the world above,

모모Springtime and bliss.May they forerunners proveOf a more perfect love,

모모Link'd on to this!

모모THE BEATIFIED CHILDREN.

모모Thus as a chrysalis

모모Gladly we gain him,And as a pledge of bliss

모모Safely retain him;When from the shell he's free

모모Whereby he's tainted,Perfect and fair he'll be,

모모Holy and sainted.

모모DOCTOR MARINAS.(In the highest, purest cell.)

모모Wide is the prospect here,

모모Raised is the soul;Women on high appear,

모모Seeking their goal.

모모'Mongat them the radiant one,

모모Queen of the skies,In her bright starry crown

모모Greets my glad eyes.

모모(With ecstasy.)

모모Thou who art of earth the queen.

모모Let me, 'neath the blueHeav'nly canopy serene

모모Thy sweet mystery view!Grant the gentle solemn force

모모Which the breast can move.And direct our onward course

모모Tow'rd thy perfect love.Dauntless let our courage be,

모모At thy bright behest;Mild our ardour suddenly,

모모When thou bidd'st us rest.Virgin, type of holiness,

모모Mother, honour-crown'd,Thou whom we as queen confess,

모모Godlike and renowned.

모모Round her, in gentle play,

모모Light clouds are stealing;Penitents fair are they,

모모Who, humbly kneeling,Sip in the ether sweet,As they for grace entreat.

모모Thou, who art from passions free,

모모Kindly art inclin'd,When the sons of frailty

모모Seek thee, meek in mind.

모모Borne by weakness' stream along,

모모Hard it is to save them;Who can burst lust's chains so strong,

모모That, alas, enslave them?O how soon the foot may slip,

모모When the smooth ground pressing!O, how false are eye and lip,

모모False a breath caressing!

모모MATER GLORLOSA hovers past.

모모CHORUS OF PENITENT WOMEN.

모모To bring realms on high

모모In majesty soaring,O, hark to our cry

모모Thy pity imploring,Thou help to the cheerless,In glory so peerless!

모모MAGNA PECCATRIX (St. Luke vii. 36).

모모By the love, which o'er the feet

모모Of thy God-transfigur'd SonDropp'd the team, like balsam sweet,

모모Spite of ev'ry scornful one;By the box of ointment rare,

모모Whence the drops so fragrant fell;By the locks, whose gentle care

모모Dried His holy members well--

모모muller SAMARITANA (St, John iv.).

모모By the well where Abram erst

모모Drove his flocks to drink their fill;By the bucket which the thirst

모모Of the Saviour served to still;By the fountain, balm-exhaling,

모모That from yon bright region flows,Ever clear and never failing.

모모As round ev'ry world it goes--

모모MARIA AEGYPTIACA (Acta Sanctorum).

모모By the sacred spot immortal,

모모Where the Lord's remains they plac'd;By the arm, that from the portal

모모Drove me back with warning haste;By my forty years of lowly

모모Penance in a desert land;By the farewell greetings holy

모모That I wrote upon the sand--

모모THE THREE.

모모Thou who ne'er thy radiant face

모모From the greatest sinners hides,Thou who Thine atoning grace

모모Through eternity provident,

모모Let this soul, by virtue stirr'd,

모모Self-forgetful though when living,That perceived not that it err'd,

모모Feel thy mercy, sin forgiving!

모모UNA POENITENTIUM.(Once named Margaret, pressing near them.)

모모Oh radiance-spreading One,

모모Who equall'd art by none,In mercy view mine ecstasy!

모모For he whom erst I loved,

모모No more by sorrow proved,Returns at length to me!

모모BEATIFIED CHILDREN.(Approaching as they hover round.)

모모He now in strength of limb

모모Far doth outweigh us,And, as we tended him,

모모So will repay us.Early removed were we

모모Far from life's story;Train'd now himself, will he

모모Train us in glory.

모모THE PENITENT, once named Margaret.

모모Link'd with the noble band of spirits,

모모Scarce can the new one feel or seeThe radiant life he now inherits,

모모So like that holy band is he.See how he bursts each bond material,

모모And parts the olden veil at length,--In vesture clad of grace ethereal,

모모Comes in the glow of youthful strength.Oh, let me guide his steps victorious,

모모While dazzled by the new-born light.

모모MATER GLORIOSA.

모모Come! raise thyself to spheres more glorious,He'll follow when thou matzoth his sight.

모모DOCTOR MARINAS.(Prostrated in adoration.)

모모O repentant sinful ones,

모모On that bright face gaze ye,And, in grateful orisons,

모모Your blest fortune praise ye!Be each virtue of the mind

모모To thy service given!Virgin, mother, be thou kind!

모모Goddess, queen of heaven!

모모CHORUS MYSTICS.

모모Each thing of mortal birth

모모Is but a typeWhat was of feeble worth

모모Here becomes ripe.What was a mystery

모모Here meets the eye;The ever-womanly

모모Draws us on high.

모모(Finis.)-----FROM IPHIGENIA IN TAURIS.

모모ACT IV. SCENE 5.

모모SONG OF THE FATES.

모모YE children of mortalsThe deities dread!The mastery hold theyIn hands all-eternal,And use them, unquestioned,What manner they like.

모모Let him fear them doubly,Whom they have uplifted!On cliffs and on clouds, oh,Round tables all-golden,he seats are made ready.

모모When rises contention,The guests are humid downwardsWith shame and dishonorTo deep depths of midnight,And vainly await they,Bound fast in the darkness,A just condemnation.

모모But they remain everIn firmness unshakenRound tables all-golden.On stride they from mountainTo mountain far distant:From out the abysses'Dark jaws, the breath risesOf torment-choked TitansUp tow'rds them, like incenseIn light clouds ascending.

모모The rulers immortalAvert from whole peoplesTheir blessing-fraught glances,And shun, in the children,To trace the once cherish'd,Still, eloquent featuresTheir ancestors wore.

모모Thus chanted the Parae;The old man, the banish'd,In gloomy vault lying,Their song overheareth,Sons, grandsons remembereth,And shaketh his head.-----FROM GOTZ VON BERLICHINGEN.

모모ACT II.

모모LIEBETRAUT plays and sings.

모모HIS bow and dart bearing,And torch brightly flaring,

모모Dan Cupid on flies;With victory laden,To vanquish each maiden

모모He roguishly tries.

모모Up! up!

모모On! on!His arms rattle loudly,His wings rustle proudly,And flames fill his eyes.

모모Then finds he each bosom

모모Defenseless and bare;They gladly receive him

모모And welcome him there.The point of his arrows

모모He lights in the glow;They clasp him and kiss him

모모And fondle him so.He e o! Pap!

모모FROM EGMONT.

모모ACT I.

모모CLARA winds a skein, and sings with Brackenburg.

모모THE drum gives the signal!

모모Loud rings the shrill fife!My love leads his troops on

모모Full arm'd for the strife,While his hand grasps his lanceAs they proudly advance.

모모My bosom pants wildly!My blood hotly flows!Oh had I a doublet,A helmet, and hose!

모모Through the gate with bold footstep

모모I after him hied,--Each province, each country

모모Explored by his side.The coward foe trembledThen rattled our shot:What bliss e'er resembled

모모A soldier's glad lot!

모모ACT III.

모모CLARA sings.

모모Gladness

모모And sadnessAnd pensiveness blending

모모Yearning

모모And burningIn torment ne'er ending;

모모Sad unto death,Proudly soaring above;

모모Happy aloneIs the soul that doth love!

모모FROM "WILHELM MEISTER'S APPRENTICESHIP."

모모BOOK II., CHAP. XIII.

모모WHO never eat with tears his bread,

모모Who never through night's heavy hoursSat weeping on his lonely bed,--

모모He knows you not, ye heavenly powers!

모모Through you the paths of life we gain,

모모Ye let poor mortals go astray,And then abandon them to pain,--

모모E'en here the penalty we pay,-----WHO gives himself to solitude,

모모Soon lonely will remain;Each lives, each loves in joyous mood,

모모And leaves him to his pain.

모모Yes! leave me to my grief!Were solitude's relief

모모E'er granted me,

모모Alone I should not be.

모모A lover steals, on footstep light,

모모To learn if his love's alone;Thus o'er me steals, by day and night,

모모Anguish before unknown,Thus o'er me steals deep grief.Ah, when I find relief

모모Within the tomb so lonely,

모모Will rest be met with only!-----BOOK IV., CHAP. XI.

모모My grief no mortals know,

모모Except the yearning!Alone, a prey to woe,

모모All pleasure spurning,Up tow'rds the sky I throw

모모A gaze discerning.

모모He who my love can know

모모Seems ne'er returning;With strange and fiery glow

모모My heart is burning.My grief no mortals know,

모모Except the yearning!-----BOOK V., CHAP. X.

모모SING no more in mournful tones

모모Of the loneliness of night;For 'tis made, ye beauteous ones,

모모For all social pleasures bright.

모모As of old to man a wife

모모As his better half was given,So the night is half our life,

모모And the fairest under heaven.

모모How can ye enjoy the day,

모모Which obstructs our rapture's tide?Let it waste itself away;

모모Worthless 'tis for aught beside.

모모But when in the darkling hours

모모From the lamp soft rays are glowing,And from mouth to mouth sweet showers,

모모Now of jest, now love, are flowing,--

모모When the nimble, wanton boy,

모모Who so wildly spends his days,Oft amid light sports with joy

모모O'er some trifling gift delays,?

모모When the nightingale is singing

모모Strains the lover holds so dear,Though like sighs and wailings ringing

모모In the mournful captive's ear,--

모모With what heart-emotion blest

모모Do ye hearken to the bell,Wont of safety and of rest

모모With twelve solemn strokes to tell!

모모Therefore in each heavy hour,

모모Let this precept fill your heart:O'er each day will sorrow loud,

모모Rapture ev'ry night impart.-----EPILOGUE TO SCHILLER'S "SONG OF THE BELL."

모모[This fine piece, written originally in 1805, on Schiller'sdeath, was altered and recast by Goethe in 1815, on the occasionof the performance on the stage of the Song of the Bell. Hencethe allusion in the last verse.]

모모To this city joy reveal it!

모모Peace as its first signal peal it!

모모(Song of the Bell--concluding lines.)

모모AND so it proved! The nation felt, ere long,

모모That peaceful signal, and, with blessings fraught,A new-born joy appear'd; in gladsome song

모모To hail the youthful princely pair we sought;While in a living, ever-swelling throng

모모Mingled the crowds from ev'ry region brought,And on the stage, in festal pomp array'dThe HOMAGE OF THE ARTS * we saw displayed.

모모(* The title of a lyric piece composed by Schiller in honour ofthe marriage of the hereditary Prince of Weimar to the PrincessMaria of Russia, and performed in 1804.)

모모When, lo! a fearful midnight sound I hear,

모모That with a dull and mournful echo rings.And can it be that of our friend so dear

모모It tells, to whom each wish so fondly clings?Shall death overcome a life that all revere?

모모How such a loss to all confusion brings!How such a parting we must ever rue!The world is weeping,--shall not we weep too?

모모He was our own! How social, yet how great

모모Seem'd in the light of day his noble mind!How was his nature, pleasing yet sedate,

모모Now for glad converse joyously incline,Then swiftly changing, spirit-fraught, elate,

모모Life's plan with deep-felt meaning it design'd,Fruitful alike in counsel and in deed!This have we proved, this tasted, in our need.

모모He was our own! O may that thought so blest

모모Overcome the voice of wailing and of woeHe might have sought the Lasting, safe at rest

모모In harbour, when the tempest ceased to blow.Meanwhile his mighty spirit onward press'd

모모Where goodness, beauty, truth, for ever grow;And in his rear, in shadowy outline, layThe vulgar, which we all, alas, obey!

모모Now doth he deck the garden-turret fair

모모Where the stars' language first illuded his soul,As secretly yet clearly through the air

모모On the eterne, the living sense it stole;And to his own, and our great profit, there

모모Exchangeth he the seasons as they roll;Thus nobly doth he vanquish, with renown,The twilight and the night that weigh us down.

모모Brighter now glow'd his cheek, and still more bright.

모모With that unchanging, ever-youthful glow,--That courage which overcomes, in hard-fought fight,

모모Sooner or later, ev'ry earthly foe--That faith which, soaring to the realms of light,

모모Now boldly Presseth on, now bendeth low,So that the good may work, wax, thrive amain,So that the day the noble may attain.

모모Yet, though so skill'd, of such transcendent worth,

모모This boarded scaffold doth he not despise;The fate that on its axis turns the earth

모모From day to night, here shows he to our eyes,Raising, through many a work of glorious birth,

모모Art and the artist's fame up tow'rd the skies.He fills with blossoms of the noblest strife,With life itself, this effigy of life.

모모His giant-step, as ye full surely knew,

모모Measured the circle of the will and deed,Each country's changing thoughts and morals too,

모모The darksome book with clearness could he read;Yet how he, breathless 'midst his friends so true,

모모Despaired in sorrow, scarce from pain was freed,--All this have we, in sadly happy years,For he was ours, bewailed with feeling tears.

모모When from the agonizing weight of grief

모모He raised his eyes upon the world again,We show'd him how his thoughts might find relief

모모From the uncertain present's heavy chain,Gave his fresh-kindled mind a respite brief,

모모With kindly skill beguiling ev'ry pain,And e'en at eve, when setting was his sun,From his wan cheeks a gentle smile we won.

모모Full early had he read the stern decree,

모모Sorrow and death to him, alas, were known;Ofttimes recovering, now departed he,--

모모Dread tidings, that our hearts had fear'd to own!Yet his transfigured being now can see

모모Itself, e'en here on earth, transfigured grown.What his own age reproved, and deem'd a crime,Hath been ennobled now by death and time.

모모And many a soul that with him strove in fight,

모모And his great merit grudged to recognise,Now feels the impress of his wondrous might,

모모And in his magic fetters gladly lies;E'en to the highest bath he winged his flight,

모모In close communion link'd with all we prize.Extol him then! What mortals while they liveBut half receive, posterity shall give.

모모Thus is he left us, who so long ago,--

모모Ten years, alas, already!--turn'd from earth;We all, to our great joy, his precepts know,

모모Oh may the world confess their priceless worth!In swelling tide tow'rd every region flow

모모The thoughts that were his own peculiar birth;He gleams like some departing meteor bright,Combining, with his own, eternal light.-----

모모L'ENVOl.

모모-----Now, gentle reader, is our journey ended,

모모Mute is our minstrel, silent is our song;Sweet the bard's voice whose strains our course attended,

모모Pleasant the paths he guided us along.Now must we part,--Oh word all full of sadness,Changing to pensive retrospect our gladness!

모모Reader, farewell! we part perchance for ever,

모모Scarce may I hope to meet with thee again;But e'en though fate our fellowship may sever,

모모Reader, will aught to mark that tie remain?Yes! there is left one sad sweet bond of union,--Sorrow at parting links us in communion.

모모But of the twain, the greater is my sorrow,--

모모Reader, and why?--Bethink thee of the sun,How, when he sets, he waiteth for the morrow,

모모Proudly once more his giant-race to run,--Yet, e'en when set, a glow behind him leaving,Gladdening the spirit, which had else been grieving.

모모Thus mayst thou feel, for thou to GOETHE only

모모Baldest farewell, nor camest aught for me.Twofold my parting, leaving me all lonely,--

모모I now must part from GOETHE and from thee,Parting at once from comrade and from leader,--Farewell, great minstrel! farewell, gentle reader!

모모Hush'd is the harp, its music sunk in slumbers,Memory alone can waken now its numbers.

모모End
